THE RED PAGE.
The excellent taste of some among the public was
shown by the pictures purchased from Sydney
Society of Artists’ Sketch Exhibition. Decidedly,
the plums of the show, at the prices, were
picked. By .the way, it requires a real mental
effort to avoid the influence of the red ticket in a
picture-corner: the fact that red gold has been
paid for that red ticket—that the picture is
approved by Somebody—does its best to vitiate
ones judgment. But far more of the pictures
saoultl have been sold : there remained many which
were interesting, and cheap. The show claimed
no higher epithet than “interesting,” but it
was that. My own fancy leaned most to Mrs.
Fred Parsons “Sketch near Mittagong,” which
has depths to dream over. Sid. Long’s “Bush
Fire, Hawkesbury” made one feel that he
has the root of the manner in him : when
he has learnt to paint bitter he will surely
paint very well indeed. An 1 there were other good
examples of his talent. Florence Fuller’s “A
Breezj' Spring Morning ” was sp icially attractive ;
Dattilo Rubbo and J. S. Watkins showed good
work; and there were others. R Sidney Cocks was
again best in a favourite water-colour subject—a
low coast under a lowering sky : his rocks seemed
less effective. And there were yet others. Really :
there should have been (with a little taste, and a
little money) people enough in Sydney to buy half
the sketches hung. There were not. Never mind:
the work is its own reward.
♦
Harry Lhurston Peck, in N.Y. Bookman, was
lately discussing the ominous significance of
names. And there is no mistake about it: we are
all what our names mike us. Is not Napoleou as
necessary le Grand as Albert is the lackadaisically
Good—for nothing in particular? What man
could achieve greatness upon whom his god-
parents thrust the baptismal titles of Reginald
Frederick ? Do we not owe the inanities of modern
womankind to Maude with an e and Alyce with a
y and Gladys in conjunction with Smith? But
what a treasury of domestic virtue in the plain
Jane of our foremothers! what cooking capacity
in Susan ! how Martha swept and scrubbed ! to
what majesty might not Mary aspire ! Question:
Why are our M lto:i3 mute and inglorious ?
Answer: Because they are named Gerald Augustus
instead of plain John. For what saith the proverb:
Give a Dog a Bad Name, and Hang Him !
His name is the one thing a man never
escapes from. He can change his creed and his
tailor, he can divorce his wife, he can throw away
every atom of flesh and get a complete new body
every few years ; but he cannot drop the conse-
crated cross of the christening-font or evade the
sinister obsessions of the birth-certificate. Post
equitem sedet Eustace Algernon, and round Glara
Ysabel’s neck the noose is drawn every night
anew. Vainly the hare doubles in deference to a
legator : Maliound is always at its heels ; and long
after the individual is annihilated his name may
be enjoying tombstone immortality at sixpence a
letter, or ninepence if Old English text be per-
mitted to more tenderly assuage the ecstasies of
Grief.
A letter —
Post Office, Armidale, April 2/1900. Dear Book-
fellow,—A report is current in Queensland I am dead.
Some months ago I was very Sick with fever, from which
—according to what I heard—the report had arisen. Now
if such a report is likely to be believed by many friends, I
should consider it a favor if you -will contradict it in Bul-
letin. I have not yet recovered my true tone, and am
' forbidden to swim by the doctor, but I’m risking it—see
enclosed.—Yours very faithfully, Artiiur A. D. Bayldon.
The enclosure—
“ Last appearance of the champion back-stroke swimmer
of the world in Armidale Swimming Baths on Wed-
nesday night at 8.30, under the Mayor’s Patronage. Brass
band in attendance. Will leave the Post-office at 8 p.m.
Programme : Arthur A. D. Bayldon’s feats never before
exhibited by any other champion. 1. Floating in 3ft. of
fresh water for 15min. without a movement. 2. The
torpedo champion movement. 3. Fish side shoots. 4.
Swimming feet first under the water. 5. The pendulum
and revolving combination. 6. The corkscrew curve. 7.
Swimming under water with hands and feet strapped,
Mr. Bayldon’s greatest achievement, recognised by swim-
ming authorities as the most difficult and dangerous feat
ever attempted by a swimmer. 8. The sturgeon stroke—
the favorite quick stroke of the English quick swimmers.
9. Swimming backward with feet and one hand above
water, the body submerged. 10. The flo.ating statue—
the most graceful movement in the swimming world,
invented b3' Mr. Ba.vldor.. During the night a sensational
dive of 35ft. will be given. Mr. Bayldon will enter the
water at 8.30. Adults, Is. ; children, fid. Reserved seats
for ladies.”
And the Muie blushes—as she ought.

In this way the Condensed Contributor revenges
herself—
“ What are you doing ? ” said the Story. “ Condensing
you,” said the Writer. “I don’t want to be con—’’
“ founded; I suppose not, so keep calm.” “ Why—”
“ Editor’s orders ; you ’re too lengthy.” “ Oh —you ’re not
condensing, Master. You ’re amputating.” “ What c.o
you know about it ? You ’re as bad as a woman. Steady 1
—now your power of speech is gone. Conversation is too
slow a method of telling things. All your thoughts are
now reduced to a few emotions.” “My speech is n’t gone.
I can still croak.” “ You don’t think anybody will listen
to that, do you ? There, you are finished. Go and ask the
editor if you will do.” “Come in,” said the editor, absently.
“I’m in,” croaked the Story, sdraggling to reach the top of
the desk. “Can’t see you. Batter come out, then I might.”
“ I’m the story.” “ That is hardly as definite as it sounds.”
“ I’m shortened. My master sent me to see if I would
do.” “ Beg pardon—ha ! I see you. There, sit on this
pile of paper. And, as you are so short, perhaps you will
also he brief?” “My imagination is gone—the ink
objected to that part of me. It didn’t care to be was ed
on anything so insubstantial. I have no regret.
The compositor said I took up too much room
and time. M3' jealousy and ficklemindedness are
gone.” “What is left of you? He might as well
have cut you in halves at once.” “I—— ” “Here!
take this to your Master.” And he wrote :- “Go
and study your dictionary in the ! a:np’ and ‘con’
columns, and condense ! condense !! condense !!! ” “ I’m
hack,” said the Story to the Writer, meekly presenting
the Editor’s message. “ Get on the table,” growled the
Writer. . . . “Now, go and see if you will do. I’m
exhausted.” “I’m back,” said the Story to the Editor.
“ Well, what are 3'ou now? ” “I can be born, married,
and buried in three lines.” “ Good 1 ” “I can cry in
half a line.” “Better.” “There is a good fml of
information left. It goes a long way in filling up
a column, when there’s nothing else to fill up.”
“ Can you laugh ?” “I can— ” “ Yes, you will do. Tell
your master to call.” Away went the Story, and leaping
into the Writer’s room, perched on the desk and said—
“ If I could speak, Master, I would tell you the kind of
dress I am going to wear next.” “What?” drearily
moaned the Writer. ‘ ‘ Have I got to dress you up now ? ”
“ No,” answered the Story, hopping about from floor to
ceiling ; “ J’m going to wear blade and white ! ”
A Transvaal war correspondent gave Lond.
Daily Chronicle a strange story of Spion Kop—
One of the Lancaster men, while in the act of firing in
a prone position, had his head taken clean off by a large
shell. To the astonishment of his comrades the headless
body quietly rose, stood upright for a few seconds, and
then fell.
This set curious people looking up records.
The British Medical Association has cases which
show that, after decapitation by the sword, the
heart has been known to beat for nine minutes,
respiratory sounds to continue for five and a-half
minutes, and movements of the larynx to take
place after the lapse of twenty-three minutes. Dr;
Stille saw a brakesman shot on a Nashville rail-
way ; the brakesman continued to extend his
hands over his brake after death. A United
States soldier in the North and South contest,
who was shot in the , act of mounting his
horse, continued after death to stand with
one foot in the stirrup and one hand grasping
his horse’s mane, until an attempt was
actually made to capture him alive. Julie
de Fontenelle- has pointed out that all the
convulsive movements of decapitated persons ex-
press anguish, and Sue and other authorities that
if a severed head is turned to the sun the eyes
close at once. There are parallels in the animal
world. Fowls, eels, and other familiar creatures
often show considerable muscular activity after
death. But science has not. yet made clear
whether, after decapitation, the head or the body
is first deprived of sensibility. In any case, the cited
reactions are ail probably unconscious—that is,
the individual practically dies with the blow, and
knows nothing, as an individual, of what comes
afterwards.

Miles B. Arnold, in a Lond. weekly, says some
true things regarding the field open to Poetry—
One of Poetry’s characteristics is that it must appeal
only to the very elements of thought and feeling. Is it
not the last of the arts to treat a subject? Is not its
work done with the oldest and mast reverenced words of
a language? Those words to which generations have
knitted innumerable ties are those oftenest used by our
poets. [Consider this verse—J
“ In my garden green
Strange flowers do grow,
Of the colour of benzo-purpurine
And mauve and snow.”
Apart from the merits or demerits of the lines, no one,
I think, could find “benzo-purpurine” anything but
comic. The word is good in its way ; it has a meaning ;
but it is an upstart, and away from its proper environ-
ment is absurd. It is quite possible that benzo-purpurine
may be transformed slowly by usage and good company,
but the poet cannot use it yet. Within certain limits is
it not a true instinct that makes the minor poet write
“He quaffed again” rather than “He took another
drink”? Could anyone make a reference to an A.B.C.
restaurant ring true to a poet’s ear ?
Now, I maintain that as it is with words, so also with
ideas and emotions ; only those that have acquired time-
hallowed associations can seem right in the poet’s art.
... In poetry the introduction of anything novel and
modern in thought jars the reader because it sets going
in his mind a train of newly-acquired, unsettled and
possibly conflicting ideas. . . . Poetry is not a pioneering
art, it is retrospective. It is for the novelist and realistic
dramatist to “ grapple with present-day humanity.”

Said a young relative of mine, trying to calm a woman’s
fears re snakes and lizards : “You ’ll always know a snake
when you hear one, because it makes no noise.”
An old digger described the sound made by a kangaroo
as “A kind of high whistling noise with a very flat
sound.” The same artist in confusion of ideas usually
had his knife, tobacco, spectacles, &c., tied with string to
his person, and he explained : “ You see, when I put ’em
down I don’t pick ’em up ; and then, when I find ’em,
they ’re lost.”

She hurried inside early one morning, crying, “ Get up,

Balls are often acted, not spoken. Old Tim Ryan, on
the River, in the heat of a race shearing lambs, flung the
shears out of the porthole, and industriously crammed
the shorn lamb into the water-pot. And I have seen a
man, about to wash his feet, put the water on a high
chair and seat himself flat on the floor.

A. .T. Evelyn, in Australian poetry, seems to 11
have written during the second quarter ol this
century. His talent was slight and delicate; but
many of his verses have a charm of feeling and of
melody which is good to linger over. The pre-
eeditig examples are taken from The Empire, and
Halloran’s memorial verses in the same paper
show that Evelyn died about 1857. Does anyone :
know his history ?
According to The, Saturday Review M. Brentano,
keeper of the archives of the Bastille, has finally
settled the identity of the Man in the Iron Mask.
He was Count Mattioli, minister of the Duke of
Mantua, who betrayed both his master and Louis
XIV. in arranging the transfer to France of the
post of Casal. He tried to secure double profits
by selling the plot to Spain and Austria, and was
imprisoned by the indignant Louis—lawlessly,
whence the mask. He died in 1703, and his death
certificate was made out in his own name.
At the dinner to Henry Lawson and A. H.
Fullwood, both leaving for London this month in
the hope of Better Things, the most memorable
speech was made by Lawson himself. “ When I
lose sight of the Leeuwin,” said the writer of
“Faces in the Street and tears came into his
eyes—“there will be a hole in my heart big
enough to put all Australia into.” That’s a big
heart; as well as a big hole.
R.W. writes from Townsville-
Many months ago V. J. Daley asked if anyone could !
locate a passage in which the Lord was represented as
viewing the faces of the dead, and, struck by their great
serenity, saying to Gabriel: “ Put away the trumpet.
There ’ll be no resurrection.” Perhaps this passage, by
Robert Buchanan, is what he meant —
“ When He returns and finds all sleeping here,
Some old, some young, some fair, and some not fair,
Will he stoop down and whisper in each ear,
‘ Awaken ! ’ or, for pity’s sake, forbear,
Saying ‘ How shall I meet their frozen stare
Of wonder, and their eyes so woe-begone f
How shall I comfort them in their despair,
If they cry out “ Too late ! let us sleep on !’’ ’
Perchance he will not wake us up, but when
He sees us look so happy in our rest,
Will murmur ‘ Poor dead women and dead men !
Dire was their doom and weary was their quest,
Wherefore awake them into life again?
Let them sleep on untroubled—it is best.’ ”
Ln view of his own approaching dust-.shaking-
off, and of the correspondence printed in another
column, the author of “ Pursuing Literature in
Australia” might more fitly write “Pursued by
Literature out of Australia.” Local Literature
just now is not a Grace who momentarily evades
you, but a vengeful Fury who chases you with a
whip.
The Bookfellow.
EVENING.
ovenm ff> aqd an orange blaze
The western heaven fills ;
Deep blue o’erhead : a purple haze
Dreams o’er the eastern hills.
The air is fresh, and cbol, and sweet;
The tender wave doth sigh ;
And one by one the starry fleet
Sails out into the sky. ”
Oh, look ! the moon which, pale and dim,
We gazed on yesternight,
Now lifts above our planet’s rim
A disk of yellow light.
Before I loved, my life was wan
As the moon of yesternight;
I saw thee, and like yonder moon,
My life grew warm and bright.
There glides a meteor ! Round the sun,
A dull mass it did fleet,
Until the warm earth drew it down,
And claspt it into heat.
And Love ! my life, both dark and drear,
Roll’d round without an 1 aini,
Till in thy beauty’s atmosphere
It kindled into flame.
Place o’er my heart thy white hand small !
To its true pulse’s tune
Thy lustrous hand doth heave and fall,
AIS on the sea the moon.
Oh, bend on me thine earnest eyes !
• So look, and do not move ;
And let the stars of Feeling rise
In the high heaven of love.
A. J. EVELYN.
SUNS .TS.
1.
The setting sun’s red round of light
Glows through the rifted cloud afar ;
So the fierce eye of warrior-knight
Hath glared above his vizor-bar.
Oh ! like a stag that dies at bay,
He sinks in his unconquer’d pride;
And strikes, aslant his golden rage
Into the heaven on either side.
He drops below the sphered world :
The tinted vapours o’er him wave;
Like banners gloriously unfurl’d
O’er hero going to his grave.
The great deeds of the hero burn,
And all his cloud of death is bright;
And glittering stars arise and turn
Themselves around the sunless night.
11.
The orange west is sombrely
O’erdusk’d with eve, and slowly die.
The purple lights along the sea
And pale pink tints within the sky.
And now the darken’d wave doth sigh :
It waits the stars, it waits the moon ;
For they will in its’bosom lie
All night, and they are coming soon.
And ah ! from me a light is fled:
The heart is chill within my breast;
And many purple hues are dead
In which at morn my life was blest.
And in my soul a longing lies
To hail thy forehead’s moony gleam.
The dawning of thy starry eyes,
Lady unknown ! of whom I dream.
in.
All hush’d in deep foretaste of night,
Great Nature’s heart is full of rest:
In languid length of yellow light
The sunset lolls along the west.
Still is the sea, and seems to say—
“ Come ! find in me a quiet grave."
There thickly fall the shadows gray,
And here pale sallow streaks the wave.
We gaze upon the azure steeps
’Neath which the rosy vapours lie;
And in the spirit’s deepest deeps
We feel that we can never die.
We gaze upon the beauteous whole,
Made up of sky, and land, and sea;
And stirring strong within the soul
We feel our immortality.
IV.
The slant and serried sunbeams crowd
Along the grassy lawn :
A low dense wall of sable cloud
Around the west is drawn.
Low down the lord of day is whirl’d;
The sky is hlue and bare :
The rays glance upward from the world,
And strike into the air.
Low down, behind that bank of night,
The lord of day is driven—
And lo ! a blaze of golden light
Goes flashing up the heaven.
So, when within some city’s lines
A King doth drive his oar,
The banners wave, the armour shines :
The gleam is toss’d afar.
A. J. EVELYN.
E VELVETS GRAVE.
Oh, dropping cloud, that corneth near this spot,—
Let fall thy gentlest showers his grave above;
Oh, grateful wind, that sighs and tarries not, —
Gather all sweets and shed them here, in love;
Oh, birds of gentle song, lament the lot
Of him whom Sorrow lov’d not to reprove ;
And ye, sweet flowers, of sad repining breath,
Spring o’er the Poet’s grave, regretful of his death.
Ah, stricken deer, pierced by a viewless dart, —
Ah, tender soul that could not bear the sky,—
A gust hath shatter’d that fine harp, thy heart;
No more its strings shall echo mournfully
The music which was of itself a part:—
The shatter’d strings within the darkness lie, —
Darkness and silence, void and desolate, —
Where Love sits weeping at the shadowy gate.
Up on the breezy hills at early morn,
Thou shoulast be with me wandering in the
. light,—
Marking the sun-shafts pierce the depths forlorn
Of wild ravines profound, —even as the might
Of Truth, which moves in love, and knows not
scorn,—
May make the d -pth's of rug 70 1 ID-or bright:
Or, at the noon of day, by tiiymy rill
Thou shouldst repose, and dream a poet’s fill.
Ah, me, lost brother ! with a vain regret,
I mourn thy loss, —and yet some comfort find,
lhat thou hast pass’d from all the pain, and fret,
Ajj4 toil, and hopeless anxiousness of mind, —
Which wrap a friend,—thou thought’st a foe,—
even yet
Braying with naked breast the untemper’d wind :
-He treads thy shadow with a hasty stride :
The Dead and Li ving soon rest side by side.
August, 1857.
HENRY HALLORAN.

NOTICE.
A NY LADIES coining in my Hall after this date
A- with their under-garments hanging off and hair
deranged in any way, also ladies coming in the Hall
with aprons and hats on, I will have them expelled
from the Hall, as I believe in my Hall being respect-
able ‘ (Signed) W. HESLIN.

and she has to be expelled lest she wreck the re-
spectability of Bodangora Hall they still call her
a “lady.”

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
Pious William’s Lay.
Bill Psalmyell was a pious youth
Who early praise did win
By walking in the paths of truth
And shunning every sin.
He kept him ever to this rule
Whatever fate befell,
And always went to Sabbath-school
And learned his lessons well.
He never played at “ headin’-em,”
Or “Nap” or “ Yankee sweat,”
But quoted some sweet Gospel gem
Which proved them Satan’s net.
The tear of joy my eye bedims,
And all my bosom stirs,
For William, though au fait at hymns
Knew naught of guileful “hers.”
And though the Gospel Mail oft would
A fund of joy supply,
The evening female never could
Attract good William’s eye.
He blushed at Fielding’s, Zola’s names,
And hid their works from sight ;
When told Don Juan’s little games
He even wept outright.
At Milton’s tale divine his eyes
O ice stole a timid look ;
But at the scene in Paradise
He blushed, and closed the book.
These for the Bible he forsook,
And turned to it with joy,
And from that high, immortal Book
Drew sweets that never cloy.
And everywhere that William went —
Like Mary’s lamb, you know— _
Be sure his little Testament
Was always bound to go.
“ My soul but throbs to gain,” said he—
“ When this poor body dies,
A home beside the Jasper Sea—
A mansion in the skies.”
Keristian William never sought
To hoard a golden pile ;
Oh no ! his spirit scorned the thought
Of things so base and vile !
He deemed not that the metal bright
Was life’s sole balm and honey ;
The only coin this godly wight
Stored up was sanctimony.
And when for bread a beggar sued,
He said : “You know ’tis true
God’s ravens brought Elijah food ;
They’ll bring it unto you ! ”
And mark what good may come to those
Who live a life devout—
Who don the Christian’s holy shoes,
And know their way about.
A banker who had heard of Bill
And his Keristian work,
Required a pious youth to fill
The post of junior clerk.
Quoth he to Bill, without delay,
“ You’re just the one I seek ” ;
And William was engaged straightway
At thirty bob a week.'
The president of that same bank
Where William was a clerk
Saw he deserved the highest rank
For his Keristian work.
“ With such religious, godly men,
Pm sure there is no fear,”
So said the president, who then
Made William his cashier.
Alas! that mankind weakly kneels
To the seductive pelf !
Alas ! that piety conceals
The native hue of self !
Good William strayed from virtue’s rank
And over Honor’s bounds :
Good William shortly eased the bank
Of twenty thousand pounds.
He slipped the peelers diligent,
He laughed the “ D’s ” to scorn,
He dusted off the continent
And left the bank to mourn.
And so my moral proves the truth
Of other pious lays :
It is that Vice meets bitter ruth,
But Virtue Always Pays !
F. H. WYSE.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, Postal Note,
One Pound Note, or stamps of any Australian province,
you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every
week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, managing director.
Literary communications to be addressed to J. F.
Archibald.
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that toe
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Blade and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
'any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, APRIL 14, 1900.
The Appeal unto Caesar.
CiESAR, in this case, is the Judicial Com-
mittee of the British Privy Council. It is
the last Court of Appeal in “ colonial ” cases
—an appeal court situated 16,000 miles away,
and therefore painfully slow in its dealings,
and hideously expensive, and difficult of
access—in fact, to 99 per cent, of the people,
quite impossible of access. Also because of its
remoteness and consequent expensiveness
it is bound to remain impossible of access,
so far as 99 per cent, of the people are con-
cerned. The Australian Federal Convention,
when drafting the Commonwealth Bill, wisely
recognised that the great essentials in regard
to a Court of Appeal are that it should be
near enough and cheap enough to make it
attainable by others besides the enormously-
rich. It also recognised that until the speed
of a fast steamship can be accelerated to 600
miles an hour the British Privy Council,
however perfect in its wisdom, can’t be a
satisfactory Court of Appeal. Therefore
it devised the idea of a Federal High
Court, and over this matter the Com-
monwealth Bill seems to be fast coming
to grief. For the British Government
insists on retaining the costly and inacces-
sible Privy Council as the final court in
Australian matters, and in this respect it is
backed up by some so-called Australians like
George Republican Dibbs, who holds that
his native land, and the land which has
always supplied him with some kind of a
livelihood, is an inferior and degraded kind
of place, which can’t even manufacture its
own justice on the premises. So George
Dibbs leads the way in protesting against the
fitness of his countrymen to interpret their
own laws, and the absurdity of trusting to
his countrymen’s intelligence and sense of
honour, and the necessity of keeping them
in a state of judicial tutelage. Which, con-
sidering all these countrymen have done for
the painfully-mediocre Dibbs, is somewhat
rough.
The reasons why the Convention’s idea of
a Federal High Court sitting in Australia
should be adhered to are numerous and ob-
vious. .In several ancient English charters
“delaying justice” is specified as one of
the most heinous of crimes, and over
and over again the English people made
bitter old feudal tyrants sit up and
swear, among other things, that they would
delay justice ”no more. So far as Aus-
tralasia is concerned, the Privy Council does
delay justice ; being 16,000 miles away it
can’t help doing so. It is alleged that the
justice it serves out is pure, unbiassed and
reliable, and so it is ; but just because it is
so pure and reliable it is the worse shame
that it should be denied to 99 per cent, of
litigants. Only the very rich can avail
themselves of it. It is only available in a
very small proportion of cases, where more
than a certain sum is involved. It is only of
use to those who can afford to wait for the
necessary slow processes of a tribunal at the
far ends of the earth. And even if it could
be made cheaper, and a stupendous improve-
ment in the means of locomotion could make
it more accessible, so that it wouldn’t be
necessary to mark 3500 guineas on Isaacs’
brief when next he goes to England to
appear on behalf of the Melbourne Tramway
Co. as compensation for a compulsory
absence of six months—even if it could be
brought within three days of Australia—it
would still be wholly unsatisfactory. The
Privy Council is a court of appeal for the
Empire in general outside Great Britain,
and if, by any impossible possibility, it could
be made available to the poor litigant as well
as the rich one, it would only mean that the
Court of Appeal would be so overcrowded
with cases that it would take eight or ten
years to get a decision, and the way into the
hall of justice would be lined by the bones
of the weary ones who had died of hope
deferred.
* • * •
So, as a matter of necessity, the poor man
has no Court of Appeal. If a poor man and
a rich one litigate over a matter large enough
to be carried before the final court the poor
man has one chance and the rich man two.
Australia wants a court which will be near
enough and cheap enough to give the poor
litigant a chance, and which will have
time enough to deal with both classes.
It wants a whole court of final appeal
—surely one such court isn’t too much
for a continent and some adjacent islands
—not a mere fragment. If the present
institution doesn't give good justice, then the
system whereby litigants are dragged all that
way to be defrauded of their rights is an
infamy. If it does give good justice (and in
the main it is probably about the fairest
tribunal on earth), then the system which
puts such justice so far away that it is im-
possible. of access to the great bulk of the
community is also an infamy. The excel-
lence of the justice which is served out to
the rich makes the excellence of the justice
which is denied to the poor so much the
worse a wrong. Even if a local Court of
Appeal, near enough and cheap enough to
make it attainable by the poorer classes,
were established, it would do very little real
good unless all recourse to the Privy Council
were abolished. The distant institution—
the one whose slowness and costliness break
down the poor claimant—would still be ap-
pealed to by the rich man as a means of
delaying things till the poor man was dead,
or “ broke.” Of course, when the poor man
lost his case he might haul his rich antago-
nist before the cheaper local tribunal of
appeal. But if the local tribunal didn’t
serve out as good law as the British one it
would again be a case of good law for the
rich and an inferior class for the poor. And
if it did serve out equally good law—why
have any appeal unto Cacsar ? Why give
the rich man the option, out of pure malice,
of dragging his less wealthy antagonist to a
court a whole globe away to get no better
law, and mostly to break his heart by long
delays ?
* * * *
The question at issue, in fact, may be put in
a sentence : Even if the justice of an Austra-
lian final court would necessarily be worse
than that of an English one—if the person of
English descent in Australia is necessarily a
rogue or a fool as compared with the person
of English descent in England, and G. Dibbs
and his countrymen are to be viewed with
suspicion as a race of ignorant or unprincipled
degenerates—is it better to have a moderately
good court of final appeal available for the
many ? Or is it better to have a super-
latively good one available for the few,
and none at all for the rest ? The
Bulletin reckons it is better to put all
litigants on an equality as far as can possibly
be done, and chance the consequences. It
has no wildly-exaggerated opinion of its
own countrymen, but it doesn’t think ill
enough of them to measure them all by G.
Dibbs and the one or two other Tories who
recently protested against the proposed
Federal High Court. And it believes in
following English precedent—for once, any-
how. The English people planted their
final tribunal in their own capital not more
than 600 miles from the remotest part of
their islands—not in Kerguelen or Esse-
quibo—and if possible we should plant ours
in our own capital to be like them. They
would shriek with horror if it was proposed
that they should travel 16,000 miles for final
justice, and we should shriek also. They
have protested for centuries against the evil
policy of needlessly delaying justice, and we
should be guided by their good example and
protest too, unless the old English sense of
justice has died out in Dibbs’s country.
Dibbs and Want and M‘Laur.in claim
that the Australian would be deprived of his
sacred rights as a British subject if he were
not allowed to appeal to a judicial body
sitting in another country at the farthest end
of the globe, but had to put up with a mere
local institution. The proposition suggests
something akin to insanity. The Englishman
has to put up with a local tribunal. He has no
appeal to a judicial body in another land
at the far side of the globe. He has to put
up with the final judgment of a body com-
posed of his own countrymen, whether his
own countrymen have deteriorated or not.
And—to argue the question on grounds of
Toryism and precedent—what better is the
Australian than the Englishman that the
system which is good enough for the English-
man is not good enough for the Australian
also ?
* • * *
It is claimed that the present system fur-
nishes a “link” between “the colonies”
and the mother-land. This paper, at all
events, has serious doubts about the value of
that link. To 99 persons in 100, or, perhaps,
999 in 1000, the link only amounts to this—
that the last Court of Appeal is hopelessly
out of their reach should they ever want to
use it. So far as concerns the vast majority
in Australia there is an equally good link
between this country and France or Chili or
Japan, for they all have courts of final
appeal which are no more useless to the
average Australian litigant than the present
British Court is. For most people the alleged
link isn’t a link, but a breakage. Still if the
British Government insists that the judges
of that final tribunal shall be English
there is a way out—not much of a way,
certainly, but a way that is better t han none.
Let Britain send out judges to sit in the
Federal High Court at Australia’s expense.
They could administer as good law here as in
England, for wisdom isn’t like beer which is
liable to go bad and blow the cork out on the
voyage. So the link would still be preserved,
and yet the final source of equity and justice
would be brought within reasonably con-
venient reach of every Australian, and at
the same time brilliant but consumptive
British jurists with only one lung would be
provided with a valuable sanatorium. It
would be a bad compromise, but still it would
be a great improvement on things as they
now are.
* * * *
Yet whether the compromise be better
or worse, if the British Ministry insists on
altering the Commonweath Bill in any par-
ticular its action will be a grievous outrage
on the alleged principles of Representative
Government. The Bill is the work of the
Australian people’s elected representatives,
and it was accepted by the Australian people.
The British Government and Parliament
no more represent the Australian people
than the Turkish Government represents the
British people. The Australians have no
hand in creating one, and the British have
just as much in creating the other. How-
ever democratic may be the system whereby
the British Government is created by the
British people, that Government is, in its
veto on Australian legislation, as much an
outside despot as were the Spanish kings in
their dealings with their one-time South
American colonies—the despotism is far
milder, but the principle of self-government
is abrogated just the same. This aspect of
the case, however, is a mere casual matter.
The veto exercised, nominally by the Queen,
but really by the British Ministry—a Minis-
try which is answerable to a Parliament in
tha election of which Australia has no voice
whatever—squashes the beautiful principle
of self-government every day of its exist-
ence, and Australia is accustomed to the
situation. All the same, the action of Dibbs
and Want and M‘L auk,in in urging an
outside power—for the British Parliament
is as much an outside power to us as
the Japanese one, for the sufficient reason
that we have no more voice in creating one
than the other—to overrule the prin-
ciples of self-government in this matter
was a needless act of disloyalty to Aus-
tralia. The person who invites any
outside authority to suppress the will of
the people of his own country with a sword is
commonly called a traitor. The same name
should apply to the person who urges the
same authority to do the same thing with a
pen or any other tool, weapon, implementor
utensil. The Australian Dibbs, urging a
British Government to overrule the will of
the Australian majority in Australia, isn’t so
very distinguishable from a South African,
of British descent, inviting a Boer Govern-
ment to do something the same in Cape
Colony. Each is inviting an external
authority to sit on and thwart and humiliate
his own people.
The Great Fake.
The truly wonderful ex-Premier George Reid
has resumed writing to the Sydney papers about
his awful deficits. George R. claims, as usual,
that he never had a deficit, and raves greatly
about the aspersions cast on his financial charac-
ter. The following extract is a beautiful reminis-
cence of the shuffling George we all used to
know: —
This false and injurious imputation I am determined to
have fully exposed, if Mr. Lyne does not run away from
appointing the commission. It is arrived at not by
straightforward “book-keeping” on the part of Mr.
Lyne or the Auditor-General, but on the contrary, by
preparing statements at variance with law—statements
which Mr. Lyne must by this time know to be at variance
with the books of both the Treasury and the Audit Office
since the new system was established in 1895. It is
arrived at by violating the express provisions of statute
law both as to the finding of an old deficiency which
Mr. Lyne and the Auditor-General fasten on my
shoulders, although it existed before I took office
(the Treasury Bills Deficiency Act of 1895), and, as to the
establishment of a new system of keeping the public ac-
counts, starting from Ist July, 1895, with a clean slate
(Audit Amendment Act of 1895). All the confusion and
error which Mr. Lyne and Mr. Rennie have produced
between them have arisen from those causes. They will
not recognise the law, and they put old deficiencies oh
the clean slate, in defiance of the law ; and only by dis
figuring the clean slate of 1895-9 with tbs' old deficiency
of 1891-5 can they produce the elements of attack upon
me and upon the public credit.
* * » *
The Bulletin, for one, is pleased to say that it,
at all events, won’t “recognise the law.” Said
law—the law that Reid had the ineffable cheek
and his followers the unspeakable folly or servility
to pass through Parliament—is the cause of all the
trouble. Reid’s plea is that when he passed a
law which gave him a chance to assert that in his
accounts two and two would be five, it is a wicked
imputation on his character to say that two and
two are not five. But two and two remain four
just the same. Reid arranged : (1) that his own
deficit from 3rd August, 1894, to 30th June, 1895,
should not be charged against him, and should
not be his deficit at all, but should be put into
what he called the old “ Dibbs deficit ” ; (2) that
the year to 30th June, 1896, should not be charged
either with the unpaid accounts left over from the
previous year, or with its own unpaid accounts
which were carried forward to next year, and by
sticking to this process every year’s unpaid
accounts are now charged to next year; (3) he
faked the accounts by a new system whereby the
half-year’s interest on the public debt for, say,
Ist January to 30th June, 1896, instead of being
charged to the year ending 30th June, 1896,
according to the old practice, was charged to
the next financial year commencing Ist July,
1896 ; (4) by altering the date of the financial
year he dodged the payment, on paper, of
nearly six months’ contributions to the sinking
funds. Also he did many other strange fakes
all of which were quite legal by the law which he
himself passed. But 120 laws wouldn’t alter the
fact that the 11 months’ deficit to June, ’95, was
Reid’s own deficit, and that the interest due from
January to June, ’96, was the interest for that
half-year and not for next half-year, and that two
and two make four in the same old way.
* 4 * *
Georoe Reid in his own defence grows more
and more curious as he goes on :
The new system began on Ist July, 1895, so that on 39th
June, 1899, four years were completed. In his Budget
speech, Mr. Lyne admitted there was a credit balance on
each of those four years, as follows (Hansard, 6th Decem-
ber, 1899, p. 2990)
Credit balance, according to Mr. Lyne :—1895-6,
£274,878 ; 1896-7, £55,099 ; 1897-8, £62,364 ; 1898-9,
£44,664.
On these admissions, what becomes of the alleged de-
ficiency ?
Now, I call the Auditor-General, and quote from his
annual reports, except as to the last year, for which his
report is not available. I take for that year the figures in
Mr. Lyne’s statement :
Credit balance at eud of financial year.—lB9s-6,
Auditor-General’s report, p, 151, £349,878 ; 1896-7, ditto,
ditto, p. 136-7, £130,099 ; 1897-8, ditto, ditto, p. 152-3,
£62,363 ; 1898-9, Mr. Lyne’s Budget Speech, £147,702.
Where, on these figures, can the deficiency be traced ?
Again, if we refer to the Government Statist, and look
at Part L, “ Public Finance,” of the Statistical Register
for 1898, p. 3-4, what is the account given of the four
years, 1895-9?
1895- —Credit balance, 30th June, 1896, £300,535
1896- —Credit balance, 30th June, 1897, £84,831
1897- —Credit balance, 30th June, 1898, £92,274.
1898- —Credit balance, 30th June, 1899, £74,674.
In those pages, Mr. Coghlan obeys the law, and keeps
the accounts according to law, and makes up no returns
that mislead people who do not know the law and the
facts. Hence he shows the finances from Ist July, 1895,
under two distinct heads : (1) “ Old Deficiency Account”;
(2) “ New Account Under Audit Act Amendment Act of
1895. All the trouble arises from a mixing of the two
accounts by those who ought to know better.
By Reid’s own figures (those he quotes from the
Government Statist) he had two surpluses and
two deficits in four years. In 1896 he says he had
a surplus of £300,535. In 1897 it was reduced to
£84,831 (deficit for that year, £215,704). In 1898
it rose again to £92,274 (surplus for 1898, £7443).
and in 1899 it dropped again to £74,574 (another
deficit of £17,700). But the most joyful humor
lies in the persistence with which Reid
starts all his calculations from the begin-
ning of his famous fake at Ist July, 1895.
Apparently wild oxen won’t drag him into making
any reference to his doings for the 11 months
before that period. Yet, according to the Govern-
ment Statist (N.S.W. Statistical Register for
1896, pages 764 and 786), in the six months from Ist
January to 30'th June, 1895, Reid had a stupendous
shortage, all his own, of £383,669 which he put
away into the “Dibbs deficit” where it remains
to this day. And since then no power on earth
can make the great fakir even mention that pain-
ful epoch. All his history starts from Ist July,
1895. The Great Fake is to him what the Great
Trek is to the Boer or the Hegira to the Moham-
medan. There is nothing before that—nothing
worth mentioning at least. In fact, Ist July, 1895,
was the date of Reid’s Great Trek. On that day
he trekked right out of the multiplication table
into a wilderness of Zulu arithmetic, where he has
been adding up accounts with an assegai ever
since.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Premier Seddon’s “More Friendly
Attitude.”
The following cablegram from Maoriland Premier
Seddon recently appeared in the Sydney dailies,
with joyful headings, such as “Mr. Seddon again
Explains,” and “ A more Friendly Attitude” :
I am pleased to hear that the misunderstanding which
has arisen has been removed. You have my assurance
that there is nothing unfriendly towards your colonies in
the attitude New Zealand has taken up. Any course I
have advised only caused by looking forward to good of
colonies for years to come, and providing for emergencies
that may arise. Take, for instance, our wish that your
Federal Court of Appeal may be open to New Zealand.
There cannot be any reasonable objection to this nor to
the power to be given in the Enabling Bill for reciprocity.
I am glad to hear a conference has been agreed upon
between the Federal delegates from Australia and our
Agent-General, and 1 have every hope that the little diffi-
culties in London will be smoothed over and an arrange-
ment made which will be satisfactory to all the colonies.
This seems to be exactly the same attitude with
snuffle added to injury. Premier Seddon explains
that the alterations he wants made in the Com-
monwealth Bill are quite small and harmless and
unobjectionable. They may be so, or they may
not ; but even if they are as harmless as a glass of
boiled milk that doesn’t make the least difference
in the fact that his way of asking them his
proved Richard Seddon to be a man w.io
ought to be given his dinner in the scullery.
He didn’t request the Australian provinces
to insert the allegedly quite small and
harmless things he wanted when the Bill was in
process of manufacture, but preferred to go to the
British Government and ask it to compel Aus-
tralia to agree to these things. That isn’t a cour-
teous way to ask for anything. He knows per-
fectly well that the Commonwealth Bill is. the
result of five years’ stupendous labor. If it is


accepted by the British Government exactly as it
stands then the labor is finished. If it is altered
in any way then it isn’t the same Bill which the
five Parliaments and the five provinces accepted,
and the work of getting it through the five Parlia-
ments, and possibly (though this is uncertain) that
of getting it passed by the five communities per
medium of the Referendum, must be started again
at the cost of great delay and toil and cash. Of
course this toil may have to be done in any case,
if the British Parliament alters the Bill, but
that doesn’t in the least affect the fact that
Premier Seddon was quite ready and willing
to pull down all the stupendous work that
has been done, solely to suit his own whim
—his whim being that he chose to apply to the
British Government at the eleventh hour, instead
of to the Australian Convention somewhat earlier.
If, as he alleges, the alterations he wants are such
as Australia can’t possibly object to, his conduct is
all the more gratuitously mean and shabby. He
says in effect: “I knew you would have granted
these concessions if 1 had asked for them at first,
when they could have been granted without incon-
venience. Knowing that, I preferred to wait
until your work was finished, and then demand
that it be pulled down again so that I might get
what I could have got before without causing any
such trouble.” And if, as he alleges, they are very
small alterations, it is the more disgraceful that he
should make so much trouble over them. When
a man wants a parcel out of the bottom of a ship,
and waits till 1000 tons of cargo are put on top of
it, and then demands that the cargo be removed to
let him get his parcel, he is apt to be called an
animal frequently mentioned in Scripture, and
never once mentioned respectfully. And when,
by way of assuming a more friendly atti-
tude, he explains (1) that it is a very small
package, (2) that he knew all the time that
he wanted it and waited of deliberate and set
purpose, and (3) that he was quite sure there
would have been no objection to giving it to him,
and he made all this extra work just out of mean-
ness and cussedness and mischief —when he ex-
plains these things by way of holding out the
right hand of friendship the person addressed
often feels inclined to put some decomposed sub-
stance in the right hand of friendship as a means
of expressing feelings that are too vast for words.
■ ♦
A Matter of Broad Principle.
Dear Bulletin. —A person signing himself
“Loyalist”—a genuine, all-wool Jingo—holds
forth thus in Launceston (Tas.) Examiner
“Can’t we have our own opinion?” says one. My
answer is, “ No.” The time for individual opinion is past.
Those brave men who are fighting for the Empire and
staining the broad veldt with their blood have but one
opinion, and everyone who remains in the quiet of his or
her home should have the same opinion. The utterance
of Mr. Tucker covers the whole ground—“ We are at war ;
which side are you on ? ”
That sort of letter lays down a fine, broad general
set of principles—one that has nothing to do with
the rignts or wrongs pf this particular war. Said
principles may be enunciated thus :
(1) When our country (whatever country that maybe)
is at war with anybody on any grounds whatever, it isn’t
sufficient to hope that our country will win, and to assist
it to win if assistance is required.
(2) It is also necessary to say that our country is in the
right, whether it is or not. When it isn’t in the right it
is necessary to be a liar in order to be a real loyalist.
(3) More than that, it is necessary to think that our
country is in the right, whether it is or not. Even being
a liar isn’t sufficient. When our country isn’t in the right
it is necessary to be an ignoramus as well.
(4) Therefore, when Spain carried on a war of extermi-
nation against the Dutch Protestants, and burnt them
alive when it caught them, and when the Turks waded in
to abolish the whole Christian population of Greece, and
in all the Other horrible and unjust wars that have filled
the earth With blood and bones and infamy, every honor-
able and intelligent Spaniard or Turk, or other person
concerned, should not have protested or opposed the war
in Any way. He should have simply said, ‘ ‘ My country is
at war. The war is a just one because my country wages
it. The methods pursued are all right, because they are
my country’s methods.”
For isn’t it the duty of every loyal person to say
aiid believe that his country is always right when
it is at war ! And if he has the misfortune,
as some people have, to belong to a country
which makes war on unjust grounds, or does
it in a horribly barbarous manner, it is his
duty, not to try to reform its methods
(because he couldn’t put his country right
without first saying that his country was
wrong, which would be shameful disloyalty), but
simply to be loyal and back it up in its injustice
or barbarity. Even the Boer is bound by “ Loyal-
ist’s ” logic to hold that his country is right in this
war, whether it is or not. And seeing that it is
his duty to say that his country is right and to act
accordingly, why do the Jingo papers curse'him so
horribly for doing his duty ? —Yours, &c., T.

Melbourne Takes a Wash..
The prospect of the plague has stirred Melbourne
to sweep its front-doorsteps and to flush-out its
back lanes. The Yarra is beyond all hope of
cleansing, till the city is properly sewered ; but
sundry corporation tips, and the Merri Creek, the
Reilly-street and Hawthorn drains, Gardiner’s
Creek and the Moonee Ponds ditto are equally
discreditable to the local councils and their health-
officers. The worst of all these disease germinators
is Moonee Ponds Creek, which various parties
have been endeavoring to improve for over a
quarter of a century. It meanders lazily down a
gully between N. Melbourne and Footscray, and
is really a succession of slush-holes, mostly covered
with green slime. In the depths of the slush lie
and stew things that are better left unsaid
and matters which wouldn’t look well in print.
Decayed belltoppers with fantods and jim-jams
and unhealthy eels in them are about the nicest
things found in Moonee Ponds Creek. On the
adjacent banks and flats goats and cows
browse, thus enabling the local milk-vendors
to" see that the typhoid rate is pro-
perly maintained. The sanitary history of this
gutter forms an interesting story. There are
four councils concerned, and for a long time they
couldn’t agree about what should be done. When
they did, the Railway Department, which is also
interested, said it would see them further before it
would pay its share of the costs of improvement.
Then the Health Department took a hand, but
found it hadn’t power to compel action. Next the
Turne’r Ministry was deputationised so often that
at last, for very weariness, it started cleaning and
channeling the stream, but it didn’t allow enough
money to complete the job and it was thrown out
before it could vote any more. But at long last,
with the plague in view, the M‘Lean Govt, has
promised to find enough cash to finish the contract.
It is noteworthy that, among all the rich Vic-
torians who have tried, in the last quarter century,
to buy peace when they were dead or titles when
they were living by handing over big sums to put
up statues or to build more or less useless additions
to colleges, there wasn’t one who remembered that
he might buy fame, and be a public benefactor,
and save more lives than if he founded two whole
hospitals, by putting Moonee Ponds Creek in good
order at his own expense, and turning a supply of
water into it to keep it permanently clean. Yet
so many men give money to colleges, and so few
clean out a notoriously dirty creek, that the latter
device would bring twice as much fame at less than
half the oost.
A Burlesque Court of Appeal.
A bit of quaint imbecility from the Australian
Star :
Mr. Haldane, Q.C., may be thanked for a proposal
which promises to end the difficulty raised about the
Privy Council appeal. . . . This . . . lawyer and Liberal
statesman . . . proposes a court which may be of great
service to the Empire, and acceptable to all its peoples.
Instead of moving heaven and earth to maintain the
appeal to a court which is, possibly, slightly antiquated,
why not, says Mr. Haldane, erect a new court, which
shall be thoroughly representative ? Such a court would
be created by the fusion of the Judicial Committees of the
Privy Council and the House of Lords, and the amalgama-
tion of the resultant product with certain colonial Judges,
new gilt, and crowned for the position as life peers.
That sounds very feasible, and seems to promise a solu-
tion of a great difficulty; and subsequent general satisfac-
tion.
It is alleged that Alfred Deakin has expressed a
modified approval of this proposal, and if he has
Deakin doesn’t possess the brains he is popularly
credited with. The new idea has every one of the
disadvantages of the old one, and an extra one of
its own. It would still necessitate an elaborate
and wearisome alteration of the Commonwealth
Bill. It would still leave our court of final appeal
16,000 miles away, and by reason of its remote-
ness it would still be too slow and too expensive
to be of any possible use except to the richer
class of litigants. It would still be a court
which had to, hear appeals from all parts
of the empire, so that if it could be made
accessible to the poor as well as the rich
litigant it would be completely bogged by the
rush of business. And the new court with its new
name and its newly-gilt “Colonial” Judges,
would give no better justice than the old one—the
present brand of justice is good enough when one
can get at it. Also it would have one element of
foolishness which the present court is free from.
To send an Australian case to England to be
heard by English Judges is at least understand-
able. English Judges have a habit of being in
England. But to send an Australian case to
England to be heard by Australian Judges who
might as well hear it in their own country and the
case’s own country—to send the Judge to the
other end of the world and the case after him
when they both might as well remain liei'e—looks
like a bit out of a burlesque.
A Matter of Sentiment.
The news that the British naval authorities have
called tenders for carrying 2000 tons of Westport
(M.L.) coal to Albany (W.A.) and 1500 tons to
Sydney, has caused many coal-owners in their
rage to kick the waste-paper basket right into the
middle of next week. The idea of bringing Maori-
land coal to N.S.W., of all places, is felt to be
premeditated insult. Sydney Telegraph, how-
ever, makes the great mistake of taking up the
sentimental aspect of the case, in this fashion :
The reasons other than strictly commercial are con-
nected with the colonies’ recent liberal provision of men
and horses for the war in South Africa, purely voluntary
offers of help on which the question of expense was never
allowed for a moment to have the slightest influence, and
there is a feeling 1 amongst those more nearly associated
with the coal trade that a graceful return might be made
in the shape of British trade both here and at the scene
of hostilities.
This is just where the Sydney paper errs grie-
vously. Instead of bringing this aspect of the
case to the front, it should have heaped 2000 tons
of shale on top of it, and spread the tarpaulin
of reticence over the shale in order to prevent
inquiry. It is just that “recent liberal provision
of men and horses for South Africa ” that
damages N.S.W.’s cl „im. Before the war,
N.S.W. got practically all the money the
British Govt, had to spend hereabouts in the
way of coal purchases. But all the provinces
made such liberal provision of men and horses
that they all feel entitled to claim some of the
coal business in return —all of them that have
coal to sell. It is probably in recognition of
Maoriland’s liberal outpouring of men and horses
that the British Govt, feels almost boifhd to take
part of its coal trade away from N.S.W. and give
it to Maoriland by way of the “ graceful return ”
which the D.T. mentions. In fact, if Britain once
gives way to the virtue of gratitude, it will pro-
bably feel bound to take away a lot more of its
coal business from N.S.W., for Queensland and
Tasmania, as well as M.L., are coal-producing
provinces, and they all want to have their loyalty
recognised. If there had been no contingent, and
no war, and no loyalty to recognise, then N.S.W.
could go on the plain business plea that it supplies
the cheapest coal in Australia. But if it be.comes
a question of recognising Australasia’s loyal en-
thusiasm, then N.S.W.’s practical monopoly of
the business must go.

Editor Bulletin, —Apropos your assertion, many
weeks ago, that the Australian gold-miner was not
a Jingo : I was present, as an approving listener,
at Holman’s meeting at Warraderry rush, N.S.W.,
which, as almost the first public occasion on which
criticism of the Jingo policy has been delibera-
tively ladled out, deserves a deal more notice than
it got from any Sydney daily. The speech was
remarkable enougli as a bitingly clean and forceful
presentment of the Peace arguments, but the
meeting was infinitely more so. The three or four
hundred men on the field all rolled up. Chair-
man and speaker talked off a perch into absolute
blackness, as a strong head wind was neutralising
the primitive lighting resources of the rush. From
the back of the crowd I could only see the speaker
when someone in the front ranks re-lit a neglected
pipe. The miners stood for an hour and a half, in
Eiteh darkness, in the stiff cold wind (and it is
eginning to be very cold of evenings up hereon
the tablelandjin a solid mass, occasionally cheering,
but mainly listening like Supreme Court judges.
Holman treated us like a jury, listening to
counsel for the defence after long presentation
of the prosecution. The miners entered into the
spirit of that relationship with more than
judicial gravity. There was only one interjection.
In answer to some grave references to English
policy, “ You couldn’t say that -at Hobart!” was
projected from the edge of the crowd like a
missile. Holman is not good at repartee—at least
I have never heard him do anything special that
way up here —but out of the depths of his indigna-
tion at the taunt there came flowing a passionate
rejoinder which crushed the interruptor. He
was not at Hobart then—surrounded by larrikins
and drunken sailors—but on a N.S.W. gold-field—
among free men —his own friends and constituents
—to whom he was responsible and by whom he
must be judged —who would hear, him to the end
before they judged him—and “ thank God,” said
Holman, “ I can say it here!” It looks little
enough on paper, but in the darkness, in those
wild surroundings and before that audience, it
was most telling. Interruption ceased then, and
Holman pursued his theme pitilessly' to the end.
I have never seen an Australian meeting devoted
wholly to foreign policy before. It was completely
.successful here, and the concluding vote of confi-
dence, and cheers for Holman, and cheers for his
chairman (a prominent local “pro-Boer” who has
been boycotted out of Grenfell) were as real as
anything I ever saw. The entire experience was
as wholesome and refreshing as a summer breeze.
—M.

As a sample of magnificent assurance, it would be
hard to beat the cablegram of Dibbs, Want and
MTiAUKIN to the British Government, protesting
“on behalf of the 82,000 N.S.W. voters who
opposed the Commonwealth Bill ” against the
proposed abolition of appeals to the British Privy
Council. By that cablegram the three distinctly
imply that the 82,000 “No ” voters in N.S.W. are
against the abolition, of appeals to the British
Privy Council. And the three have no means of
knowing that they, or half of them, or a tenth, or
twentieth, or a ninetieth were against the Bill on
that ground. For all they know to the contrary
there may be 40,000 of them who regard the aboli-
tion of Privy Council appeals as the Bill’s one
good feature, and 41,000 more who are quite
indifferent. It would be no more insolent a
piece of assumption if some Dutch tailor or publi-
can telegraphed the cordial sympathy of the 82,000
N.S.W. anti-Federalists to President Kruger.
The Dibbs-Want-M‘Laurin triumvirate says
that the 82,000 object to “the taking away
of our rights as British subjects to appeal to the
Privy Council.” Among the old hardshell Tories
the fact that you can’t get final justice without
going 16,000 miles, and waiting a year or two, and
spending very large sums of money, is always
called “one of our inalienable rights as British
subjects,” and the offer to bring justice 15,500 miles
nearer is called taking away one of our rights.
There are people in this world who are so wedded
to old forms and methods that if anybody pro-
posed to deprive them of the sacred right of being
robbed, or kicked, or dog-bitten, or flogged, or
having dirty water poured over them, they would
say it was an outrage and the Constitoosh’n was
thereby endangered. Even the prospective loss
of the inalienable right of being damned drives
a section of the community to fury, and its
protests against the abolition of a material
Hell are painful to listen to.

John Forrest’s anti-Federal policy of Yes-No
has fallen with an unexpected shock on his own.
strongholds of Perth and Bunbury. The first
result of it was the Goldfields Separation move-
ment, which was so enthusiastically taken up that
all the regions westward as far as the 119th deg.
of longitude, and in the extreme south a patch as
far west as the 117th deg., are demanding sever-
ance. And when this country gets Separation its
trade will go to Esperance or Albany; and Perth
and Bunbury will go up the cold, unwholesome
spout. The prospect is so awful that new build-
ing contracts in Perth are being cancelled, and
properties in Perth, Fremantle, and Bunbury are
being offered at slump prices by scared holders
who want to get out before Big-JOHN’s capacity
for putting his foot in it reduces them to ruin.
And Big-JOHN, not knowing what to do or how
to untie himself from the hard knot he has got
into, and driven hither by Hackett, and thither
by Harper, and with the Separation movement
on his chest by night and the Ooolgardie water-
scheme by day, wanders back and forward in his
mind, and asks himself where he are, and replies
wildly that he dunno.

If the Sydney daily papers could conveniently and
profitably run more than two horrors at once, we
should be hearing a lot now of an impending
general strike of coal-miners. With war and the
bubonic plague on hand, though, it would be a
waste of good material to exploit the other ; so
the troubles of Newcastle are held over until some-
thing is wiped off the slate. The chances are, how-
ever, that by that time the miners’ best argument
will have a hole in it somewhere. Their position
is, absolutely, not much worse than usual, which
certainly isn’t saying much ; but they say that but
for the cussedness of the owners, it might be a
great deal better. The selling price governs the
hewing rate ; and the men’s chief complaint is
that the owners will not seize the chance now
offered by the war, and the rush for transport,
and the call-out of so many miners in England,
and a score or so other things, to put up the sel-
ling price. The owners say they can’t ; but they
will not meet the men to tell them why. And by
that refusal to confer, the owners, whether their
case is good or bad, utterly forfeit whatever
shreds of good opinion the public had of them.

Thus “Rooinek”in Sydney Daily Telegraph :
“As was stated weeks, months ago in this column, the
side which has most men, armaments, and money—
especially the money—wins every struggle that is suffi-
ciently prolonged.”
If you say a thing often enough you may get
people to believe it, but you don’t make it any
truer, and if “ Rooinek ” repeats his alleged maxim
till doomsday, History—and British History with
the rest —will go on saying it is a preposterous lie.
To begin with—in the long struggle between the
Greeks and Persians from Marathon to Arbela,
the Persians had most men, armaments and money
all through, yet "they were beaten. The Carthagi-
nians began with the same advantages ; but Rome
crushed them. Poor little Sweden beat the big,
rich German Empire in the Thirty Years’ War.
Little Holland beat Spain, which had the best
army, with some of the best generals in Europe,
and all the wealth of the Indies to boot. That
contest was “sufficiently prolonged” in all con-
science. Less than half the present Switzerland
knocked both the Duchy of Burgundy (then the
richest monarchy in Europe) and the German
Empire out of time. In the Seven Years’ War,
little Prussia fought France, Austria, and Russia
at the same time, and beat them. “Rooinek’s”
maxim is something like Napoleon’s alleged maxim
about “big battalions.” Napoleon said Providence
was on the side of the “strongest” battalions —
quite another thing. Skill and brains count for
more than men, arms, and money all put together.
One trifling side-result of the Boer war will pro-
bably he that “New Zealand” will think seri-
ously about officially changing its senseless name—
which, by the way, was long ago changed for it
by The Bulletin, and the province re-christened
kiaoriland. Efforts in this direction have
been made previously, but the authorities fell
asleep over the proposal, and on -awakening re-
garded it as a passing dream. The subject has
been revived by Mr. J. P. Maxwell, railway ex-
pert, who writes to the New Zealand Times : -
The old Dutch name of New Holland was, I believe,
discarded about 1817. In this colony, after 60 years as a
colony and over a century of what we may call occupa-
tion, we still adhere to the name of New Zealand, which
conveys only its Dutch origin to us, while it is uncouth
in form and sound. An alteration in name has
often been discussed, without any practical re-
sult. The present time seems a suitable one
for getting rid of our Dutch brand and for adopting a
more euphonious title. There is a name we are all
familiar with, and one by which the colony is already
pretty well known, viz., Maoriland, which has the ad-
vantage of combining the aboriginal with the Saxon ; it is
also adaptable to poetry, but no one, I fear, will ever suc-
ceed in jamming “New Zealand” into a decent line of
verse, and we must be wanting in imagination to have
put up with this Dutch monstrosity of a name for so long’.
Maoriland is also more compact and more easily written,
and space would be economised by using- it!
Writer goes on to suggest a newspaper poll, and
says Govt, would likely take notice of the expres-
sion of opinion elicited. “ Colonists might then
begin the new century and record a great his-
torical event in their life with a new name.”
Dear Bulletin.— Re the proposed Sydney crema-
tion of germ-harboring rubbish, let me commend
to the municipal solons the method by which
many mines are ventilated—the furnace air-shaft.
No two things resemble each other more than the
ramifications of a sewerage-system and the work-
ings of a coal-mine; so, why not have the
destructors so placed over the sewerage-system
that they would act as furnace air-shafts ? They
would then not merely allow the germ-laden
sewer-gas to escape, but their up-draft would
suck the foulness from the sewers and burn it, as
in many coal-mines. Then the destructor-ash
could either be incorporated (where sewage
filters or farms were convenient) with the sewerage
solids for manuring purposes—or dumped into and
carried away by the sewers.— Microbe.
A correspondent puts it all down to the rabbits:
Ed. Bulletin.— Re Hr. Millen’s theory of overstocking
in relation to Western N.S. W. land (“ A Vanishing Asset,”
B. 3/10/’00). Surely one holder could be found who is not
guilty of ruining his country by overstocking—men, for
instance, who have no interest to pay, and nothing to
gain by overstocking. Yet their country at present is no
better than their neighbors’! After 27 years’ residence in
different portions of the West, I challenge Mr. Millen, or
anyone else, to point out any country there that has not
deteriorated from 30 to 75 per cent, during the last 15
years. I know station horse-paddocks that are always
saved as much as possible, and on which there have been
no sheep for 15 to 20 years, yet they are no better than
the adjoining land. This shows that the cause must be
sought for in something against which the people were
unable to contend ; and that is the rabbit pest. The only
country which retains anything like its former capacity
is in small areas which were netted when the rabbits first
appeared, and on which they were never allowed to enter.
Years ago, in Victoria, where the rainfall is much better
than here, largo areas were abandoned solely owing to the
rabbits, and it was not until the Government came to the
rescue with wire-netting and other assistance that the
land could again be profitably occupied. The N.S.W.
Govt, was altogether to blame for the present trouble,
for it introduced the bonus system—the very thing that
had been condemned in Vic. When that system failed,
as was foretold, the Govt, simply did nothing else.

So far this year the ambulance has been called to
a Broken Hill mine every other day, on an
average. Of men killed outright the average has
been one a week. None of the accidents have
been sensational; just a casual stone has dropped
on a casual miner, or someone has fallen down
an opening left unguarded, or something lias
gone wrong with an explosive charge. These
ones and twos get little notice taken of
them. Killed in the morning, they are gene-
rally inquested the same afternoon, and the
funeral takes place next day. Only when
four or five are shattered at once is there a stir ;
and when the public aren’t too busy dodging the
plague or shouting “Rule Britannia” they raise
funds for their widows. The ones and twos, who
count up far more, leave widows and children the
same as the others, only more of both ; but they
can compete with Ah Chow for the washing, or—
or do other things. Wanted badly then, and
getting wanted worse every month, some sort of a
pension-fund—for miners, just for a start.

Just now in view of the Cecil Rhodes worship
which is rampant in Australian journalism and
elsewhere, the case of Burroughs v. Rhodes,
Jameson and others ought to be better known, in
order to appreciate what kind of a crowd the Char-
tered Co. gang really are. The matter was tried
recently before a British court, and, as reported in
the Law reports, is somewhat as follows: Bur-
roughs was a trooper in the Chartered Co.’s
Horse. His time was up just before the Jameson
raid. However, he was induced by the false
representations of Jameson and others to re-enlist
—the false representations being, inter alia, that
the women and children of Johannesberg were in
danger from the Boers, that the expedition in
which the troopers were about to engage had the
sanction of the Imperial Government, and that
they would be joined by Imperial troops when
they entered the Transvaal. On these represen-
tations Burroughs enlisted, took part in the raid,
was shot in the leg, lost his leg and was captured ;
was brought into Pretoria as a prisoner, was in
danger of losing his life for assisting in a State
burglary, and became amenable to the laws of his
own country for taking part in an attack on a
friendly State. As soon as he could get back to
England (minus a leg) he applied for help to the
Chartered Co. people and was hunted out. Then
he brought the action, claiming £3OOO damages.
Cohen, Q.C., and Lord Cecil (junior bar-
rister) argued that the J ameson crowd having been
convicted and punished as criminals, Burroughs
was particeps criminis, and, therefore, could not
recover, on the principle of the legal maxim that
an ill-doer could not profit by his own misdeed.
Judge Grantham, a well-known Jingo, could
hardly contain himself during this argument, and in
giving judgment described it as the most monstrous
argument he had ever heard advanced. He said
the learned Q.C. had referred to a case as
an authority on the maxim cited It was
a pity, said the Judge, that he did not
say what the case was, as it was rather
apropos of the one before him. He then quoted
it himself. Two highwaymen had quarrelled as
to the distribution of the plunder obtained by
them on Hounslow Heath. They brought an
equity-suit for an account and a division. The
result was that the solicitor for one of them was
fined £5O, the barristers on either side were struck
off the roll, and both the highwaymen were hanged.
He then gave judgment, against the Rhodes-
Jameson gang for the full amount claimed, with
costs.
The war-cablegram is often a humorous
and incomprehensible affair, but in a quaint way
this one puts up a record :
London, April 6.
Fifty cavalry-men of E squadron, belonging to Major-
General Lord Kitchener’s command, were guarding a farm
at Reit River on February 15, when they were surrounded
by 400 Boers.
The imprisoned troopers made a desperate resistance,
and loop-holeij the walls to use their carbines.
For three (lavs the besieged were foodless, and they
then caught and killed a goat.
All the horses died of starvation.
On the fourth day, the little garrison was completely
exhausted, and surrendered to the enemy.
These heroes had 50 horses to eat, and yet, after
being foodless for three days, they had to depend
on a goat —which makes no better victuals than a
horse. Obviously the horses were shut up with
the men; if they had been outside they wouldn’t
have died of starvation. Didn’t anyone think of
horse-steak ? And can’t one horse out of 50 hang
out without food till the fourth day ? What are
the daily papers giving us ?
