Robert Brough decided to give the
“Happy Life” a six nights’ run only, and “The
Physician ” was advertised to fill in the remainder
of the week. It is only a fragmentary week, any-
how, for the Good Friday Bun rises up like a
brown moon in the road, and that sombre, dark
indigestible is supposed to suspend all festivities.
Possibly Brough was right in thus cutting the
“ Happy Life ” short. ' It is a reasonably strong
piece, played admirably by a powerful combina-
tion, but it isn’t in the least happy. “ The Phy-
sician” isn’t especially happy, either, but it is
stronger and more original than its predecessor.
The intoxicated teetotaller who spends his fortune
in trying to suppress drink, and implores the pub-
lic in deadly earnest to swear off the brown
October ale, when he knows that he dare not turn
his own belltopper upside-down for fear a cataract
of snakes and jim-jams will pour out of it, is a
remarkable study. And Carne’s physician, the
strong, wise man against whom the drunken
reformer leans as a refuge from the strange
red animals that wander in his mind, is a
really great character. These are about all that
there are in the play. The girl that they both
love is only a little, commonplace, kindly vision in
a white frock and an innocent straw hat, beaming
with simple piety beside a well. She knows
nothing of life ; she isn’t tragic and she isn’t
powerfully dramatic, but she is the sort of girl
that a middle-aged and hardened co-respondent
would like to marry when he has realised that
barmaids are vanity, and ballet-girls are dust, and
that chappies and late hours and cards and cock-
tails are a weary kind of devilment at the best, and
the co-respondent business has lost its savour. And
the “Physician” has a quiet, restful, ordinary,
Story-book ending where the big world-worn man
marries the little confiding girl, and they are
happy. So many plays struggle to unusual end-
ings that a play with a usual ending is a trifle
more unusual than theother kind. After Brough is
done with the “ Physician ” the pretty, quaint little
Slav of “Lady Ursula” is to till in two nights at
ydney Royal (Saturday and Monday); then
there will be two nights of “ Lord Quex ” and
two more of the labored confusion of the “ Biix-
ton Burglary.”
* • • *
It is understood that Joseph Carne
and the Brough company part company next
month. The present scribe will deeply regret the
severance. Came is more than a fine finished
actor; lie is an unusual kind of actor as well. He
did more to replace Australia’s lost Titheradge
than any other man has done or seems likely to
do, and this country has a kindly feeling for any-
body who reminds it of that fine old favorite.
Carne looks like a man with a history—at least
the Young Female Person of this country so
decided, and when he lived at a Sydney waterside
resort she used to blew her coppers recklessly on
tram-fares merely to gaze on that inscrutable
countenance and those unfathomable eyes (that
was what she called them) on the wav out. There
is a certain class of character which Joseph C. can
do better than any other man in Australia—the
kind of character to which the masterful, reserved,
gentlemanly, battered physician of Jones’s play
belongs. He can do plenty of other things, too,
and do them admirably ; but as a proud, dignified,
man who has found out the tilings that Solomon
knew in his old age, when the glare of the Temple
was an ache in his dazzled eyes, and he loathed
the girlish giggle of liis 400th Moabitish wife,
Carne is unrivalled.
* * * sfc
“The Irishman” is to be produced on
Saturday at Sydney Lyceum, and there are
reasons for suspecting that it will be staged with
all the excellence that is usually associated with
the honored name of C. Holloway. As Friday is
a holiday it is probable that the dry and com-
bustible bishop in “ Defender of the Faith ” will
have blazed for the last time before this issue is
placed in the infected hand of the community.
The “Defender” has had a six weeks’ run, for
which the gorgeous clothing, and the combustible
character of the bishop, and the revelations as to
the ancient method of treating the insane, were
largely responsible. Probably, also, Dora Win-
ton’s singing of “Abide with Me” in Bedlam,
when she was crucified in white clothes with the
limelight on her, and her deranged mother
prowled round with much ironmongery and
couldn’t decide whether to brain her or not, kept
the drama going one extra week anyhow.
* * * *
Her Majesty's has been closed this week,
but Miss Nance O’Neil returns on Saturday to
play the soul-stirring drama of Camille.” The
company this time includes Thomas Kingston,
Harry Plimmer, and Miss Emily Hughes, so it
will probably be found a stronger aggregation than
the one which supported Miss O’Neil at the Royal.
Miss O’Neil looked very imposing there, partly
because she is really an actress of unusual capacity
and partly because even a giraffe looks a little
taller than usual when it is walking arm-in-arm
with a kitten. The arrangement is good for the
star, but it is sometimes decidedly bad for the
drama, and this time it has been arranged to give
the drama a better show.
* * * *
As already mentioned Alfred Dampier
returns to Sydney Criterion on Saturday, when
the consistently successful “Robbery Under
Anns ” will once more transpire under the gum-
tree of this bright and happy land. M‘Adoo’s
Georgia Minstrels are due at the Palace on the
same night.
* *• Ht *
Shortly after this is read by the public a
huge crowd will be pouring along to see the open-
ing of the new and glorified Sydney Tivoli. At
the Cri., whence Rickards moves this week,
Amann was on his last legs, and the only new
feature on the face of the dying season was Tom
Browne, the whistler. Thomas is a tall, chubby-
faced gent with red cheeks, and wears his hair
parted in the centre and plastered down hard on
both sides. As a whistler Browne is a man of
mark, especially when he does the “Swanee
River ” and gives his peculiar effect as if two per-
sons were whistling at once. As an imitation
trombone, flute, cello and banjo he is also a re-
markable Browne. Years ago, at Dunedin Exhibi-
tion, there _ was a whistler with one arm and one
leg, and this half-man used to toot as probably no
human being ever tooted before. Browne gets
nearer to the record of that amazing half-man
than anyone else this scribe ever heard.
* * *
This is an off week with Melbourne shows,
but on Saturday things will begin to look as in
the Saturdays of old before the Great Slump.
There have been signs that Melbourne could do
with more theatrical stuff than it was getting, and
the time has arrived for the city to increase its
weekly consumption. At the Princess’s old friend
Charles Arnold will make a second or third re-
appearance in Australia, after an absence of five
years, in his successful British character of Jones.
“ What Happened to Jones ” is said to be a pecu-
liarly funny farcical comedy which reveals Arnold
in a new bright light, and as a matter of fact it
was warmly received by the Boers a while ago
when they were not disposed to laugh at any Lon-
don comedy without great provocation, bio pre-
vious stage-play has been applauded by so many
people who didn’t know they would never live to
see another like it.
The veldt and the kopjes are strewn with the bones
Of many who witnessed “ What Happened to Jones.”
This is mentioned by way of encouraging Mel-
bournians to hurry up to the Princess’s and sample
the latest joke from South Africa, ere the uncer-
tainty of life is brought right home to them.
* * * *
The Alexandra is to break out again on
Saturday, under the enterprising management of
William Anderson, whose companies appear to be
all over the country of late. Alfred YVoods and
Miss Maud Williamson figure at the head of the
new co., and the opening drama will be a stupend-
ously warlike affair, “ For Queen and Country,”
wherein Britain’s loyal sons will humiliate Oom
Paul and avenge Majuba as much as possible. The
ad vertisement talks of storming Pretoria with 200
trained warriors and a full battery of mounted
artillery, so, judging from the recent popularity of
a drama which relieved Ladysmith with only 20
soldiers, “For Queen and Country” ought to
boom like a Long Tom cannon.
• * * •
J. C. Williamson’s designs on the Alex-
andra are not as reckless as was rumored. At the
termination of Manager Anderson’s season (seven
weeks) J.C.W. will take possession on a three
months’ lease, with option of renewals, but he is
not likely to attempt any expensive improvements
of the property until the possibilities of the
“Aleck ” as a fashionable theatre have been fairly
tested. Meanwhile Anderson’s productions of
melodrama on a big scale will have helped to
settle the question before his successor comes along.
* * m *
Dan, Tilly, and the infant proceed on their
domestic way at Melb. Royal. Also, the course
of Dan’s disguises runs smooth, and his method of
casting burglar Jim through the window of the
Den leaves nothing to be desired. “When the
Lamps are Lighted,” being a serio-comic shocker
that gives plenty of scope to the players, is easily
improved in the performance. The grief and woe
seem less tiresome than at first. H. Willard and
one or two other people have begun to appear
natural as a rule, which is a considerable ac hieve-
ment under the circumstances of the case, and the
tall young Sinclair, who takes a header into the
Thames, almost persuades one that he is about to
get wet. Whilst praising the artistic earnestness
of Sinclair’s plunge, The Bulletin must not for-
get to speak kindly of the invisible conspirator
who throws up a splash from the painted river. It
is a soulful splash.
* * * *
A “ classical sacred concert,” with tab-
leaux and talk in it, is to happen at the Royal on
Good Friday night. On the same night, at the
Town Hall, the Philharmonic Society will give a
performance of “ Elijah,” with William Pauli as
the prophet. Pauli is rather young for a prophet;
but this is a common objection to the modern
Elijahs, and Pauli has a Biblical name, anyhow.
Moreover, he is a fine singer, and the oratorio will
give him the chance to make a suitable farewell
appearance in Melbourne. There will be yet
another holy show to assist the public’s digestion
of their annual buns. At the Exhibition Building
the Ancient Druids will run a biographical Passion
Play and an incidental lecture by the Rev. Edgar,
price of admission only one shilling—a nimble,
secular shilling.
** * *
Amann, the German gentleman with the
adaptable face, is due at the Bijou on Easter
Saturday. News of Amann has trickled over to
Melbourne from. Sydney, and the arrival of Queen
Victoria’s countenance is anxiously awaited. The
Macarte Sisters are making their final efforts on
the uncomfortable wire, but Irene Franklin will
sing till further orders concerning the Golden
Gates. Everybody likes Irene. They would like
her even better if she didn’t alw'ays make a point
of introducing “Golden Gates” as her “trade-
mark,” and explaining that she will sing it to the
ladies and gentlemen with their “ kind attention.”
** * *
From San Francisco to The Bulletin :
Maggie Moore has come to ’Frisco and created a frost,
which was real mean of Maggie, for the frost was labelled
“ Australian,” and brought the land of the wattle into
theatrical disrepute. If there be one thing your genuine
San Franciscans abhor, it is the “ harrowing-up ” process.
They will stand any amount of stage idiocy if it be rushed
through and called a farce ; they will accept joyfully the
doddering, inane, domestic plot that ends in placid hap-
piness ; but morbid introspection and tragic finale they
will not endure. So, by way of cheerful catering, Maggie
offered them that lugubrious, long-drawn agony, “ The
Silence of Dean Maitland,” and S.F. declined to be
pleased. What is more, she brought her Dean with her—
Mr. Roberts, advertised as the great Australian Star.
Now, Roberts is a pretty fellow, cut out by nature
for the gentlemanly stage-lover, but hopelessly swal-
lowed up in the life-long remorse of the dreary
Dean. Two or three other Australians helped
him through his trouble, but the bulk of the co. was
made up of waifs and strays, hopeful amateurs and hope-
less professionals picked up in California, the whole being
styled, forsooth, “an Australian company,” whereat
those who love not Australia found matter for rejoicing.
Miss Madge Corcoran made a pretty Marion Everard, and
Winnie’s part was charmingly played, but at the hands of
the rest we suffered many things throughout some weary
hours. Also, a ghost arose, substantial enough to knock
Photo., Eden.
Mr. DAN. O'CONNOR, M.L.A.
Sydney’s “ Jaynial Dan ” O'Connor is again to the
fore, having been returned for Phillip division as
successor to Mr. Henry Copeland, now N.S.W.
Agent-General.
the affrighted Dean down, and altogether the audience
had a bad time. So had Roberts when the morning
papers appeared. Maggie reserved her own efforts
for “ Mrs. Quinn’s Twins,” written for her by Clifford
Robertijpn years ago, when her voice was more tuneful
and her foot lighter—an out-of-date piece, with nothing
but Maggie to account for its re-appearance. Neverthe-
less, she made a delightful, natural Irishwoman, and the
hearts of old admirers went out to her afresh. Moreover,
she had secured two admirable local twins : her pretty
piece, Osgood Moore, who had failed as Alma, proved to
be a charming Irish girl and a graceful dancer ; Miss
Madge Corcoran was a winsome heroine ; and Roberts—
Roberts, cut loose from his clerical trammels, was a
revelation. A debonair Irish squire, with a perfect
accent and a convincing manner ; and he wore his Court
clothes as one unto the manner born, a signal accomplish-
ment in a land where your actors in Court dress look like
lackeys. But the fiat, had then gone forth—the “ Austra-
lian co.” had been pronounced a failure, and Mrs. Quinn
and her twins played to empty stalls.
An old identity, a native of N.S.W.,
Thomas Tom, J.P., died suddenly at Parkes last
week aged 75. He was son of an old Western
identity, “Parson’’Torn, who lived at Guyong,
and brother of James Tom, who disputed with
Hargreaves the claim to be the first discoverer of
gold in N.S. Wales. The father was not an
ordained clergyman, but in early life, when clergy-
men were scarce, he filled the Wesleyan pulpit.
War-correspondent Donald MacDonald is
on his way back from South Africa. A rest at
Capetown after the horrors of the siege of Lady-
smith failed to restore his shattered health. The
best his friends now hope is that his splendid con-
stitution is not utterly wrecked. He is not the
man to give up unless thoroughly knocked out.
The Argus has tried to keep it a secret that he is
returning. Goodness knows why, as every one
must know he arrives in about a fortnight’s time.

In spite of the great increase in revenue, the
financial position of New' South Wales looks none too
beautiful. Total ordinary expenditure for the nine
months is £7,721,764, so that there is a deficit of £356,623
to pull up if the year is to end without a deficit, and there
are only three months to do it in. Still, the revenue has
increased £429,630, and the expenditure has only increased
£221,050, so the deficit is £208,580 less than it was at 31st
March, 1899, which is a promising feature. And the in-
crease of expenditure is wholly in the first quarter of the
financial year, for which quarter the Lyne Government
isn’t responsible. During the three months to 30th Sep-
tember, 1899, under the Reid Ministry, the expenditure
increased by £306,446 as compared with the corresponding
period of previous year. During Lyne’s six months of office
expenditure has been £85,395 less than for the correspond-
ing six months of previous year, so up to date Lyne comes
out remarkably well. The place where he comes out very
badly is in the loan expenditure. For the first quarter of
the financial year (Reid’s quarter) loan expenditure
diminished by £33,947, and in the last half-year (Lyne’s
half-year) it increased £260,815, as compared with corres-
ponding periods of previous year. So apparently Lyne
has been at the old miserable game of saving the ordinary
expenditure by charging more outgoings to loans, and has
been at it even worse than Reid used to be. In the past
nine months N.S. W. has gaily got through £1,722,922 of
borrowed money, so if it keeps going at that rate the
year’s addition to the public debt will be £2,297,330.
Victoria’s increase of revenue is fading away so
fast that by 30th June there will probably be none at all.
The cautious Turner anticipated a falling-off, as last year’s
figures were enormously swollen by the unexpected in-
crease in the death duties. Turner’s estimate for the
year was £7,156,225, so for nine months he expected to
receive £5,367,169, and on this basis the revenue is
£88,848 ahead. But the incautious Shiels, in his anxiety
to show that the country could afford to go on a loyal
contingent burst without having a deficit, and without
harassing any loyal person with extra taxation—that the
Lord, in fact, would send his ravens to feed the Boer-
shooter —joyfully re-estimated the revenue at £7,294,000.
This is the estimate Victoria is working on now, and at
that rate the nine months’ revenuo should be £5,470,500.
It is £14,500 behind, and getting further behind with
awful persistency. The luck of Jonah Shiels apparently
still sticks to him.
Queensland’s expenditure for the nine months
is £2,887,049, and the surplus, so far, is £609,502.
Queensland’s big surplus, however, mostly disappears
towards the end of the financial year, when its interest
bill falls due in a lump.
* * * *
The South Australian Government’s 3 per cent,
loan of £1,000,000 wasn’t violently rushed in London,
the amount offered being £1,298,700. The average price
realised was £94 10s. 9d. per £lOO. But £1 per cent, w'as
paid for underwriting the loan, and the interest starts
from Ist January, 1900, so another £1 ss. 9d. per cent, is
lost in accrued interest, and there are sundry expenses, and
the Government only gets between £9l and £92 for every
£lOO. It is really a3l per cent, loan or thereabouts, and
the 3 per cent, is only a pleasing fiction. Considering
that S.A. has always flatly refused to let the local investor
have 3 per cent, stock below £96 net, its joyous readiness
to let the British investor have it at £9l or £92 net is un-
accountable. S.A. has floated four 3 per cent, loans in
London, and the last two of them show a terrible slump.
But even at its best S.A. never got £9B per £lOO for a 3
per cent, security in London, though it has had the gall
to demand that price quite recently from the Crow'-eater
capitalist.

Messrs. W. & .T. Lempriere, Mining Machinery
Merchants, 506 Little Collins-street, Melbourne, report
that the Luhrig system of concentration is gradually es-
tablishing itself in the principal mills of Australia. The
latest huh rig vanner has a large capacity, and owing to
important improvements the life of the belt, &c., is greatly
increased. Vanners and spare parts are stocked in Melb.
and prompt delivery' is thus assured. A small testing
plant is available near Melbourne. Upon receipt of
necessary data, estimates for complete plants, including
batteries, rolls, trommels, jigs, classifiers, vanners, &c.,
will be proceeded with by competent engineers.*

Mr. Mepban Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
4cc., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
Inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, andall sorts of iron-work.*
* * * •
Mr. J. A. Wallace, tbe pioneer of tbe gold-
dredging industry in Australia, holds a long stretch of the
Sandy Creek, near Yackandandah, and intends installing
a ver’v large electrical plant to operate several dredges.
An order has just been placed with the Austral Otis
Engineering Co., of South Melbourne, to supply high-class
compound condensing engines, with Corliss valve-gear, of
400 h.p., together with boilers and flitings to operate the
first section of the plant. A duplicate plant to this will
be installed later and will work three electric dredges on
a stretch of about two miles of the creek. Each dredge
will have a capacity of about 150 cubic yards per hour.*

The Milburn Copper Corporation, conducting

Burbank’s, one of the few Westralian mines
which settled down to steady graft on just fair stone in
’97, still keeps going in a workmanlike way. ’98’s average
was loz. 7dwt. ; last month’s, loz. 3idwt. Annual yield
keeps steady at about 15,500oz., though, so far, things
are shaping' for more this year. The stone generally is
not big ; but lately one or two small “ makes ” of richer
stud have been broken into. Still, it has not got on the
div. list this year yet.

It is only recently that the Equitable commenced to oe
an “ American Giant,” as American giants are nowadays
—it was called one in 1891 certainly, but the kind of
giant it was then would now be reckoned rather a stumpy
institution. The Equitable’s total funds are £57,907,896,
and more than half of this sum has been piled
up since 1892—therefore it is interesting to speculate
as to the possible size of the institution in, say, 1950. The
chief visible drawback about the Equitable is the heavy
expense rate, though that seems inseparable from the
kind of business mostly done by American offices. The
percentage of expenses to the total income from premiums
and interest has been as follows since 1892:
1892 .. £lB 12 0 per cent. 1896 .. £l7 7 5 per cent.
1893 .. 17 6 5 „ 1897 .. 17 12 5 „
1894 .. 18 4 2 1898 .. 17 10 2 „
1895 .. 18 6 7 „ 1899 .. 19 5 1
* • • *
The Equitable, even if it assumes that its assets
will only realise 3 per cent, per annum interest in the
future, can show a splendid surplus of nearly £8,000,000
over all liabilities. The only questionable-looking item
is that always dubious and debateable paper asset
“ Deferred premiums on policies when paid semi-
annually r>r quarterly, deductable from amount in-
sured in case of claim, £473,820.” Only two Australian
offices reckon this an asset, and one of the
two is wisely swearing off the practice. Even rejecting
this, however, the Equitable has a huge surplus and its
solvency is guaranteed by State auditors and a State
valuation of the assets. Another casual disadvantage of
the American offices is that interest rates are lower than
in Australia ; in 1899 the Equitable realised only £4 4s. 3d.
per cent, on its average funds. But the Australian rates
are falling so steadily that it is probable the local offices
will also be down to £4 4s. 3d. in a few years. In 1892 the
Equitable realised £4 6s. percent., so its interest rate has
fallen only ninepence per cent. The fall that has struck
some of the Australian offices in the same period is painful
to think of.

The Union Trustee 00. certainly has had no very brilliant
luck of late. Just when many other companies are ris-
ing above the troubles of the boom-burst years, it shows
n tendency to fade, and things are worse with it now
than in the awful days of 1894 and 1895. The average
earnings during the last nine years have been only
£3 18s. 4d. per cent, on capital, and the dividends have
absorbed £l7l more than the earnings, thereby reducing
the profit balance from £979 to £BOB. The Union Trustee
00. never had a reserve fund, and the above £BOB with
£Bl9 investment fluctuation fund (£1627 in all) are all it
has to show after an existence of 15 years. From the
very beginning the management went in for paying about
the largest dividends that the profits would admit, and
cheerfully let the consequences go hang. They have
hung accordingly. In 1897 the co. wrote up a number of
securities from the actual purchase price to full market
value, and thereby created a reserve of £2789 to cover
depreciation of other investments. Only £Bl9 of this now
remains, the other £1970 having been absorbed. More
than a fourth of the mortgages are now represented by
foreclosed properties, though mortgages are a very
small item with the Union, so this isn’t of very
great importance. This co. lumps together commis-
sion received from the trustee business and interest on
investments, so it is impossible to say definitely what the
trustee business is worth, but one of two things is prac-
tically certain : Either the trustee business has been a
dead loss during the last five years, or the investments
have been so unremunerative that it is evident a
good deal of the shareholders’ money is lost. And as the
investments look quite sound the inference is that the
Union Oo.’s trustee business isn’t big enough to pay
expenses, and that the shareholders are keeping the
management on more as an act of charity than anything
else. The investments are debentures at 3, 3£ and 4 per
cent., bank deposits, mortgages (which should be worth
4i or 5 per cent.) and a very small amount of property,
and if the trustee business has been even paying its way
during the last five years then these investments have
only returned £2 16s. per cent, per annum. This is
obviously impossible.
The second balance-sheet (that for 1899) of the
Emu Bay Railway 00. (Tas.) is another lurid commentary
on the utter shiftlessness and ruinousness of the ways of
the private shindykits which occasionally get concessions
to build lines in Australia, and make Australia feel very
sorry for it ever after. The Emu Bay Co. isn’t quite so
awful as some, but if a Government—even the worst
Government in Australia—went railway-building in its
fashion, the deadest community in this continent would
throw it out of office straightway. To begin with, the
record is this :
1898— Loss £1395
1899 „ £5078
This is probably unavoidable, seeing that the line is still
incomplete and not in full work. But there are many
weird things in the Emu Bay Co.’s balance-sheet. Ap-
parently no less than £lOO,OOO of the capital has
disappeared without leaving any palpable assets behind,
or it never really arrived, or something, and the
balance - sheet is partly squared by this gorgeous
and most amazing good-will item, “ By rights acquired
under V.D.L. Co.’s Waratah-Zeehan Railway Act and
other concessions, £100,000.” Where the £lOO,OOO has
gone to the balance-sheet doesn’t explain, and this writer
doesn’t know, but the gall of assuming that a railway
which, so far, earns no dividend and shows a big loss is
worth £lOO,OOO above its cost price is something awful to
■contemplate. Another amazing asset is “ Registration,
law costs, obtaining leases, &e., £4691.” A third terrible
alleged property is “ Expenses on issue of debentures,
£37,373.” The Emu Bay Co., it seems, like almost every
■other private railway co. in Australasia, started without
■enough capital, or the mysterious absorption of the above-
mentioned £lOO,OOO left it without enough capital. And as
the shareholders hadn’t enough faith in their enterprise to
put up sufficient extra capital the co. floated debentures.
Up to 31st December, 1899, it had borrowed £100,150 on
debentures for 24£ years, at 5 per cent, per annum, and
the cost of raising this £100,150 was, apparently, the
appalling sum of £37,373. So the co it would seem
really got, up to end of 1899, £62,777 for its liability of
£lOO 150 and the interest is therefore at present at the
rate of almost 8 per cent. Assuming that the rest
(£99,850) of the debenture money comes in without any
further expenses, the interest will be over 0 per cent., and
allowing for the loss on repayment it will average nearly
7 per cent.
And, in addition, the co. apparently contracted
to deposit £16,000 with trustees as security for payment
of debenture interest, so it seems to have actually only got
the immediate use of less than £47,000 in return for a
liability of £100,150. Considering only the actual and
visible assets (goodwill and preliminary expenses and cost
of floating debentures aren’t assets) the Emu Bay Railway
Co. appears to stand as follows :
Liabilities. Assets.
Capital paid-up .. £298,410 Various visible
Debentures .. .. 100,150 assets £302,624
Interest on ditto.. 2,042 Deficit .. .. 148,538
Bank overdraft .. 42,675
Small debts .. ~ 7,885
£451,162 £451,162
This seems to imply that half the paid-up capital is
already lost. And the Emu Bay Railway Co. was only
registered on 2nd October, 1897.
The situation, as regards the Tasmanian people,
is this : They have to find the interest and dividends on
the Emu Bay Railway Co’s capital and debentures just as
much as if it was a State line, and they were finding the
interest on the State’s borrowings. That is to say, they
have to find it if the co. can possibly get it out of them in
fares and freights. But the State could raise the money
at 3j per cent., while the co. borrows at nearly 7 percent,
net, so with the private co. they have to find an immense
amount more than the other way. The State would never
have had to account for any mysteriously vanished
£lOO,OOO by putting a goodwill item in its balance-sheet,
for it doesn’t let money vanish like that. It would never
have borrowed at 5 per cent.—even Tasmania’s credit is
much better than that It wouldn’t have lost £37,343 in
the process of borrowing £100,150 at 5 per cent. —or even
at 3 per cent. It wouldn’t have spent £4691 in register-
ing itself and such like. Taking it all round, private rail-
way ownership is one of the lamentable curses of this mis-
guided and much-cursed country.
* * * *
The New York Life Insurance Co. presents an
imposing balance-sheet for 1899. Some of the principal
results compare with those of previous years as follows :
These are certainly stupendous figures. In eight years
the New York Life’s income has increased by £4,400,000,
and it has added nearly £22,000,000 to its accumulated
pile. Like all American companies, its assets have
been inspected and valued by an independent
State authority, and that authority certifies that
it is really and truly worth £48,652,335, and that,
assuming future interest at 4 per cent., it has
£8,525,823 more than all liabilities. Even on a 3 per cent,
basis, which is a more stringent one than is adopted by
any Australasian office, the New York Life has a very
large surplus. It is regrettable to see an office of such
high standing including in its assets such an item as
“Quarterly and semi-annual premiums not yet due,
£463,866,” for this asset is always a debateable one, but
even with it deducted, and reckoning future interest
earnings at 3 per cent., the New York Life could show a
very large surplus.
The expenses of the New York Life Cos. of late
years have been as follows, as compared with total
income:
1890 .. £2l 1 2 per cent. 1895 .. £2O 15 2 per cent,
1891 .. 20 10 2 ~ 1896 .. 20 4 6
1892 .. 24 15 0 „ 1897 .. 20 2 0
1893 .. 24 15 2 „ 1898 .. 19 15 2 „
1894 .. 23 4 7 „ 1899 .. 20 2 5 „
The amount is certainly smaller than it was six years ago,
but it is still very high according to the ideas of this
country, where the A.M.P. spends less than 9 per cent.,
and the Maoriland State Department less than 14J per
cent., and the Citizens’(in its ordinary life branch) less
than 16 per cent, of total income in working expenses.
And the New York Life only realises a moderate interest
rate on its investments, according to Australian ideas.
The 1899 rate was £4 10s. 2d. per cent, on the average
funds, which does very little to counterbalance the high
expense average. The two points on which the great
American offices keep definitely ahead are in their size
and in the most important fact that an independent State
audit guarantees their assets to he real and not over-
valued, and their solvency to be beyond dispute.

An extraordinary general meeting of the share-
holders in the Overflow Cos. was called for Wednesday this
week to consider this resolution :—“ That a Company
(hereinafter called the New Cos.), to be registered under
the No Liability Mining Cos. Act, be formed with a capital
of £15,000, in 60,000 shares of ss. each, to purchase from
the Overflow Silver Mining Cos. No Liability (hereinafter
called the Old Cos.), the whole of its mining .leases and
other assets, for which it is proposed to pay the old co.’s
liabilities, amounting to about £1,500, more or less. That
the shares be offered in the first instance to the share-
holders of the old co. in the proportion of one share for
every two shares, whether ordinary or preference, held by
them, upon payment of Is. per share, and in case any
shares remain unapplied for by shareholders in the old
co., such shareholders will be entitled to make further
application for said surplus shares, which will be ratably
allotted to them. That should there still be any shares
unapplied for they be offered for subscription by the
public.” The Overflow shareholders are real stickers.
They know they have a splendid-looking show, and they
hang on to it like grim death ; but they don’t hang on the
right way. The Overflow, consisting of a mile and three-
quarters of lode, which can be prospected practically right
along for at least 7 dwt. gold, and which at the bottom of
the deepest hole made in it has a most promising show of
mixed metals, is an alluring proposition ; but the scrippers,
simply through the most wicked, however well-meaning,
mismanagement, have had woeful luck.
* * *
Messrs. Clarke & Cos. are the movers in the
matter Of offering the B.H. South a price for its two
southern blocks. The co. has six blocks altogether, in-
cluding 5,6, 7, and 8, all supposed to be on the main lode,
and 87 and 88, which are on the eastern slope of the hill—
the same side as the North Central is on. Block 5 was
once “ floated off,” but the co. did no good. This block
occupies the southernmost part of the Broken Hill ridge ;
in fact, it is in this block that the ridge dips into the plain
which, with an occasional outcropping here and there,
extends for miles. Whether the great lode breaks up at
this point, or what becomes of it, has never been yet
satisfactorily shown. Block 6 is further north—nearer the
Central (Sulphide Corporation). The syndicate also asks
for blocks 87 and 88.
• • * «
Cobar Gold Mines (N.S.W.): One of the slime-
tanks burst last week, carrying away several miners’ huts,
Ac., and almost drowning several people. To be drowned
in slime would at any rate be a novelty. Damage esti-
mated at £IOOO. Treatment of slimes the mine’s only
remaining trouble. Some 10,000 tons have already
accumulated. Filter-press or treatment by agitation—
which? Both are effective and economical —not more
than 6s. a ton —and if these slimes contain sdwt. there is
a lot of money in them, and the mine is one of the best
and most permanent in N.S.W.
South Tharsis (Tas.) sent 50 tons concentrates to
the Lyell smelters and shareholders are told that the
value can’t be given at present and that they will have to
be converted into briquettes before smelting, as such fine
material tends to clog the furnaces. Now what are they
giving us? Surely this little parcel of concentrates can
be safely spread over the eleven Lyell furnaces without
making them into bricks.

North Mt. Lyell. “ In consequence of a rumor
that an attempt was being made to bear the London mar-
ket, Mr. J. P. Lonergan, chairman of the local board and
a large shareholder, has cabled an offer to the London
board to purchase for £250,000 the co.’s railway line (with
all rights and concessions) which is under construction
from Kelly’s Basin to the mine. This offer represents a
sum considerably in excess of the cost of the line. The
conclusion drawn from this offer is that the work has
come in for criticism in London. Up to to-day no official
reply has been received.” Just what might have been
expected from this management, which is generally
making a fool of itself either in London or in Melb. It is
now the London board’s turn to cable some offer to buy
the mine, and leave the railway, which will be a very
expensive thing to maintain anyhow, as land-slips have
already shown. The co. seems to have done nothing right
up to this very day. One of its gigantic mistakes is the
manner in which its so-called open-cut system is being
carried out.

Mt. Lyell Proprietary. London cables to shut
down the mine and to apply for suspension of labor con-
ditions ; of course negotiations are proceeding for further
capital. Fortunately for some people many alleged
copper mines are in country inaccessible to most of those
who waste their money on them, otherwise more than
half of them would have been shut down long ago. In
fact many of them would never have been started.

Golden Gate (Tas.): Brock, the boss shareholder,
willed that his interest should not be sold on any account.
His'executors got an Enabling Act, empowering the sale
at £l2 a share—£4oo,ooo for the mine. At this price an
option was given to a London crowd recently. Herbert
J. Daly examined. No sale resulted, and none is likely
to take place at such a price. A big mine, it has paid
enormous divs., with many more in sight; but, ’tis whis-
pered, the bottom is by no means the best part of it.

Playford, Warden of Northern Territory gold-
fields, is down south, talking. He shifted his head-
quarters years ago from Pt. Darwin to Burrundie,
reckoning that the centre of the gold area. Mostly
Chinese in the mines, he says, even in the few European-
owned ones. Australians have nearly dropped out of
N.T. mining, even as to owning claims. Five Bull co.’s
are, however, more or less at-work. Chief of these co.’s
mines is the Eureka, whose trouble is water ; 459,000ga1.
lifted daily, and then they are only surface-scratching.
The ore wants cyaniding ; a £20,000 plant has been put
up for this treatment. He reckons there is close on
20,000 tons of ore exposed, and it runs over l£oz. Wages
(this is at Burrundie): Ah Chow, ss. to 7s. a day ; Euro-
peans, not wanted. Mr. Playford apparently does not
think much of dredging prospects, but there should be
money in sluicing.
* * * •
Still another S.A. gold mine which looked like
doing great things is rushing down the steep place. The
New Golden Record, of Waukaringa, which has both a
battery and a cyanide-plant, is unable to give either of
them work to do ; after it had exhausted itself putting up
the chemical plant it washed up 7oz. from 220 tons—and
then it gave that best. And the moral of that part of
the business is : Always get two independent assayers to
sample tailings before erecting cyanide works to treat
them. Also, as there isn’t enough reef to keep the battery
going, and as there is an overdraft, and as shareholders
forfeit when calls are made, the co. has decided to offer
the property in Europe. Probably through a fine spirit of
patriotism,’Germany is to have the first chance to pay
“ £SOOO cash and one-fourth the shares in anew co., or
£15,000 cash right out.”
* • • •
Wallaroo and Moonta Copper Cos., the one big
S.A. mining concern, has just had the most successful
half-year since the Wallaroo and Moonta amalgamated in
’9O. ’This was wholly due to the jump in copper ; but for
that the co, would have had a rather bad time. Moonta
ore, in fact, is now very poor, and the chairman admitted
at the recent half-yearly meeting that with copper at the
price which has ruled for years it could not pay to work.
Most of it—this refers only to the Moonta mine—runs only
U per cent, copper ; and that even at present prices
means only about 225. per ton. The co.’s balance-sheet
shows a profit of £85,799. But that is either too much or
too little—it depends upon the point of view. Thus, in-
cluded in the amount is the increase in the value of accu-
mulated stocks through the rise in price ; while, on the
other hand, there have been exceedingly heavy deductions
for depreciation and the purchase of machinery, together
something like £44,000. The co., which hitherto has
been most conservative, having almost a horror of the
Stock Exchange, is, alas ! becoming demoralised. It is
going to seek quotations on the Melb. and Sydney
’Changes, and even mentions London.
• • * •
Six weeks ago Westralian stocks took a sudden
but temporary jump, whereupon Moses Moss, of London
Truth, jumped to the conclusion that they were going to
the prices of six months ago. “ The recent movement in
the W.A. market shows that the ‘bears’ had their
innings, and we may now look for an all-round advance.”
He declared that colonial investors were buying heavily,
and the British he advised to hurry up ere it was too late.
Lake Views were then £ISJ, and Associateds £7, but this
was nothing to what they would be in a little while, as
“the bears had played their last card and could nolonger
stem the recovery.” Yet within a few days of this ap-
pearing, Lake Views were down to £l3 and Associateds
back to £5, around the vicinity of which they have been
hovering ever since. And what they would be but for
the tens of thousands over-sold, Heaven only knows.
* • » *
At the recent London meeting of N. Boulder
G.M. Cos., Kal., some sensational facts transpired. The
profits for year ’99 were £42,000. 10 per cent div.jvas de-
clared, and £23,000, in which was included some previous
reserve, was carried forward. This was all right so far.
But the directors claimed for their sweating trouble a
per centage not only on the amount of the divs., but also
ort the £23,000 1 “Monstrous! outrageous! never heard
such a thing ! ” from shareholders. There was a long and
angry discussion, but the directors had taken care to havf
their friends at the meeting. They proposed graciousI|
to reduce their claim of £5000 and take a percentage o|
only £18.000 of the reserve. 49 members present; voting
—25 for directors, 24 against them.

* * • *
Lady Loch, which has had a couple of years of
bad time, was picking up nicely when more bad time
struck it. Last half-year its monthly outturn rose
gradually from 411oz. to 809oz. ; but in Feb. it slumped
to 364oz. despite the fact that the directors had just cir-
cularised shareholders telling them to look out for bigger
returns. “ The small yield is due to the delay caused by
the breakdown of the pump,” it was officially explained.
Lucky breakdown ! —the fall from 17£dwt., last half-year’s
average, to 12dwt. per ton had, of course, nothing to do
with the “small yield.” Still, taking the co.’s Forrest
King and Lady Loch leases, there is a fair lot of easily-
got average stone to go on with.

SOCIETY
On Tuesday, 3rd April, day of memorable
storm and rain in Sydney, the Government am-
bulance proceeded to Canterbury, there picked up
a plague patient, drove the considerable distance
to Rockdale, picked up there another stricken one,
and then conveyed both through cataracts of
tropical rain to Woolloomooloo; whence, at 6
p.m., they were despatched by launch to quaran-
tine. According to a local medico—and most
persons of sense will agree with him—the chances
of recovery in these two cases were much lessened
by such treatment; and it is hard to see why the
houses containing the patients should not simply
have been isolated until the following morning.
* * * *
> For once the influential hush-it-up policy
has over-reached itself. When the plague first
started to spread into York and Clarence streets,
Sydney, the papers published the addresses of the
warehouses afflicted, and the reports read like
THE GREAT UNDRESSED.
SPRAWL : “ Lucky man, Brown.”
DRAWL : “ Yaas. Has all the indelicacies of the
season.”
this : “John Jones, a green-grocer whose business
is carried on at 20001 Sussex-street, was last night
found to be suffering from true plague. The
premises have been quarantined and the patient
and six contacts removed to the North Head.”
But influential people protested, so, though the
papers kept on printing the green-grocer’s address
in full, it dealt with the larger places thus:
“James Jones and Samuel Jones, employed in a
warehouse in York-street, were found to be suf-
fering from the disease and removed to the
Quarantine Station.” As the result of this sort of
thing, when the representative of a big York-
street warehouse called on one of his best customers
in Orange, the other day, he was met by the pro-
prietor and a stern countenance, and asked point-
blank if it was his place where the plague cases
had occurred. He loudly ridiculed the idea, but
could not allay the storekeeper, who remarked,
“Well, yer oughter make the papers publish
the names of all them as has got it; but some-
how I think it was your place; because they’re
so influential, and only influential coves could
have got it hushed up. "No, I won’t give an order
to-day. I’ll get the stuff from Melbourne till this
scare is over,- even if I only make exes.” And so
it is. It is said that hundreds of tons of goods av.e
being brought from Melbourne right past Sydney,
and a tremendous dislocation of trade is taking
place, all because the papers and authorities at the
request of a few big traders kept their sacred but
infected names out of the papers.
* * * *
At a recent meeting of Sydney City Coun-
cil, when an alderman wanted to know if it were
true that dereliet-dog-slaying took place on the
premises of the Town Hall itself, the Acting-
Mayor replied, in a large, vague way, that he
believed something of the sort took place some-
where about the rear of the building. It is quite
in keeping with the general smellfulness of Sydney
matters-municipal that the forlorn and casual tyke
should be brought for slaughter to the palatial
seat of city mis-government. The municipal
slaughter of humans, however, does not (except
indirectly, through aldermanic gorging and guz-
zling) take place on the premises ; but it goes on
gaily just the same.
* » * *
At least one man in Sydney has bubonic
scare badly. Owing to the absence of anti-plague
serum, he made a-special journey to Melb. to be
inoculated, and left for Sydney again immediately
after the operation. Stated that he is a leading
dentist.
* » * *
Records of 1840 show that Sydney, then
mainly George-street, was known as “the dirty
city.” The gallows was then in George-street, and
in brisk demand, and the common saying was that
the town was only good enough to be hanged in.
* * * *
Less than six months ago the Sydney
Jingo papers were saying that the filthiness of the
Boers was proof of their unfitness to rule their
country. They don’t say much about that now—•
the reports of the ravages of that pre-eminent
filth disease, bubonic plague, among themselves
take up all the space.
* * * *
The second area quarantined in Sydney—-
the small space, not a quarter of a square mile,
bounded by King, Market, and Sussex streets,
and the water-line —paid a handsome dividend of
700 tons of filth. Seven hundred more reflections
on the dirty and imbecile City Council which has
allowed Sydney to degenerate into a dirt-bin.
* * * *
Sydney’s town clerk on Sydney : “The
city, because of its peculiar configuration and the
faults of original, laying out, and because of the
great traffic therein, will in some localities always
present the hovel appearance, and thoroughly
cleansed as they may be to-day, they will be
equally bad a few months hence.” But why should
it always be so ? Sydney must be a cramped city
where even fine buildings look like hovels while it
is built as it is at present. But the French laid
out Paris afresh and rebuilt it, and London
County Council is gradually doing the -same with
London. Paris was four times the size of Sydney
when it was rebuilt. London is twelve times the
size. Why should Sydney not be laid out on a
completely new plan and rebuilt ?
* * . -> *
Premier Lyne had before him last week a
proposal to suspend Sydney Municipal Council,
and appoint instead a working sanitary commission.
It is said that a very little puff would have blown
him the way of the proposal. It is curious that
that unfortunate Council has twice been booted
out of office by the State Govt. Over 50 years
ago it was abolished by Act of Parliament, and a
body of commissioners set up instead, and 25 years
ago it went into the Insolvency Court. It is
almost as bad an example of municipal incapacity
as the history of civic government reveals. The
vice of the. body from the first day of its creation
was that, it consisted, sometimes wholly and at
others chiefly, of men directly or indirectly owning
land and house property in and around the city.
Reform or criticism which might presentlv affect
rents was crushed as heresy.
* * * *
It is to be hoped that, before any garbage-
destructor contract is finally accepted by Sydney
City Council, the Council will carefully follow up
, any scientific or other'information on the subject,
which may happen to be in the possession of
Police-Inspector General Fosbery.
* * * *
Sui’ely it cannot be true that it was not
until after attention had been called to the subject
in the newspapers that the Protestant churches
took steps to have themselves represented by
clergymen among the plague-stricken patients at
Sydney quarantine station !
* * * *
When is it to be arranged that arrestees
apparently drunk, or otherwise off their mental
balance, must be examined by a medical man
before being locked up in the police-cells ? The
other night a well-known and decent-living Sydney
citizen failed to come home. He had been arrested
for drunkenness and had spent the night in dur-
ance ; but he was not drunk —he was paralysed.
It would cost little to have a doctor at call at
each police-station.

Everyone is glad that no serious conse-
quences attended the attempt on the Prince of
Wales’ life by a 15-year-old Belgo-Italian assassin,
allegedly hired. Altogether apart from his
official position, the Prince appears to be
thoroughly popular among those who know him
as a genial personage who would never willingly
do a bad turn to anyone. The Prince’s escape
affords yet another illustration of how lucky and
certain it is that the average man armed with a
pistol couldn’t hit even the biggest man at five
paces.
Albert Edward’s coolness under fire would
undoubtedly have been represented as a marvellous
case of heroism, but for the fact that all sorts and
conditions of men are taking pot luck in Africa just
now, without entertaining any doubt as to whether
the gun is loaded. As it was, the community was
spared the “thrill of horror” which might have
gone through the newspapers under ordinary cir-
cumstances. Melb. Age, it is true, seized the
opportunity for remarking that the Heir Apparent
“if permitted to take the field in person, would
match the best of his future subjects in physical
courage,” but the utter impossibility of rating him
higher than Tommy Atkins under the circum-
stances was evident even to the Age.

“ Johnnie ” Aitken made another appeal
to Vic. Government last week on behalf of his
“ Gentleman Scouts.” This time it was suggested
that Premier M’Lean should cable to Chamberlain
offering the services of the gilded fifty to some
regular Bwitish force under the command of Bwitish
officahs, but poor Johnny was gently ticked off.
If the Noble Half Hundred insist upon dying for
the Empire there is nothing to . prevent them
joining the Bushmen’s Corps (provided they are
up to the mark) with that end in view. Any able-
bodied superior person who means business can
get killed by a Boer nowadays; so if Aitken and co.
want to cut a respectable figure they will make a
rush for the nearest kopje without delay.

* * *
A refreshing incident is reported
from a seaside Melbourne suburb.
One fine afternoon lately the presiding
constable was seen to prick up his ears,
and, after glaring wildly in the direction
of the ladies’ baths, to agitate his regu-
lation-boots with quite unusual energy.
He scrambled over 50yds. of sand and
grabbed a man with a patriarchal beard
who was sitting by tne baths. The
Law hustied the stumbling
stranger along, whilst the prisoner
protested amazedly in a thin,
small voice. A crowd was waiting
on the footpath, and there the
pright officer paused and said
breathlessly : * ‘ It’s no use, me mahn—
not a bit ! I saw ye lookin’ in at the ladies
schwimmin’ there, an’ there’s witnesses here
to corroborate me shtatement. ’ The old
man chipped in stammeringly. “I tell yez
it ain’t no good ye talkin,” said the Law.
“Ye seen him, didn’t ye?” it continued,
addressing the crowd. Two women of the
type that sees everything joyfully acquiesced.
“There nhow ! ” said the Law triumphantly.
“ Ye’ll come along wid me if ye plaze.” But the
prisoner made himself heard at last. “ I tell you
I am blind,” he said; “my daughter left me to
go and buy some fruit.” The daughter arrived at
this juncture and heaped scorn on the Law as it
drifted away wearing an expression of profound
melancholy.
The people of Greymouth (M.L.) got off a
very grey-whiskered chestnut when they sent
their present of a gold brick —it only weighed 3oz.
in all, and was worth about £l2, so it wasn’t
much of a brick —to Sir George White, the de-
fender of Ladysmitl). They had engraved on one
side “This is a brick,” and on the other side
“You’re another.” The same inspiration has
been worked-off many times on many different
people. Years ago, however, a Bendigo man
made a pleasing variation of it. He sent a large
gold brick to some overwhelming tin-god of the
moment with the inscription, cut by himself in
straggling letters with a jack-knife, “You’re the
same thing as this is.” The giver of the brick
IN SHEOL; “FANCY MEETING YOU!"
died very poor, his only assets being a barrow-load
of wild-cat scrip ; and the receiver drank himself
into the horrors with the brick, and died of it.
The Greymouth people evidently intend that no
such calamity shall fall on their god. A seasoned
veteran can’t get many horrors out of £l2.

A certain free-lance pressman visited the
cashier of a daily paper, the other day, to collect
an account of £l. On finding it cut down by ss.
he made enquiries, and at last interviewed one of
the big men of the office. “ You see, Mr. Scribe,”
said the B. M., “our expenses have been so terribly
heavy of late ; when the war is over, we shall be
pleased to pay higher rates, but, at present, we are
compelled to reduce them, so as to meet our very
large and unexpected outlay. Do you know that
every time (who represents the paper in
South Africa) has his horse killed, it costs us £5O ? ”
Adelaide has had a delirious time during
the last fortnight. The two theatres are closed,
but the vacancy has been filled by a W.C.T.TJ.
convention. The spectacled, hard-visaged ladies
have mighty influence in the apostolic city, so the
dailies pander by printing columns of stuff about
barmaid-abolition, cigarette-smoking, and the
enticing subject of further raising the age of con-
sent to something under 50. This convention
comes under the heading of “ amusements ” in the
pious city, which has indeed learned to take its
pleasures sadly.
A well-known member of Vic. Legislature,
who lately visited a Paris dance-garden, was so
deeply interested in watching, from the outer
edge of a crowd, the charming evolutions of the
premiere danseuse in the cancan that he inadver-
tently fell backwards into a flowering cactus. A
friend was occupied for three days, more or less,
picking out the spikes.

ruffian—also “a Boer.” But that was the paper

Re tlie murderous overworking of Sydney
’bus-horses, it is significant that, whereas the
London ’bus-horse averages five working-years,
one year is enough to do-up the miserable moke of
Sydney, which it is now arranged is in future to
draw (in a two-horse ’bus) 24 adults. This, at an
average of 10st., give Itons ; or—with an addi-
tional 30cwt. of ’bus —Tg tons per horse. Surely
an unmerciful load to haul up the long Sydney
slopes.

The particularly Naice but indigent
scion of an Australasian family, and heir to an
hereditary title, was to be seen staggering about
a bar, the other day, loudly proclaiming Ins
identity, and endeavoring to touch utter strangers
for a long beer. At last a friend entered, and,
seizing the youth by the shoulders, bustled, him
into a corner. “Look here, Plantagenet,” he
observed, “ take a pull at yourself. You’ll be Sir
Plantagenet Yere de Vere in a few years, you
know.” The noble one lurched forward into the
middle of the crowd, “I know,” he yelled.
“ Pantageneevere my (hie) blanky title (hie)—
why, ” as a sudden gleam of reason burst through
his alcoholic craze, “if any of you chaps has got
30 bob, the blanky title’s yours.”
* • « *
A Sydney Hospital regulation adopted at
the instigation of Dr. Foreman a couple of years
ago and reading “No medical officer shall hold
office after attaining the age of 60 years, except as
a consultant,” has attracted from other Australian
hospital bodies a number of close enquiries as to
how the rule works out. So far as Sydney is con-
cerned the rule does some good and, it is alleged,
no harm.

During 1899 over 500 deaths occurred at
the Liverpool IN.S.W.) Old Men’s Asylum ; 142
being due to consumption and 85 to cancer.
* * * *
T’other day, a fat and dignified lady of 50
summers entered the office of the swimming-bath
of a Sydney suburb. She was about to ask for a
bathing-gown, “ the widest that you have,
please,” when there rose up from a chair
inside the office a large hulking brute in
the form of a whiskered man. “Oh,”
cried the lady, “ I thought that this was a
ladies’ bath ;'you liaveit advertised outside
—‘Ladies’ Day.’ Is there no woman in
attendance?” “Yes, Madam,” replied
the man with impressive respectfulness,
“my wife and daughter are both in atten-
dance ; I am only the manager and stop
here to obey my wife or daughter should
they need me to save anybody from
drowning; or should they need me to run
and get brandy for any lady who might be
taken with cramps.” “Oh, I under-
stand,” replied the lady; and with that
she went off to select a dressing-box on the
side where the sun was shining. She
entered the box and drew the curtain.
Shortly afterwards the curtain was drawn
back, and a fat and lovely lady emerged
in pink bathing-gown with embroidered
frills. She picked her way with exact
care down the slippery steps and over
the greasy green rocks; and then
with an affable smile depositedherself
in 4ft. deep of water, and wobbled
about, and splashed, and puffed, and
kicked. Suddenly she yelled and hastily
crawled on hands and feet out of the
water, back over the greasy rocks, and
up the treacherous steps. Reaching the
top of the steps, she bowled along to the
manager’s wife, and said : “It was bad
enough when I came in to find that you
had a man in the office ; but I find that
you have also a man in the bath swimming
amongst us.” The manager’s wife looked
in great surprise and replied : “ Madam,
I beg your pardon, but there is no man in
the bath.” “ What! ” ejaculated the fat
ladv, “no man? What’s that ?” . “That,”
replied the manager’s wife, ‘‘ is one of
the local leaders of our fashionable society;
she has her hair out short, that’s all.” I
am bound to take your word,” answered
the fat lady; “nevertheless, I am not
going to be seen in the water with -
her. She is too like a man for my fancy. Oh,
bless me ! she’s doing the shark-trick ! She 11
burst her bathing-dress in a minute. And with
that the fat lady escaped to the protection of her
curtain.
* * » *
Consider what a cheerful gamble and
lottery and toss-up is the Law ! In Melbourne,
some months ago, a man attacked a woman with a
knife, and inflicted on her a very slight wound.
The arresting constable, however, charged him
with attempted murder (it probably was attempted
murder), and started him on a road which led to
a death-sentence, since commuted to 10 years’
gaol. The other day, at North Melbourne, a man
stabbed his mother much more severely in the
side, and almost killed her. But the different
kind of bobby who ran him in called it merely
assault, so he percolated through a different
stratum of the law and wound up with a sentence
of one month. He committed the heavier assault,
and jeopardised the life of his own mother but he
struck the ligl-.ter bobby. The first thing a
criminal should learn is to be judicious in the
selection of his policeman.
* * * *
One reason very commonly assigned why
a great part of Sydney Domain should be confis-
cated for railway purposes is that it is largely
used as a doss-house for very unsavory vagrants,
and that the abolition of their camp would be no
misfortune. But if Sydney can only get rid of its
sleepers-out by taking the ground from under
them it will have to abolish all its parks. And
then, when it has driven the vagrants into the
streets, it will have to abolish the streets too —in
fact, it will h ave to abolish everything.
* * • •
The sweet girl graduate mostly gets tired
or married (probably much the same thing) while
still on the lower rungs of the academic ladder.
At Melb. University “Commencement” the
other day there were 11 girl B.A.’sto 15 males ;
but while 13 males had proceeded to the M.A.
degree, only four girls had staggered up so high.
* * * *
During a smallpox scare, some years ago,
a certain Australian city ran short of lymph. Two
of the leading doctors had made a corner in it,
and there was none else procurable; but for all
that an outsider professed to have any quantity,
though he would part with none of it. His
patients were all satisfied with his vaccination,
and it was not till months after that they dis-
covered they had been “ done” with —glycerine.

* * * *
“V.K.” to THE BULLETIN:—
“ Yarrum’s ” yarn of atavism (B. 3/3/’00) in the case of
a Chinese octoroon’s baby emphasises one of the many
reas >ns for the Tartar. No other colored race on earth
has so much latent vitality, force of type reproduction,
as the Mongol. Not only is he an economical factor, but
pre-eminently a danger to the Caucasian on account of
his greater virility, a tough, passive sort of virility that
you can’t kill with an axe. The Chinese and bis cousins
in their countless numbers threaten to take the world by
storm, fiooks as if Nature had, in their case, stayed the
law of evolution and bottled them up for thousands of
years for future use. And we are just now fooling around
trying to pull the cork from that same bottle out of the
Arabian Nights.

One M.L. old-age pensioner, although
turned 96, skips blithely to the pay-office for his
monthly instalment. Its regular receipt has made
the old man feel 20 years younger, so he says.

housekeeper £ll5, and the watchman £lB5. As
Parliament sits only about two-thirds of a year
on an average, this rate of pay for such services
was described by a judicial functionary one night
lately as tending to demoralise the labor-market.
• * * *
Disgraceful and unnecessary reminders of
the good old times are the facts that men are to be
seen any morning walking down from Melbourne
Gaol to the railway station in twos and threes
handcuffed together, and that convicted men, in
prison uniform, guarded by armed warders, often
drive drays through Melb.
In Paddington, Sydney, just on the con-
fines of the city, there is a plot of land just being
built on for the first time. Curiously enough, it
was the first piece cleared by old Absolom West,
grantee of what is now Paddington, in about the
year 1817. West got his ground because he
brewed the best beer in Australasia. Seems a large
order, but beer was only brewed then in two
places in Australasia—Sydney and Parramatta.
