A WOMAN’S LETTER.
_ _ Sydney, April 10, 1900.
My Dear Myek,—
Up at Govt. House, evening parties all the win-
musical evenings, religious evenings, artistic
® t venin &s> (Society evenings, and—yes—l suppose
there will be literary evenings ! It is a long time
since there has been a literary evening at Govt.
House. By the way, are they dying out ? Or is
it possible people are having them all the time,
and never asking me ? Never mind, there will be
the religious evenings!
And never mind. The last literary one I went
to is still fresh and fair in my memory. When I
want another I feast on that, and am satisfied
Oh, quite, quite satisfied. The host was a nasty
man. The hostess had views, and wasn’t taking
any smiles. One of the visitors—“ The Worm,”
the host called her, because her heart had affini-
ties for cocoons and mulberry-leaves and the silk
industry had affixed herself to the host, and was
holding forth in a rapid, disconnected way about
nothing. She annoyed him. “Talking about
worms, he said casually; “I made a remarkable
discovery the other day.” “ You did ? ” “ Yes.
You know Shakespeare— poet and dramatist.
(Poor worm! She wriggled a little at that paren-
thesis.) He says somewhere, ‘Men have died
and. worms have eaten them, but not for love. 5
Remember?” “Oh, yes, yes, certainly.” “What
play is it m? ” suddenly. ‘ ‘ Play— er—er— which ?
Isnt it Julius Caesar’—yes ‘Julius Cassar, of
course.” “Not ‘Romeo and Juliet’—Romeo
says it to Juliet, does he not?” “Yes, now I
come to think of it, so he does.”
He knew she would look it up when she got
home. But his heart was hard. His wife gave
evening parties. And he went on: “ Well, I have
found it a scientific fact that worms do eat men
because ( they love them.” “How very interest-
ing 1 Isn tit ? Mcti have died and worms have
eaten them because they loved them ” is how it ought
to go. People try to jump the fact that Shake-
speare made a mistake about those worms, and
they make out that he meant to say, “ Worm-
eaten men never died for love.” Some folks take
it, no man ever died for love. Leaving out the
worm question altogether. Do you follow me ? ”
And the Worm said, “ I do indeed. A clever
subject. I must make a note of it for my next
silk paper.” Whereupon he bowed, and went
into the obscurity of the hall to choke unseen of
his wife, who stood talking in the middle of the
room, hopelessly wrecked on the desert island of a
new metaphysical fact.
But, vice-regally speaking, those parties are an
excellent idea. Wny should we always dance
when we go Govt. Housewards ? To sit quietly in
the shadow of the red drawing-room, quite at
home, quite at ease, just dropped m (in a new frock
from London) to spend the evening informally
with the Earl and a few dear friends—ask anyone
if that isn t better than getting lost among thou-
sands of other people in the hot crush of the Govt.
Hquse balls. I think his Ex. has done the right
thing this time.
But what an Easter ! Even the Agricultural
Ball has evaporated since I wrote last. And now
there s nothing more frivolous to look forward to
than race-horses and and prize show pigs. In fact
it is quite common now to meet a woman who
isn’t getting a new dress this winter.
t t he the y are sa ying in Melbourne that
Lord Beauchamp had invited Lady Madden to do
the Easter honors at our Govt. House, accompanied
by the glittering Ruby. Sydney violently contra-
dicts this. To pass over our own fine Chief-
J usticess, who can, when occasion arises, act the
vice-regal with so superb an air, and go to Mel-
bourne for an hostess is a charge that even Sydney
won t lay at Lord Beauchamp’s door.
Indeed, I have good reason to believe that our
Governor is fast becoming furiously popular.
What do you think of this? An “unappro-
priated country lady lately wrote to a town
friend: I want yob to send me twelve yards of
black satin and twenty yards of silk fringe ”
(Ihis was for a race ball to be opened by His Ex )
Next day it was published that all Vice-Regal
engagements were cancelled. By wire, afternoon
from the country lady: “Changed my mind!
Satin only. Not having any fringe on that frock.”
By the way, you really must mind, nowadays,
whom you call “ Unappropriated.” The old Old
Maid has had ten good years added to the years
wherein she is merely a maid. The old maid of
twenty-nine is as dead as the young married
woman of seventeen who used to be such a heinous
success m the 60’s. In fact, there’s no such thing
as an old maid under forty to-day. And that
spinster is quite out of date who remarked lately :
How nice to be married and turned from an old
maid of thirty into a young married woman of
thirty-one ! ”
Many people coming home from early church last
Sunday earned in one hand the healthy spiritual
. aac i of green from Palm Sunday service, and
m the other the Sunday paper, in which they
grubbed religiously for fresh cases of plague.
Sunday s excitement was the hotel in King-street
that everybody passes going into town. I hap-
pened to pass there myself on Sunday. It was
then about lunch-time. A policeman in yellow
leather leggings—the public supposes these to be
a guard against fleas —strolled up and down out-
side. On the very front doorstep, right on King-st.,
stood a rubbish-box, overflowing. Things falling
out over the sides. That was in the middle of the
day. Coming home at sunset I passed that hotel
again, and looked out curiously. The policeman
was still there. And still there was the box of
rubbish, standing just as it had been standing all
day on the front doorstep, wafting itself on every
wind that passed. Extraordinary manoeuvres,
that hustle healthy people out of their homes
i_ a * nomen l ; ’ s notice, and leave the rub-
bish - box of an allegedly - infected house
exposed on the front street of the city half
a day. Was it because it was the Sawbath ?
And 1 suppose we must put it down to Plague
that society is trying so hard to sell its new things
ordered from “ home ” six months ago. A common
request is apropos of “My new English frock
thats too—too large for me, and do you know
anyone who wouki take it off my hands, dirt
cheap. Phis year’s box of London clothes
threatens to be an awful thorn in the side of
society. Indeed, women are fast coming to the
conclusion that far worse than having nothing to
wear is the state of having new frocks and nowhere
to wear them.
The boarding-house advt. up to date adds the
new allurement—“No Rats!”
Despite the plague-scare and the lack of gaiety
this Easter, the shops are not behindhand with
their Autumn novelties. I was in at Pitt-street
-Hordern s. the other day, and was struck with
many feminine fripperies, chief of which were a
line of fascinating silk petticoats, cut in the
newest shapes, and priced as reasonably low even
as a pound ; some beautiful square Luxeuil lace
collars not to be had anywhere else ; and of course
silks in higLly-satisfying variety. Another speci-
ality of Hordern’s this year is a line of sailor-made
coats and skirts, within the reach of the ordinary
dresser. What a change this season will be, with
every second woman—shop-girl or “Society”—
tailor-made, coated or jacketed some way or
another from the beginning of the tailor-made
epoch five years _ ago, when the double-breasted
woman with a tie and collar was something to
stare at.
i “ A Budget of Advanced Fashions ” is a little
booklet from W. T. Waters, telling about this
firm s Autumn goods, particularly to be recom-
mended to country people who do their shopping
by post, and like to see pictures of the smartest
hats and frocks before ordering them."
This week has been a wild struggle between
friends and enemies and Potts Point and under-
takers and greengrocers all mixed, and mingled,
and thrown into each other’s houses without any
introduction. In a word, our telephones have
gone raving mad. My experience is that when I
ring up anybody the answer comes back “ The X
Laundry’s speakin’.” We can’t wonder that it is
so common to hear of a healthy man solemnly
giving up his telephone now and for ever, when he
is invariably put on to an undertaker.
The only matrimonial news I can offer you this
week is the marriage of pretty little Miss Annie
Weston, one of Sydney’s belles, and Mr. Ernest
Watt, at St. Mark’s Church, Darling Point, last
Tuesday. The bridesmaids were four, Miss Beat-
rice and Miss Clive Weston, sisters of the bride,
and Miss Nora Dangar and Miss Maysie Chisholm,
all frocked alike, simply, in cream Indian-muslin-
flounced skirts, lace zouave bodices, and big hats
of turquoise-blue velvet. The bride’s going-away
frock was a blue cloth coat and skirt, lined with
cerise, and worn with toque, muff, and collar of
ermine. Need I add the wedding presents were
both but you can finish that well-worn sentence
without vie.
Another society wedding that comes off next
week is that of Miss Alice MacG-eorge, daughter
of Mrs. Frank Denison Brown, with Mr. Wick-
ham, from Wellington (N.Z.).
A great shock to her many friends in Sydney
was the death (in Tasmania, last Thursday) of
Mrs. Gr. F. Anderson, wife of Capt. Anderson,
lately of the Orient steamship Austral. Mrs. An-
derson was the eldest of the three pretty Miss
Hoskings, one of whom married Mr. B. Mac-
arthur, and is living in London. Last October
the Andersons went to live in Tasmania, the Cap-
tain giving up his sea appointment for a settled
position on land. Mrs. Anderson’s bright, sweet
manner had made hosts of friends for her, both
here and there, and they are all terribly grieved to
hear of her death and that of her infant child.
A second visit to “ Lady Dolly ” found the little
Palace Theatre cheerfully full after a run of one
week, which was a succession of excellent houses.
So good was the business that an extra three
nights were added. After that I think the chorus
would have knocked up, for the amateur hasn’t
the staying power of the professional, and I noticed
several of the caste looking very tired.
Nance O’Neill on Magda : “ Every night, when
I go home from playing Magda, I am in a state of
uncertainty. Do I marry the parson ! or do I not ?
I have never yet satisfactorily answered the prob-
lem ! ” Which is just the impression her acting of
Magda leaves with you ; you, too, go home uncer-
tain as to Magda’s future. And not only uncer-
tain, but unable to see any solution that might
ever please you.
In Newcastle, the beautiful Californian gave
them a taste, last week, of “Camille,” a play
which Sydney is not to see till next Saturday
night, at Her Majesty’s. For some reason or
other, the Newcastle houses were mere handfuls ;
the co. opening to a miserable first night’s audi-
ence, that nearly sent Nance packing back to
Sydney in disgust. And it can’t be a case of
taking coals to New.castle, either.
Apropos, Mr. and Mrs. Essie Jenyns-Wood are
back in Sydney, after a long trip abroad, bringing
with them a new baby and a little girl rapidly
growing into the image of her mother.
You know, of course, that Kingston is to play
Armand to Nance O’Neill’s Camille. It is not a
new part to him. He played it in London with
no less a person than Olga Nethersole, who was very
fond of acting Camille to the good-looking boy.
Do you remember me telling you, a long while
ago, about some photographs Olga showed an
Australian visitor, remarking joyfully as she
showed them, “These are rather— er— are they
not ? ” Those were photos, of Olga and Kingston
in Camille.
Speaking of acting, a well-known and high-
art actor lately declared that Mrs. Brough was
the most satisfying woman he had ever acted
with. Some people found her acting monotonous,
but her warmth and earnestness were always an
immense stimulus to anybody playing her lover.
On the other hand, to make stage love to Ada
Ferrar was the hardest feat this Thespian had
ever attempted. “From beginning to end she
was an icicle. When she looked at you there was
a cold wind in your eyes. When she threw her-
self into your arms it was like meeting a blast of
snow full in your face ! ”
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“There is genuine grief, and also grief of the
not unselfish order, in Melb. over Earl Beau-
champ’s bereavement. The Maddens, it was said,
were going to be installed in all their glory at
Sydney Govt. House pro tem., and the widow of
Cliveden, with her lately-returned daughter, had
accepted an invitation to spend the Race-week at
Government House.
“Lord Brassey has sent on a subscription of
£5O to Melbourne Hospital with an explanation
that his failure to subscribe during his residence in
Victoria was due to an oversight. He ordered all
the subscriptions of his predecessors to be renewed,
and the instruction was not carried out. The ex-
cuse is very thin. He was frequently written to,
circularised, reminded, but no cheque was sent.
It is proposed that the Faller-off shall continue to
be nominal Governor of Victoria until the Gov.-
General is appointed. That will entitle him to go
on receiving a proportion of salary while he is in
London ; a fine income in itself. A man of sound
character would not take the money under the cir-
cumstances.
“ When Lord Dicky returns from his pleasure-
jaunt, he will have to work day and night to over-
step the mark of Private-Sec. Campbell’s devotion
and fealty to the reigning family. During the
whole of the benefit matinee at the Princess’s on
Wednesday afternoon, the Colonel, in a nutmeg
vest and Paisley tie, stood in the back of the box,
lending an attentive ear when anybody deigned
to speak. Considering it was a four-hours’ per-
formance, Col. Campbell showed untiring pati-
ence, and carried off opera-glasses and wraps with
a wonderfully well-pleased air at the final drop of
the rag. In the opposite box, the Mayoress (in grey,
like the Acting-Governor’s wife) had a party of
lady-friends, the sharp profile of Mrs. John Gavan
Duffy’s face being just visible from the greater
part of the circle. In that charmed circle, where
all the fashionable flutterers were gathered, a pert
little Sydney matron, formerly belonging to these
parts, was the most interesting object. She gazed
through an ebony-rimmed lorgnette for the greater
part of the performance, and wore a real chic
grey voile gown touched up with pink, black hat,
and milk-white furs. Her golden locks defied the
recognition of old friends, and brown eye-brows
arched above a saucy pink-and-white faee.
“ A special train to Colac is to be turned on for
the marriage of Miss Queenie Calvert and Mr.
Gordon Lyon, down at the squatting township,
next Saturday week (21st). The wedding-cards
have a time-table at the back to facilitate matters.
Melb. people have to leave town in the dewy morn
at 9.20, and the return journey brings them back
at about same hour in the evening; but such a
spectacular knot-tying should be worth it. There
is some hint that the hunting-men are going to
wear their pink coats and that bugle-notes are to
mingle with the jingle of wedding-bells.
“One of Melbourne’s most conservative and
dignified suburbs has had an elopement. Its
parson’s daughter, aged 19, has run off with a
young capitalist-expectant, aged 20 or there-
abouts. It was purely Sentiment and love of
romance which induced them to do it. No one
objected to the engagement, but they felt it was
commonplace, and ran away, and now they have
to meekly return and beg for the necessary bread
and butter, and do without wedding presents and
trousseaux and other things that go to make
ordinary marriages cheerful and delightful.
“At Toorak, t’other day, when Miss Alice
Muir, Mrs. Robert Harper’s niece, married Lieu-
tenant Rowley, R.N., her brunette bridesmaids
brightened their white net and silk by toques of
scarlet velvet and posies to match.
“A Victorian bride-elect, with money to spend,
is at present rejoicing in the safe arrival of h'er
trousseau, forwarded by smart relatives from the
Big Smoke. The apple of its owner’s eye is a
loose, wet-day pelisse of khaki cloth, with scarlet
rosette in the accompanying forage cap. An even-
ing wrap is of red and gold brocade, and a recep-
tion gown of khaki silk lined with crimson has a
long fringed scarf of the latter color, knotted
carelessly round the waist. There are red-fringed
ties and hat-bands to wear with hollands and sich,
and under-skirts, stockings, and notepaper in
khahi tints. For meeker moments of muslin and
chiffon there are black velvet throatlets and brace-
lets, and spangled snakes to coil in the hair;
while, instead of a pearl necklet, there is a long
chain of coral and pearls. Many of the skirts have
box-pleats at the back, with a welcome pocket
nestling underneath.
“A remarkable old Buck has married again
to a young wife, at the age of over 70. The
old fellow is worth approximately £50,000, and
lives in a huge mansion close to the abode of the
millionaire Paynes on Toorak road. He wrote
down the names of four desirable girls to whom he
determined to proposa Numbers one and two
declined, but number three didn’t. She gets a
honeymoon trip to Europe, a white-headed boy
who laughs at the Psalmist’s age limit, and a share
f t, S | tun ® when Cupid resigns him to the care
of Death. Some people profess to be shocked,
prospective beneficiares say it was very careless of
him.
11 "4* <^me war like-red has been rampant through-
out the cold season, and now with warm weather
khaki crops up everywhere. At the winter
wedding (London) of beauty Enid Wilson and
Lord Chesterfield the seven fair maids wore sable-
trimmed scarlet coats over their white frocks of
silk and lisse. The bride’s gown of snowy crepe
shimmering with silver, was draped with point-
lace. Her bouquet was orange-blossom, her veil
Brussels-lace, and her train of ivory-satin under
silver-gauze, was lined with ermine.
‘ ‘ Dorothy Vane was very much offended because
it was reported that her legs were bare as ‘Little
Red Riding Hood.’ Certainly her fleshings were
illusions—one could have sworn that it was her
own calf above the white socks.
Now that ‘ Red Riding Rood ’ is over, one
has time to think of all the bright Victorian girls
who helped to make it go. Carrie Moore is the
daintiest, though not the snappiest boy, on record.
Cesca Vollugi, pretty and sweet, but handicapped
by lack of go and smartness of carriage, so that
she shaped as rather a sheepish Highlander.
Annie Cubitt, in pink frock and dark curls, made
a dear little school-miss, and they’re all vouched
for as ‘ really nice girls, exemplary as so many
baa-lambs.’ All the same, unlike imported dam-
sels, they’re not pampered by local Naicest.
“Want of teeth, or brisker lungs, prevents
Archbishop Carr’s voice being heard more than 20
yards off. At a lecture for the Mayoress’s stall,
in the Town Hall, on Monday night, it was only
by dint of breathless attention, unflagging vigil-
ance, mental straining, and stringing together of
occasional clear-spoken words that anyone seated
half-way down the hall could get a notion of what
the lecture was all about. Those behind could
not have heard a word, but they sat as patient,
immovable, and stolid as the Sphinx— of course
being mostly the faithful of the diocese. Dr. Carr
is a poor lecturer. He introduces the long pulpit
pauses, and dallies like a funeral service. This
sort of thing at a lecture, when the intelli-
gence wants tickling and the fancy an excursion,
isn’t a brilliant success. It was a treat, when the
slow drawl of the Archbishop ceased, to lean back,
close the eyes and absorb in comfort the warmth
and glamor of His Waxellency’s copious speech.
One had to smile to hear the Lieut.-Governor—a
sheep long lost to the R.C. fold—calling on the
Almighty to prolong the useful and beautiful life
of the Archbishop in this sphere of usefulness and
goodness. One just imagined what that urbane
prelate conjured up in his mind at this flourish of
rhetorical piety from the unregenerate one.
“A sensation was created at a fashionable
suburban R.C. church last Sunday by the Jesuit
father declaring from the pulpit the excommuni-
cation of two former members of the congregation
now ‘ living in sin ’in the parish. This is almost
an unprecedented event locally. It is said that
the couple—or one of them—had been attending
church service and possibly partaking of sacra-
ments, else this extreme step forbidding them
entering the church while ‘living in sin’ would
not have been resorted to. One of the parties was
divorced, and the marriage is thus rendered null
and void by Holy Church ; or the position of
Claudio, who defended himself by
“ She is fast my wife save that we do the denunciation
lack of outward order ”
is his, or vice - versa. However it be, the
pair are alleged by the priest to be ‘living in
sin,’ and are a ‘public scandal.’ You can guess
how the water-sprinklers of the parish are aiscuss-
ing the case. Their prayers were exhorted for the
alleged ‘ unhappy ’ sinners that they may come
back to grace. But as they can only come back to
that state singly and separately, and at an
enormous self-sacrifice (it may be presumed) the
prayers are not very likely to ‘come off.’
11 That clever, brave and amiable surgeon, Dr.
Hopkins, is dead of fever in S. Africa. His wife
was bitterly opposed to his going, and he re-
marked to a friend a few days before his depar-
ture, ‘ It has been difficult to persuade Mrs. Hop-
kins that lamin no danger whatever in going. I
fear there is little chance of seeing service, and
even if our men get among the Boers a doctor is in
no danger.’ But the danger lay in disease and
exposure, which are killing more men than Boer
bullets.
ours affectionately.
GOULI-GOULI.
Rumored that the vacancy on N.S.W.
Civil Service Board will be tilled by either a
certain well-pushed Sydney stipendiary magis-
trate, or by a certain brainy member of the pre-
sent Parliament, who was credited with legal
ambitions. In a word, Mr. Ashton, member for
Goulburn, who would probably do justice to the
position and be popular with the service.
The case of Benjamin Bridge, lately sen-
tenced by a N.S.W. court to two years in gaol for
breaking out of prison, brings up again an old and
much-debated subject. Bridge was sentenced to
be imprisoned— i.e., it was ordered that some other
person should keep him adequately confined in a
certain public building. If he was expected to
stay there when the other person didn’t keep him
safely confined, then he was expected to imprison
himself, which wasn’t in the bond and specifica-
tion. The law doesn’t order a man either to
imprison himself, or to flog himself, or to hang
himself, and it ought to act accordingly.
The bacillus which is causing all the
present Sydney plague-trouble, is a very tiny but
active germ. Dr. Lemann, an Indian expert, says
that it would take 500,000,000 of them laid side by
side to cover an ordinary postage-stamp. He also
asserts that inoculation through abrasions or
scratches of the skin, however minute, is the
commonest method of invasion of the plague, and
attributes the high mortality therefrom among
the Hindus to the fact that they go about bare-
footed and wear little clothing, thus exposing any
scratch or cut to the incursion of the bacilli.


ERRORS IN FOOD AND DRINK.
By a Specialist.
It is dawning on the public to try and prevent,
or, at least, to arrest, disease. It is in prevention
that Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa plays an important
part, acting solely as a lirst-class nourishing food
—it strengthens the system to resist, oppose, and
overcome the attacks of disease. You’ll hear
someone say, “So-and-so has a strong constitu-
tion ” ; follow that up and you’ll find that So-and-
so follows the golden rule of being temperate in all
things, and pays attention to diet and exercise.
Does he or she keep up this strong constitution by
taking medicine or swallowing pills ? No indeed !
They have discovered that prevention is better than
cure. Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa places a means in
the hands of everyone to build up and maintain a
sound constitution, which enables its possessor to
travel his life’s journey without the aches and
pains which are in many cases preventible. Thus
we come round again to sound common-sense
based on experience.
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa can be obtained from all
chemists, grocers and stores, or from Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, Limited, 269 George-street, Sydney.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Rudyard Kipling is all right. His position
is secure, no man can question the stability of
his reputation. He figures, miraculously dressed,
as a tailor’s dummy in a Collins-street shop.
Editor Bulletin. —Your paragraph about
the number of the Antill (N.S.W.) family at the
war does not reach the fact. There are seven of
them —two sons and five nephews of Jack An till’s
fathsr!— Jingo.
General Roberts, writing to a niece at
Geelong shortly after his arrival in Africa, spoke
of the Australians as “splendid material,’’ and
said that if his wife joined him in South Africa it
was their intention to pay a visit to Australia
after the war.
Apparently the linotype is just as affable
in its blunders as the old-time compositor. A
correspondent, writing to Melbourne Age about
Vic. dairy-expert Potts, was made to say “I
understand Mr. Potts joined the department as a
dirty expert, lecturer, etc.”
The death of Major Eddy, of the Vic.
Mounted Rifles, will set Australian life insurance
companies thinking. The Major was insured for
£2600 with two local offices, who took him for a
“ good risk ” in the full belief that his soldiering
would be restricted to Easter encampments, &c.
Startling announcement in Sydney Truth
the other Sunday—John Norton’s Truth :
WOE ! WOE ! TO THE CITY 1
IF I WERE MAYOR.
If that is how the fiery John feels, thank Heaven
he isn’t Mayor. Sydney has enough woe already.
The cool capture of a trench-full of scared
Boers by Dr. Fiaschi, of Sydney, as reported in
the press the other day, reads so much like Dr.
Homabrook’s capture of 40 Boers some time ago,

absence.

A smart and pushing Sydney newspaper-
man, who lately went to England in a semi-official
capacity, is to receive an appointment in the
N.S.W. Agent-General’s office —not one of the
present subordinates in which have ever seen
Australia. Indeed, it is more than suspected that
some of them have not yet even heard of it.
When Gov. Beauchamp recently visited a
northern N.S.W. town, the Mayor thereof, who
had to present the inevitable illuminated address,
was in a fearful state of fuss, and kept worrying a
friend next to him as to what he should do next,
until at last the latter exclaimed, “Oh, give the
and kid the picture—and let’s get home to bed ! ”
It comes as a sad shock to the pious
Jingoes of the big Methodist flock in S.A. to learn

most cultured men, who a few years back spent
some time in the province, where he was much
petted by Chief Justice Way, is among the thou-
sands of England’s brainy men to denounce the
war as one based on gold-lust.
J. A. Cameron, who rode out with Mel
bourne Herald Reay to see about correspondent
Lambie after he had been shot, and who after-
wards gave what looks like a frank, honest account
of the Boers’ courtesy and kindness, is a young
N.S.W. native, who one-time struggled with a
handful of public-school children somewhere in
the Mountains. His mother, Mary Cameron, for
years in far western N.S.W., went ov*r to Perth
three or four years back, did much press work,
and died recently. It was she who introduced
her son to journalism there.
In a small way one of the unconsidered
victims of the war is Max Hirsch, the German-
Victorian freetrader. Max was once a power m
that little speck called the Victorian Freetrade
Party. He had a paper in which he published
wonderfully mixed statistics, also letters purport-
ing to be from a squatter (name and address
not given for reasons unexplained), in which
the alleged writer said that since Protection
went out in N.S.W. and the Land Tax came
in he was making something _per annum
more than he used to do. Then Max
would append a footnote to the effect
that he really hoped, after the over-
whelming evidence contained in the
alleged letter of the man who didn’t
sign his name, that protectionists
would see the advisability of imme-
diately dying out. But everybody
named Hirscli is viewed with suspicion
now, and everything he says only
rouses additional doubts about his
loyalty. When Max talks about the
advantages of Freetrade old disciples
mutter that if he says it again they’ll
call the attention of the police, and when
he preaches Single-Tax one-time suppor-
ters call him a Kruger and a blanky Boer;
and if he preached anything else, some-
body would be sure to say that if he said it
again even a law-abiding community would ha •
to stoush him. No cause can afford to have a m «■/'
named Max anything attached to it just now —in
Victoria at all events.

Samuel Tomkinson, M.L.C. (S.A.) turned
into his 85th year last month. Many South Aus-
tralians have an idea that old Samuel is immortal,
and certainly he is the oldest and toughest practis-
ing Tory in Australia. There ought to be a retir-
ing age for legislators as well as for civil servants.
After 65 almost every man is opposed to every
kind of reform for the same unconscious reasons :
“I won’t live to see the results so what’s the
use? ” and “ Where’s the good of trying to reform
the world when I’m just going to leave it ? ”

Said Joubert when last in England :—
I have been warned by my friends not to stay too long
as British hospitality is so insidious that it will either
undermine my health or my principles—possibly both.
Haven’t we noticed it ? Query : How long will it
take British hospitality to worm-eat the very
foundations of any Australian principles still
possessed by the Australian Federal delegates?
After all, it is perhaps safer for a representative
statesman’s country if, like the most illustrious of
the founders of the U.S. Constitution, he never
once leaves his fatherland until he dies.
“Jack ” Spooner, who represents Sydney
E. News in S’Africa, was one of the Sydney
pressmen who attended the execution of Deeming
at Melbourne Gaol. It would be hard to find a
better man than Spooner for battle, murder, and
sudden death reporting. The writer remembers
him expostulating with a prison warder who inter-
cepted his line of vision just as the doomed one
was about to be “turned off.” Remarking that
the warder’s father was “not a glazier,” he thrust
his head over the man’s obstructive shoulder and
watched Deeming’s drop with a cold, critical eye.
“Not a kick !” said Spooner.-

Dear Bulletin. —Mrs. Fraser’s case being
no longer sub judice, let me say, on behalf of her—
to my mind—much-maligned' husband that I
worked for him for a considerable time, about
seven years ago, and never knew a quieter,
more inoffensive, or more even-tempered man. If
one-tenth of what we have recently been hearing
about him is true, never was such a complete
transformation of temperament in so short a time.

Printed that Colonel Bull, formerly of
Castlemaine, and latterly governor of Pentridge
prison, is now residing with his daughter, Mrs.
Raymond, at Varroville, six miles from Goulburn,
N.S.W. Both he and Mrs. Bull, who will have
been married 70 years on April 17, are in good
health, the old gentleman taking his walks every
day, and Mrs. Bull her drive occasionally. They
have living 10 children, 30 grandchildren, and 30
great grandchildren.
Mr. John Connie, ex-M.L.C., who takes
the presidency of Sydney’s Y.M.C. A. vice Knight
Fairfax, was once a politician with a little bugle.
The bugle blew a few blasts in N.S.W. Leg.
Council, and then fell silent. Mr. Comrie returned
to his orchard on the Hawkesbury, where most of
his days pass in peace and prayer. He is also a
book-lover to a degree, and particularly well read
in religious directions. He has, moreover, a repute
for benevolence.

R. D. Ireland, Q.C., Victorian barrister
of renown, was always a dangerous opponent. He
was once engaged in an important action at Nisi
Prius with Mr. (afterwards Judge) Fellows
against him. The latter was famous for his know-
ledge of authorities, and Ireland knew there
was in the Court library a case which would
floor him if cited. Fellows hunted high
and low for the book, but unsuccessfully.
After the hearing was over, and Ireland
had won hands-down, it was found that
the wily Q.C. had all the time been
sitting on the volume containing the
valuable precedent.

Dr. Ren wick, M.L.O., elected, last week,
Vice-Chancellor of Sydney University, was the
first student to receive the B.A. degree in that
seat of learning—43 years ago. The little medico
has been a liberal benefactor of the institution.
He figured awhile in the politics of the Lower
House, but was too forcibly dominated by Parkes
to leave any valuable Assembly reputation behind
him.
Cardinal Vaughan’s denial of Christian
burial to St. George Mivart simply means that
Mivart cannot be buried in a R.C. burial-ground
or with R.C. rites elsewhere. In such cases, some
other Christian Church (if the defunct has been
a society leader, or otherwise distinguished, the
Church of England generally appropriates the
corpse) tenders Christian burial. When a late
N.S.W. Chief-Justice died estranged from his
(R.C.) Church, the R.C. Archbishop declined to
give Christian burial, but the C.E. Archbishop
filled the social gap and let the corpse down
decently.
Treasonable item from London by Henry
Lucy (Sydney Morning Herald) :—
When Cronje set out from Pretoria he, with calm assur-
ance, said he was going straight to Capetown. He is
there to-day, but has arrived by another route, and in dif-
ferent circumstances from those he had planned. It is a
hard fate for a sturdy soldier. Happily he has fallen
into the hands of generous foes, not likely to act in the
spirit Cronje himself displayed when he was pre-
dominant at Potchefstrom. His dauntless courage, his
stubborn tenacity, his fertility of resource have commanded
the admiration of the world. If by chance he should visit
London just now he would be surprised by a popular re-
ception which would be exceeded only by that awaiting
the victorious Bobs. The wonder at his achievement is
complete by contemplation of the fact that he is a simple
farmer, who left his fields to go to the war, and who will
return to them as soon as the war is over. He is the
nearest approach to our own Cromwell modern times have
presented.

Aboriginalities.
Dear Bulletin. —Perhaps the following
will help to explain why small settlements which
prospej in Maoriland are almost impossible in
Australia. The Seddon Govt, recently purchased
11,006 acres of the Elderslie estate, near Oamaru,
the price averaging about £8 10s. per acre. The
hardest there is just over, and here are the returns
Official) from two sections : Section 25—Oats, 126 J
uushels to the acre ; barley 80, wheat 45. Section
22a (over the whole 330 acres) —Wheat, 50 bushels
iper acre. The remainder of the figures were not
available when the mail left, but all the portions
Under crop had been correspondingly good. Is it
any wonder that the Maorilanders bless the name
of John Ballance for the introduction of a system
which enables a whole people to be collectively
prosperous, though possibly none may individually
achieve great wealth? Your Australian figures
look small potatoes alongside of these, and I have
sometimes been. considered an amiable liar for
telling such things to N.S.W. farmers, who
reckoned 20 bushels of wheat a perfect miracle.—
Maobilander.

“ S.S.” : The Bunion has been put
pretty accurately by Tins Bulletin re the in-
fluence of the pay on the paytriotism of the
Australian soldier. A Melb. drummer on a recent
journey outside of Hamilton, Vic., encountered a
woman at a slip-rail. “I say, mister,” she said,
“how’s the war goin’ on?” The traveller ex-
plained that the British were on top and the
Boers having a bad time. “Then my man’s
all right,” said the woman with placid confidence.
“Do you mean to say your husband has gone to
South Africa, and left you to look after this place
alone?” “To be sure he has, an’ why wouldn’t
he? It’s worth it.” “But surely it would pay
him. better to stay here and work the land ? ”
“Divil a bit would it. We’re drawin’ real good
pay, an’ I can get a man worth two of Jim on a
farm for half the money.”

Two young men went in swimming off an
anchored yaclat in Hobson’s Bay on a bright
moonlight night last month. They were having a
pleasant time, pretending to drown, to the great
distress of a black retriever dog that was also in
the swim. Suddenly a large, dark, ominous shape
swept past one youth, so close in the clear moon-
lit water that it touched his arm in passing.
There was a yell of “ Shark ! ” and men and dog
made for the boat in unspeakable funk. The
shark headed them, paused for a moment, says
one swimmer, whilst his (the bather’s) sickening
horror almost drowned him, then made his selec-
tion, cut in—and took the dog. When the two
survivors were yanked into the boat they said
nothing for five minutes, and then “Brandy!”
was moaned faintly. “All the same,” said one
hero, when he had gulped'down half a tumbler of
spirit, “ I think it was ad insult for him to
have preferred the dog ! ” This is an anti-tem-
perance story.
* * • *
A pathetic life-story of an Austrian immi-
grant family makes some little stir about Oxford
(Maoriland). Bernard Banger and his wife came
out, 25 years ago, from a primitive agricultural
region m Mid - Europe. They belonged to an
era when the Austrian peasant was even more
densely ignorant than he is now. They had no
gift for languages, and could never learn English,
and they never could fathom the mysteries of a
new legal system. But they were quiet, clean,
religious, and intensely industrious. In the early
days they contrived to buy a piece of wild bush land
far away from roads or inhabitants, and they pro-
ceeded to build a house and cultivate. Having
gone plunging into the wilderness cm their own in
search of their bit of soil, and knowing nothing of
surveyors’ marks, they naturally built on and cul-
tivated another man’s land by mistake. It was
some years before the other man happened out
that way, and then he ordered them to
quit. All the adjacent legal authorities, in-
cluding the policeman and the goat-inspec-
tor, went out and tried to explain to them
but they couldn’t understand. After a year’s
excitement they were ejected and practically
ruined. Then they plunged again, found the
wrong piece of land a second time, and proceeded
with frantic industry to clear and build. The
new place was mortgaged beyond redemption, and
the owner had left it, so again the luckless Aus-
trians had time to make it into a little paradise
before the mortgagee foreclosed and sold. The
new buyer turned them out again and they went
silently and almost uncomplainingly, believing
that an iniquitous Government had once more
confiscated their property- They built a house on
the public road and were shifted some more, as a
matter of course. Then the police interfered and
found old Banger’s own piece of land—th e one he
had lost or mislaid years ago ; traced out the boun-
daries of it, and shifted the family forcibly on to
it. All the foreigners from the nearest towns
came out and explained to the Bangers
in so many different languages that thi3
was really and truly their property that
they began to understand. So they built
a fourth house in their old age, and attacked
the scrub with the desperate energy of a bull-ant.
But all the foreigners and interpreters and police
in the neighborhood could not explain to old
Bernard Banger that he had to pay road rates. He
simply said, m the guilelessness of his heart, that
he couldn’t owe road rates for he had no road. In
his extreme old age Banger, still belting away
desperately at the primeval forest, owed about 9s.
for taxes. And the Road Board, instead of pass-
ing round the hat and subscribing the miserable
sum, and letting a useful patriarch go on improving
the country, got a Supreme Court order, and sold
the farm. It fetched £2O, and, as the costs of
recovering the 9s. were about £ll 11s., there was
£8 left for the Austrian veteran. It was explained
to the ignorant old hero that, after tearing the
scrub off three farms in succession, he had to go
once more, and he promptly put down his axe and
died as uncomprehendingly as he had lived. The
family’s goods were put over the fence one pouring
wet day on to a stranger’s patch of scrub-land, and
the stranger sent word that they could stay there as
long as they lived and no questions would be asked.
But, even before this permission arrived, they
had put up another hut, and the old woman and
her son—a silent replica of the dead father —were
already belting down the scrub and the big trees
for 16 hours a day, and making the place fertile.
The desire to struggle desperately against big
trees with a blunt axe was in the blood. At latest
advices the State still owed them the £B, for the
old lady won’t sign a receipt so as to get the
money—she thinks the receipt is some new
form of ejectment devilry. Also, she won’t take
charitable relief, nor apply for an old-age pension,
but she is still keeping the house spotlessly clean
and whacking down tne scrub with the courage of
a forlorn hope.

The Pastoralists who in a moment of
aberration constituted so largely in money and
houses to the South African War fund seem likely
to be scourged with a whip of their own plaiting.
The Patriotic what-is-it has been both directly and
indirectly the means of depleting the back-blocks
of from 2000 to 3000, who at ss. per day and
rations all the year round as soldiers, earn a great
deal more than ever they earned at station-work.
The market for bush-workers has consequently
“ riz,” and that purely philanthropic institution
instituted for the good of bush-workers, viz., The
Pastoralists’ Union, has decided to raise the shear-

Old Pat Regan was mighty
careful in counting his sheep.
The jumbucks were made walk
slowly through a narrow race,
and as they passed, each one
was told off with a tap from
Pat’s “ shillaley.” One day a
refractory wether rushed past
the old chap before he could
administer tne usual thwack.
The tapping process, however,
had become so essential to the
counting that Pat had the
sheep brought back. “Oi
haven’t hit him wid me shtick
yet,” he gravely explained.

The price of diamonds is regulated by the output of the African mines.
During 1809 the De Beers syndicate raised the price four times, and to-day the gems are higher
than ever. They cannot fall in price for 12 months from the nature of the mining.

Fare-well, Dreamers!
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
An upstairs room in the Hotel Victoria, with
some 40 of Sydney’s artists, musicians, literary
people sprawling round the dinner-tables in an
atmosphere of wine and cigars. It is a way of
saying Farewell to Henry Lawson and A. Henry
Fullwood —a poet and a painter—leaving Aus-
tralia for the Older Lands. Farewell!
In the chairman’s seat Livingstone Hopkins.
Round the room faces in the smoke—tired faces,
bright faces, young faces, old faces, eager faces,
anxious faces —no happy faces. For they are the
faces of men who in doubt and misgiving, with
many failures and few rewards, lost in trivial
tasks or wasted in petty pleasures, yet constant
in inconsistency, though evil report and good
follow the Dream.
And the Dream of the artist, however small his
talent and weak his wooing, is fatal to the calm
old content men knew in times when Thoughts
were less than Things. For it is never realised in
this world or in any other that we wot of.
Look round!—some of the faces bear the burden
lightly. That trim little Italian with the gay eye
and curly beard is Dattilo Rubbo—he whistles
Verdi as he paints the picture which everybody
praises and so often nobody buys. Never. mind !
Next year —ah, you will see ! Over there is solid-
faced, painstaking Lister—engineer and painter—
year in and year out achieving his regular tally
of honest, excellent work. Go to him and hear
him speak. “ That year,” he says, “ I made more
out of pictures than I spent in frames. That was
a lucky year.” The musician who meets you on
your way back says: “At all events, my pupils
are prospering—in London. Forme? Bah! Do
you know that in the last few years—months—l
nave sung two hundred times for nothing. Think
of it, my dear fellow ! Two hundred polishings
of shoes and dress - suits ! Art spent, music
bought, cabs hired ! For nothing! A man
must keep his name before the public, and ‘ as it
is a charitable object, we feel sure you will not
object to giving your services gratuitously.’ ‘My
services ! • It is my life. Well, my pupils—did
you see how they spoke of in London ? For
me—Bah ! ”
But these are not the tragedies. See that man
yonder —with the bright, jolly face. “ Not a care
in the world ! ” Do you know that he has had no
dinner—no decent meal—for a week, in order to
buy the boots he is wearing ? The old ones were
really too much broken if it had been wet! And
at home his wife and children are sitting in a
carpetless house, with one bed for five of them,
debating whether the butcher in the next street is
likely to give to-morrow’s chops on credit (from
this street’s butcher, alas ! there is no more hope).
And that man is an artist—a mediocrity, no
doubt, but with the power to conceive and execute
beautiful things, things to be prized. Yes, he is
spendthrift yes, he does not persevere; yes,
with despair at his heart, he drinks too much.
Never mind ! Wljen I know what that man is
—how he lives—what he does—what he might do ;
and when I read of this or that successful
draper or ironmonger profiting so many thou-
sands a week; —why, I feel I would gladly
make one of a gang of artistic anarchists to
wring the draper’s neck. Grossly unreasonable,
of course ; but wouldn’t you ? . . . And see how
he is laughing ! What is Tragedy ? Call it Farce
and laugh—“ Ha, ha, ha ! ” And the weary little
wife at home, watering her one little piece of
maidenhair fern in the candle-box —“ Ha, ha, ha,
ha, ha ! ”
_ Hush !. . . the chairman is speaking. That
little elderly painter, with the French manner—a
flavour of happy days in Paris studios that will
haunt him till he dies—is to propose Fullwood’s
health. “A good fellow, Fullwood” —there, on
WILLIS GAUZE, Female Impersonator, with
McAdoo’s Georgia Minstrels.
the right of the chairman, with the blue-grey eyes
and the pointed beard, and his tie flowing in
harmony, with the Bohemian tradition. He is glad
to see you ; he is sorry to go; but there is no
reward in Australia for the artist —“ not even for
the” —shrug!—“first-rate artist. As for us—”
shrug !
We sing “For he is a jolly good fellow!” and
somebody proposes the health of Lawson—“still
the man who has best represented Australia in
literature.” The carefully-dressed little fellow with
the big brow and sparkling eyes is Dorrington the
THAT ABSENT-MINDED BEGGAR AGAIN.
BRISBANE SOCIETY UP-TO-DATE YOUNG MAN: “I say, old chap, heard one of those ripping
Patriotic songs last night.”
ACQUAINTANCE: “ Yaas; which was it?”
YOUNG MAN: Oh—ah—that one of Robert Kipling's, composed by Williamson and Musgrove,
dontcherknow !”
story-writer—a man who will be better heard of
yet. He wants to support the toast. The care-
lessly-dressed man with the carelessly-brushed
hair and the slouch is poet Quinn—fee wants to
support the toast. Everybody wants to support
the toast.
Lawson makes his maiden speech in reply—-he
is tallish and leanish, with heavy dark hair falling
flat on a high, narrow forehead, a mouth that
twitches this way and that in the humour of the
moment, and those great dark eyes —woman’s
eyes, dog’s eyes—full of sympathy and emotion.
He is exalted beyond himself, and looks the most
distinguished man in the room. He has been
much helped in Australia—has made many friends
—yet .. . well, there may be a bigger chance in
London. . . . His eyes are filling—there is a
lump in his throat—he “ will never forget that
night. And when the last landmark at the Leeu-
win fades out of sight there will be a hole in my
heart big enough to put all Australia into.”
11 p.m.—The meeting, is melting—it thawed
long ago. Songs—recitations —-old Tom Rain-
ford’s song the best of all; a fine old fellow, this
basso of 70 years.
Midnight.—A last bottle of whisky, and “ Auld
Lang Syne.” Fullwood is gone—he sails next
day : Lawson is standing on a chair, and we join
hands round him and clutch his hands. Victor
Daley—that thickish fellow with the ruddy-brown
face and the shock of brown-black hair is Daley—
and his is really a Bohemian tie—Victor D iley is
writing a farewell verse —it runs like this
Forty hearts all tried and true !
Forty friends to welcome you /
Come you back in sun or rain ■
Forty friends you'll find again!
Daley signs the sentiment on paper hastily pro-
vided by the hotel-keeper—everybody signs after
Daley.
1 a.m.—The last gathering of a faithful few to
escort Lawson to his ferry-boat. They form a
triumphal arch with elevated umbrellas and walk-
ing-sticks. Lawson walks under it beaming—his
wife is waiting at home to hear all about it. ‘ ‘ And
God bless you all! ”
Farewell, Fullwood ! Farewell, Lawson ! Fare-
well, Dreamers ! Farewell!

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.

woman ; ohine, young woman ; ohineroa, stately young
woman. “ Katalooe ”is not Maori at all. There is no
“1” in Maori and no“oo”in the same syllable. Can’t

Inspiriting item from the pen of Private
Doff, second Vic. contingenteer :
.. . The authorities are very sharp. One man got
15 years for being found asleep at his post, and another
was shot for divulging intelligence regarding' General
Buller.
H. W. Massingham, the brainy young
English journalist, has, since leaving the
Chronicle (from which he was hunted for lus
brilliant anti-Jingo crusade) gone over to the
Morning Leader, and has galvanised that half-
penny slieet into life and energy. Jt will become
a powerful rival of the Daily Mail —the Mail
being a half-penny morning paper of enormous
circulation, run with Cecil Rhodes’ money and
Alfred Harmsworth’s brains on Jingo lines.
Massingham has taken Charles Williams, that
fine old war-dog, over with him, and they have
taken with them the backbone of the Chronicle,
for that once great and fearless journal is but a
shadow of its former self.
Editor Bulletin. —Sir,—The paragraph
in Bulletin of 31st March referring to the
N.S.W. Mounted Rifles’ visit to. England, con-
tains the statement that I spent £lO,OOO on the
expenses of the detachment. This is quite in-
correct. The money to defray the expenses of
the men was contributed by the officers, non-
commissioned officers, men of the regiment, and
their friends. Each officer who accompanied the
detachment, of course, paid his own expenses.—
Yours, H. B. Las setter. [The paragraph in
question stated that Col. Lassetter’s trip cost him
£lO,OOO and was not intented in the least to reflect
on any of the other members of the detachment.
A very popular N.S.W. military captain is re-
sponsible for the statement’s accuracy. — Ed. Bul-
letin.}

To -the Editor of the Herald.
Sir,-*Now that the minds of most people are
oonoerned as to how to prevent the bubonio plague
from gaining, a footing in this colony, and the
several local councils are offering bonuses for the (
destruction of rats, does it not seem somewhat of
an anomaly that the counoils should continue to
charge the Dog Rate when these animals are one
of the most merciless foes of the rodent ? I think
the least the councils concerned could do would be
to abate the tax on any dog of known rat-lallin|
proclivities till all danger from this dreaded
disease is past.— l am, etc.. Schemes.

THE PLAGUE.
ear not the foul disease, though it rage along the qaayß^^
Oh, faint heart, be at ease ;
Wash and pray !
Bathe in the early morn, when the day is newly born,
Weak soul, be not .forlorn, '
Pray and wash !
When the east is growing red slide quickly out of bed.
And swiftly wash your head,
Wash and pray !
And wken day is nearly done, and westward slopes the sun
And the stars come one by one,
Pray and wash I
Oh, quickly soak your feet when the night is falling fleet,
And the evening star goes downward the dull sky-rim to meett
And the parrot crieth sadly in the fields of golden wheat,
Wash and pray
•Wash your socks-oh, wash them soon, and your mangled .shirt
And vourToilar ’neath the beams of the cold white crescent moon,
Pray and wash!
There is but one gospel true and I preach it unto you,
When the plague comes like the spectre that haunts the sombre
yew !
And its bony feet are on the street and tread them night and day
Wash and pray 1 -
But more especially wash this earthly tenement of jjlay
Wash and pray !
R. McN.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Poor ignorant Boers. They ask help
from the God of the Jews !
“ Of course, you will begin your article—
‘ Much as we deplore the Raid,’ ” said Mr. Rhodes,
with a twinkle, to a recent Sydney press-inter-
viewer (E. News). The history of the whole
South African business lay in that twinkle!
George Turner’s excited promise that
every Victorian who voted at the Federation-poll
would receive an ornamental certificate, or pipe-
light, by way of commemoration is likely to be a
lost bathos. after all. It was Turner’s one attempt
to deviate into the flowery paths of poetry, and he
gambolled there about as cheerfully as a stout
elephant Premier M‘Lean has stopped the
dismal proceeding on account of the expense
(stated as £2300 or over), and it will probably
remain stopped. At least there are loud hopes to
fiiat effect.
HER TROUBLE.
FREDDIE: “But your new blouse doesn’t go with
the color of your hair.”
FREDA: " Oh, that’s nothing; I'm tired of yellow
hair, anyhow.”
Et tu Brute ! CaDled that, speaking at
the Savage Club, Mr. C. C. Kingston said Aus-
tralia would give her last shilling to the Mother-
land. Extravagant promises have often been
made at the somewhat irresponsible Savage Club
—generally late at night.
What do the cable people take us for
when they endeavor to convey the impression
that all Gape Colony is yelling out for Britain to
annex the Boer Republics ? Australia knows
perfectly well that Cape Colony has in power an
Afrikander Bond Ministry. And that Ministry Un-
doubtedly represents the majority of the votqrs.
Question for the clergy, who can always
supply a reason for any national trouble : Has the
bubonic plague been sent to Australia by Heaven
as punishment of the country’s action in taking
part in a wicked war, forced upon the British
nation by the machinations of unscrupulous
avarice? Now, gentlemen, don’t all speak at
once.
The latest movement of the Chillagoe
Railway Cos. lies in the direction of extending its
private enterprise line from Mareeba via Atherton
to Mt. Garnet. The Philp Govt, has given various
indications of its pliability, and as the proposed
extension would run through scrub-land rich in
cedar, walnut, pine and beech, thence through fine
pastoral country, the chances are it would pay
splendidly. As for the State running the line and
scooping the profit—well, the State doesn’t count
just now.
After all the assurances that the unswerv-
ing loyalty of “the colonies” had awakened a
feeling of profound affection, &c., the British
Government’s apparent readiness to wreck Aus-
tralian Eederation rather than give up the right
to drag “colonial” litigants all the way to Eng-
land for a final hearing is disappointing. Appa-
rently when it comes to a matter of hard business,
contingents don’t make much difference.
Seditious statement of J. Spooner, South
African war-correspondent of Sydney E. News :—
Most of the Boer prisoners I have seen do not at all
agree with the general idea of the Boer. He is usually
well-dressed, not particularly bloodthirsty-looking, and
certainly clean. In fact, an officer who was in charge of
the prison-ship at Capetown told me that the harbor-
master was always grumbling because they used so much
water. Some of the prisoners are very well off. One
offered £30,000 as bail, but was refused, of course.
Right Hon. J. Bryce, M.P., historian and
publicist, whose judgments were so often approv-
ingly quoted during the Federal campaign
I can remember no case within the last 40 years in
which the real facts regarding a grave crisis have been so
far from adequately conveyed to the British public by the
press. Were these facts known, and their significance
grasped, a strong reaction against the views how so gener-
ally entertained could not fail to set in forthwith.
About the war, of course.
A small, perhaps, but aggressively-obtru-
sive line of re venue-waste indulged by N.S.W.
Govt.-system is the manner of advertising Govt,
notices in the Sydney dailies. The proclamation
shutting Port Jackson and opening other waters
to net-fishing, for instance, would, if commercial
principles ruled, have been amply exhibited in an
inch or two of space, but pounds upon pounds
were thrown away by smothering the useful facts
with such blasts as “ I, William, Earl Beauchamp,
Knight Commander of the Most Distinguished,”
and so on for 14 wholly useless lines of utter rot,
with a similar tag at the end. In other words,
pounds are thrown away where shillings would
give the information in better fashion.
Now is the time to test the strength of
those silken bonds. The latest proposal of the
London mint authorities is that it is advisable to
abolish the melting-houses of the Australian
mints, so that all bullion must be first sent to
London, and then returned for coining pur-
poses. This is practically equivalent to
abolishing the Australian mints altogether,
and it indicates the Imperial feeling with
regard to the long-demanded right to coin
silver. The way the Imperial Treasury
milks Australia over the coinage of silver
will be appreciated when it is considered
that, at the present price of that metal,
the minting of one million’s worth
means a profit of at least £350,000.
Australia sells a mass of its locally-
produced silver in England for a little
over £600,000, and buys it back for
£1,000,000. But this country has been
graciously permitted to coin gold thus
far, because gold coinage isn’t a paying
spec.
Aspirants to appointment in the
Hansard gallery of the Federal Parliament
have begun to move in search of testimonials.
Some representatives of provinces now with-
out a Govt. Hansard (e.g. S.A.) will try to
economise by throwing the reporting on the
press or leasing it out on terms to one or an-
other paper, but members in favor of reports
by Govt, officials are almost certain to be in
the majority. It is singular that although
Hansard, an English private printer’s name,
means in Australia a Parliamentary report by
a Govt.-paid staff, the Imperial Govt, never
had and has not such a staff of reporters. House
of Commons’ Hansard is a private printer’s
speculation.
Ex-Premier Kingston cables from London
that his return to S.A. in time to canvass the
Southern District for the Leg. Council is impos-
sible, and his only chance is therefore a manifesto.
The two sitting members are Attorney-General
Gordon and J. L. Stirling, Tory leader, and it is
the latter whom Kingston wants to defeat.
Stirling is a powerful opponent, and, if Kingston
succeeds, it will be a remarkable illustration of his
hold upon the people.
“ Australia is prepared to give her last
shilling to the motherland.”—Kingston.
S. K. Hocking, novelist and parson, was
the accepted Liberal candidate for a Cornish seat.
But, upon his taking the chairmanship of the
“ Stop-the-War ” Executive, the Jingoes of that
part began a howl; whereupon the chairman of
his committee wrote urging him to modify his
views. And then Hocking :
lam absolutely opposed to this war. I consider it a
crime. I cannot take my opinions from any constituency
or alter thfem to suit any body of electors. I believe with
John Morley, John Burns, A. It. Wallace, Sir E Glarke,
Sir W. Lawson, W. S. Caine, Leonard Courtney, Dr. Clif-
ford and thousands of others that this war is the blackest
crime of the century, engineered by a set of profligates,
and brought to a head by the Mephistopheles of all
Ministers.
While the chairman was wondering what had
struck him Hocking resigned his candidature.
THY BRIGHT SMILE HAUNTS ME STILL.
The Enquiry Clerk or press-agent to be
added to N. S. W. Agent-General’s staff in London
has had his duties thus defined :
“To guard N.S.W. interests, as far as the newspaper
press is concerned, to give all information possible re-
garding the resources of the province to the British press,
and to obtain as much recognition from the newspapers
as possible.”
The definition is Statist Coghlan’s and has been
adopted by Premier Lyne whose late-it promise is
that he will appoint a suitable person in England to
the post. Naturally pressure has been applied to
the Premier to appoint to the duty one of several
present Sydney candidates, but he fears that a
man fresh from Australia would not influence the
English press so immediately as a competent man
sometime resident in London. James Bon wick’s
name has received favor and were he younger and
more recently from Australia he would probably
be invited to take the post.
WHAT WONDER SHE SMILED!
"Oh, I say, Cholly, that pretty girt on the sofa is
smiling at us. Why don’t you introduce me,
and we’ll sit beside her? ”
“ Well, there’s scarcely room for one thing, and she
can’t make room for another thing! ”
The Westralian Separatists propose to call
the new province “ Auralia”—which is a dreadful
title to start business with.
One M.P. in a Southern province recently
found a neat method of vote-catching. Agents of
this tricky politician freely laid voters £1 to Is.
that the tricky one would not be returned,
which, as a matter of fact, was the general
opinion. The candidate’s representative
booky had to pay out a good many pounds;
but the unpopular M.P. got in again,
and the enlightened elector celebrated
his return in beer.
If the advanced jingo ever gets
his wish, and Australia agrees to
contribute handsomely towards the
maintenance of the Imperial army and
navy, it will, indirectly, be a pretty
cold day for the British exporter. Aus-
tralia has so much money to spend
every year—the value of its exports of
gold, silver, wool and sundries. It spends
a great part of it in paying interest on
external debts, mostly in England. It
spends the rest in buying goods, mostly in
England. And if it agrees to contribute
towards the maintenance of the British
army and navy, it has so much less with
which to buy English goods or to pay
English interest. Therefore it must either
be a worse customer to the English exporter
or it must pay the English investor less
interest.
“The Janissaries of the Jews" was not the
only striking thing in John Bums’ anti-war speech
in the Commons. He was talking of Chamber-
lain’s speech :
It was the language of the pothouse and the spirit of
the prize ring.
The Speaker : The expression that another hon. mem-
ber “ used the language of the pothouse” is hardly con-
sistent with the dignify of the debate.
Burns : I will withdraw “pothouse” and substitute
“ Stock Exchange.”
Roberts, M.P., who goes to S’africa with
the Bushmen’s Contingent, intends to hang on to
his seat for Gladstone, if S.A. Parliament grants
the necessary leave. Roberts holds an officer’s
commission, and the nice point arises as to
whether, through thus accepting an “office of
profit under the Crown,” his seat is not liable to
be forfeited. Legal authorities say “yes,” and the
Ass. party may move in the matter, for Roberts
poses as a democrat.
N.S.W. Labor and Socialistic orators who
travelled miles of platform to back up Dibbs,
M'Laurin, Want & Cos. in an attempt to slaughter
the Commonwealth Bill have now reason to fore-
swear parochial sack. The cable forwarded to
England the other day by the three hard-mouthed
Antis prayed that appeal to the Privy Council
may be continued, and represented that the cable-
senders are the mouthpiece of 82,000 voters. If
there was one section of the Commonwealth Bill
that Laborists and Socialists approved more than
another it was that one setting up a local High
Court of Appeal for the Federation ; and to be
appropriated by the grotesquely-Jingo M’Laurin-
Dibbs-Want party as portion of the 82,000 who
will wear political weeds unless the venerable
Privy Council is preserved in its integrity for
their use should teach them to choose better com-
pany next time. For this occasion they should
cable the fact that they are wrongly included in
the 82,000, and that the one wish of their lives is
to live under their own Australian-interpreted
laws. Else they have been, and are, tools.
Writes a Bulletin correspondent from 'the
spot: —
At last one N.S.W. country audience has had a chance
of expressing an opinion upon the war and Australia’s
share in it, uncomplicated by extraneous Absent-Minded
Beggary and enthusiasm about a flag. The true inward-
ness of that Grenfell gathering that presented the “ pro-
Boer ” Holman with a purse of sovereigns wants a good
deal of unfolding yet. For Grenfell is the place where a
great “ outburst of patriotism” was reported some weeks
ago —said outburst consisting of the howling of the chorus
of “ Sons of the Sea ” by about 30 youngsters, and where
the Jingo crowd have muttered for months of the hooting
and dead cats that awaited their errant and eloquent
member on his first appearance. But the Jingo crowd in
Grenfell consists principally of certain bank-clerks and the
local correspondents of the four Sydney dailies, who were
reduced to a state of violent suppression at a big public
meeting. That meeting -was one of the biggest Grenfell
had ever seen (far bigger than one held in the same hall
to hear Mines-Minister Fegan three days before), and the
most orderly and most enthusiastic Grenfell had ever
seen. Holman spoke somewhat laboredly, and seemed
weighted down with an undue need of verifying every-
thing he said by reference to some outside authority. He
quoted a good deal from the English Hansard, and in-
flicted a long historical resume of the facts leading up to
the war upon his hearers. It was not an emotional
effort, and suggested, to some extent, the methods of a
man engaged with great earnestness in proving that
the angles of an equilateral triangle were equal.
But every word of it went home. Gold-miners are tha
least affected by the ordinary hogwash of politics of any
section of the population, and a Grenfell meeting consists
mainly of gold-miners—and of gold-miners of the oldest
and the best type. The bulk of them had never heard tha
case against the war given with anything like fulness, and
Holman’s striking exposition of the contrast between the
facts as they occurred and were discussed in the House of
Commons and the facts as represented in the Australian
press practically captured the whole meeting.

Australia is willing,
But, Charley, when we’ve paid in full
The tidy lot of splosh we owe
For this and that to Bull and Co.
Where will she get the shilling ?
Of course, on second thoughts, we own
She’ll float another British loan !

“S.S.” Have kept an eye on the news-
paper returns of miners killed in Victoria since
■ ‘ our boys ” began to take a band in the South
African worry, and find that three or four Vic.
miners perish in the face to one Vic. soldier who
falls at the front; but there is no fund for the
“absent-minded beggar” who takes his life in his
hand in shaft, and winze, and stope, and no
pension for his widow and the other little things
he leaves behind him; neither does the mob
assemble to yell when he goes down the shaft, nor
the papers print columns about his heroism and
his manly beauty and the rest. “Let me like a
soldier fall!” You bet—it pays best that way.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.*
The so-called Anglo-Saxon isn’t violently
conversational —not even the Anglo-Saxon woman.
When two of her meet in a shop they generally
say inquiringly, “Shopping?” Or if they meet
in a ferry-boat half-way across the harbor they
inquire, “GJ-oing across?” just to make dead
sure that the other woman isn’t going overboard.
And it’s ten to one that when two women meet in
Charon’s ferry-boat, both of them will say in-
quiringly, “Dead?”
Captive balloons are the latest thing in
Paris as plague-preventives when dealing with
clothes. When the French washerlady has done
her little lot, she attaches the flapping garments
to the above vehicle, and up they go to dry in the
clean, frosty air where the dust from the next-
door neighbor’s rubbish-hole and the casual flea
from the dubious rat may not penetrate. Now
that every element is tainted—the fish in the sea,
the rat on the earth, and the flea just above it,
one must get as near to Heaven as possible to
avoid slipping in altogether.

A Chinaman gives, in a Chinese paper, his
impfessions of Maorilanders: —
They live months without eating a mouthful of rice ;
they eat bullocks and sheep in enormous quantities with
knives and prongs. They never enjoy themselves by
sitting on their ancestors’ graves, but jump around and
kick balls as if paid for it, and they have no dignity, for
they may be found walking with women.

EVIDENCE THAT CONVINCES.
Clements Tonic is a Scientific Specific.
A TEUTSFUL EABEATIVE.
Hope and Succour for the Afflicted.
The case of
MRS. MARIA HAPGOOD.
Reported Jor the “ Marrickville Express.”)
The reporter had no difficulty in finding
ne residence of Mrs. Maria Hapgood at
' o. 60 Shepherd-street, Marrickville, as it
s not far from the Enmore tram terminus,
this lady, a well-known resident, was
<>usily attending her well-kept flower
garden, in which she takes a great pride,
in answer to an enquiry as to whether she
had not greatly benefited by the use of
Clements Tonic, and would she object to
giving her experience, Mrs. Hapgood
replied :
“Certainly not; I owe so much to that
remedy for all the good it has done to me
and mine that the least I can do is to give
my testimony, and I recommend it ia every
case that I think suitable. ”
“ How did your own sickness arise ?”
“I do not know the cause, but three
years ago I began to experience a number
of distressing symptoms. My digestion was
seriously impaired, so that my food eauaed
me pain and distress At night I could not
rest, my sleep was broken and irregular.
Then 1 had a terrible pain in the back
across the shoulders, and I was nearly
crippled by neuralgic pains in my hands
and wrists, so that it was with the greatest
difficulty I coma struggle through my
housework. But there was worse to come.
There was a painful enlargement in my
throat, and the back of my tongue became
so swollen as neatly to fill my mouth, and
joined to these troubles I had great depres-
sion of spirits. I did not seem to care how
things went. I went to a friend, a chemist,
and he recommended me a much puffed and
advertised remedy, also a mixture of his
own compounding ; but though I took them
both as directed neither did me any good.
My husband insisted on my going'to our
family doctor, of whom he had a very high
opinion.”
“ What did the doctor say ?”
“'He gave n e no hope or encouragement,
and though I told him all my symptoms, he
did not tell me his opinion of my case or
what was the matter with me Of course
he gave me a prescription, which I had
made up and took without any benefit
whatever. Then it chanced that I came
across one of the pamphlets of Clements
Tonic, and although 1 could see no case
there just like my own, it appeared to me
Clements Tonic would he likely to do me
good. When I oalled on a chemist to
purchase a bottle of Clements Tonic he
strongly advised me not to do so, and urged
me to take what he called ‘ my own tonic. ’
I knew better, he only wanted the extra
fiofit; I had been had that way before, so
was resolute in my determination and
insisted on having the genuine article—
Clements Tonic.”
“ And what happened ?”
“To my great surprise, I derived great
benefit at once ; and I may say that, practi-
cally, that one bottle cured me. All my
distressing symptoms disappeared as though
by magic, the pains in the back, across the
shoulders, the indigestion, the want of
appetite, the neuralgia in the hands and
wrists, even the painful swelling in the
throat and mouth Mere subdued, and I
knew at once I had found a master for my
trouble. I had been worn, weak, and
miserable ; but now my good spirits and
energy returned; it was the most sudden
transformation 1 ever heard of. I said one
bottle cured me, but let me add I have
taken more than that. This sickness I tell
you of was three years ago, and I find by
keeping a bottle of Clements Tonic in the
house and taking a dose at. the least sign of
my ailment returning it is effectually warded
off and my naturally good health preserved
I noticed when I was sick that my distress-
ing symptoms were largely aggravated by
the hot weather, and it is now during hot
spells that I keep on my guard and protect
my health as I have told you, and you may
judge how my health is restored. You
have seen my brother and heard his case, so
you can understand the faith I have in
Clements Tonic, and why I have persistently
recommended it to my friends.”
"And you are wining that this testimony
should be made public !'
“If it is of value do wi.atyou think, best,
you have my full permission; lam only too
pleased to assist any sufferer oit the road to
recovery and point out the genuine remedy
against the host of quack medicines new «e
freely advertised. ”

An invention which should commend i tself
to householders, the Primus kerosene bath-heater,
which instantly heats a flow of water up to HOdeg.,
is being introduced by Messrs. Brandt Bros., lamp
manufacturers, Sydney and Melbourne, price 755.,
also a primus room-warmer, 15s. They announce
they can alter any buggy lamp to burn acetylene
gas without interfering with candle. Their newly
patented portable acetylene lamp with two burners
for inside and outside, price 605., is to be exhibited
at the Imperial Institute, London.*
Premier Seddon intends pulverising J. G.
Grey, chief of M.L. Parliamentary Hansard
staff, when Parliament opens. “King Dick”
wrote Grey asking if he was the author of a
caustic article in New York Times re the despatch
of “colonial” troops to the.war. Grey owned
up, and gave vent to some exasperating senti-
ments. He has made a special study of the
Transvaal question, he says, and is of opinion
that the war can only be looked upon as one of the
most unrighteous in history; that the British
nation has been deluded by a band of grasping
boodlers, and that unscrupulous greed lies at the
bottom of the tragedy now being enacted. Grey
goes on to say that he has viewed with sorrow.and
alarm the progress of the Jingo wave which has
inundated Maoriland, and the maddened rush of
much-needed population from its shores. Hi
thought he was living in a free country, and per
tinently asks the Premier if it is possible that he
has been laboring under a delusion, and that he
is now to realise that freedom of thought and
speech in M.L. is nothing but a sham. Seddon
fairly gasped on receipt of this mutinous missive,
and wrathfully declared that he’d ask Parliament
to settle once and for all the extent of a public
servant’s liberty to utter sentiments opposed to
those of the majority.
