SPORTING NOTIONS.
Hawkesbury (N.S.W.) Autumn meeting (Satur-
day) sustained its reputation as a paradise for Mo. Not
one first favorite got home. Ball was set rolling by alO
to 1 (and any amount of it) chance—Autocrat—landing
the Park Stakes. Jenny Moore (Clarendon Handicap) and
Portland Light (Nursery Handicap) followed, with the
same odds plentiful. Old Wyalong secured the big money
(Autumn Handicap) and Jenny Moore the Welter, start-
ing at s’s—there being two better favorites in each
instance. Manganese and Yellow and Black (first and
second in Rosehill Cup last week) were heavily supported
for the final event, but neither- got a “ shop.” Long
journey from the “’Oxboro” to Sydney supplies ample
opportunity for backers to chew the cud over the rotten-
ness of their end of the game. On this occasion, the
stony faces of professional punters relaxed thoroughly.
Ikey was extra jubilant—great was the harvest.
• * * *
“ Tout ” : Backing ’em is a great game. Owner
of Hawkesbury Autumn Handicap winner (Wyalong),
Saturday, had a sov. on his own nag and two" on the
second—Surbiton.
* * * ♦
“ Gun ”: For the first time for perhaps 30 years
“ Jack ” Mayo’s colors were not seen in Hawkesbury
Handicap last Saturday. The crack Maitland (N.S.W.)
trainer has appropriated so many of these events that he
was popularly credited with having a mortgage over them.
* » * «
“Filbert”: The all-prevailing subject is Don-
caster Handicap. Auld Lang Syne can be well recom-
mended on her Spring running at Randwick, her recent
third when only half fit in Rosehill Shorts Handicap, and
her subsequent good track work. Of other events on
opening day (Saturday) of A.J.C. Autumn meeting, First
Hurdle Race may be won by The Deemster, a Queens-
lander, who jumps in fine style, and is fairly fast; or
Rackstraw, a country performer both over hurdles and
on the flat. For the Autumn Stakes, w.f.a., Paul Pry looks
a good thing, whilst Hautesse should about win Two Year
Old Maiden Stakes. The Leger can only be looked upon
as a match between Parthian and Merriwee (if he starts)
and will settle the moot question as to former’s
supremacy. Without questioning the excellence of
last V.R.O. Derby and Melb. Cup winner, on
recent performances, present looks, and track work, ,
Parthian should once again prove Merriwee’s master.
Concluding race on first day (Highweight Handicap) has
an entry of over 50, of whom a likely trio are My Queen,
Duke.of Kent and Hall Caine. One of the important
events at Easter Monday’s racing at Randwick will be
Champagne Stakes, for tWo-year-olds. Malster’s recent
V.R.C. Hopeful Stakes and Ascot Vale Stakes victories
entitle him to most respect, whilst Haulette would appear
on paper to be next best. Sydney Cup is run over a
stayer’s course, and, therefore, hardest to beat, all things
considered, will be Vocalist, who possesses good stamina
and speed, as shown by his easy win of Tattersall’s Club
Cup. Most danger may be apprehended from Hi Hi, who
has improved with age, and is favored by a light weight.
At a recent Sydney suburban race-meet hap-
pened something but seldom to be seen. For the last
race so many successive scratchings took place that
finally but two competitors were left. In the ordinary
course of things this would be a matter of Congratulation
amongst the metallicians. But in this case it seems to
have evoked the ire of some of them. One representative
was seen to jump the fence, enclosing the flat, and make
his way towards the grand stand. Not many minutes
passed ere it was posted : • All bets declared off.
The reason for this extraordinary proceeding was said to
be, that bookie had laid the two horses, which were left in,
for longer odds than he would have done had he known
that there would be only two runners. Poor soul ! The
public runs every kind.of risk of scratchings and other
combinations not to be mentioned here. But Mr. Booky
has only to go to the stewards if anything is unpleasant,
and he will meet with obsequious attention to his wishes.
A novel and admirable solution of difficulties, but
scarcely reassuring to the public. Shades of Lillie
Bridge 1
* * * ♦
In a prominent Sydney paper are the following
lines to be read under the heading of “ Late Sporting
“ Unless sweep inducements alter owners’ intentions, a
number of Doncaster candidates, entered in Rosehill Cup,
will not sport silk at Rosehill.” How considerate of the
paper to take such interest in the weal of the public !
And how grateful the public ought to be towards its
quasi-benefactor 1 It is, of course, only to the advantage
of everybody that the paper draws attention to the fact
that you will not have any show for your sweep-
ticket if you do not pay a good, big lump sum to
the horse-owner. The great- maritime powers paid in by-
gone days a tribute to the Moors to escape
being robbed. They were unable to get at them a.;d fol-
low them into their holes, so they did the next best thing
and paid tribute. The same thing exactly goes on to-day.
For want of unanimity among the public it has been im-
possible to reach the robbers in their fastnesses, so the
public, as a rule, does as the Poyvers—it prefers being
robbed in part to being robbed of all. And to keep this
last possibility up before the public eye, what is more
handy than the Press? “The Press, gentlemen, the
Press 1 ”
* • • *
. The death of the bluff and good-natured Mr.
John Hourigan, at the age of 75, removes a widely-popular
Sydney turf figure. Mr. Hourigan, though well off, did
not own horses, but there was no better-known racecourse-
habitue, and he followed the game from pure love of
sport. His obsequies were numerously attended.
• m * *
“ Snablats ” is the latest and vilest .addition
that silly craze in turf-nomenclature of bestowing upon
nags the names of their sires spelt backwards. This in-
fliction figured in the list of runners at Moree (N.S.W.)
R.C. races recently.
* * • »
Re the death of Sydney trainer “Bill” Kelso,
and his sack-racing capabilities. Said that Larry Foley
once bet Kelso that he couldn’t, while enveloped in a bag,
get fromsomewhere near Centennial Park gates, Woollahra,
to the Cliff Hotel, Bondi, inside half an hour, and Kelso
won easily.
* * * *
A good spec, would be to go halves with Mrs.
Fraser in Tatt.’s big-sweep tickets. Her luck is so certain
that anybody should be glad to trade with her—Mrs.
Fraser to pay nothing ; the prizes to be halved. N.B.—
No shooting accidents to be involved.
* * * *
Two Northern mares just now in Sydney are
real top-notchers when the word is given. Both won in
their own province with “ impossible ” weights.
They are remarking in Melbourne that patriot-
ism is the last refuge of a distinguished bookmaker.
And he worked the oracle, too.
« * * m
Winner of Dunedin (M.L.) Cup, Mr. Gordon’s
colt Advance, only had three to settle, Saint Ilario being
the most formidable. Punters went bald-headed for Ad-
vance, as out of £l9Ol toted £1044 went on him. He
carried 8.7, and cut the mile and a half in 2.37. Advance
and Screw Gun will make Sea Horse move in the Century
Stakes at Auckland R.C. meeting this month. All three
are same age.
* * * *
Although the horse-sweep is not tolerated in
moral M.L.—thousands a year are thus drafted to Tatt. —
the popular art-union gamble swings along merrily and
unhindered. One recent prize-winner was indignant on
receiving as a “ work of art ” a quaint pencil-drawing of
a wheelbarrow. He was a new-chum, and was much
mollified when the sec., after pointing out that they had
to formally comply with the precious Law, handed him
40 golden representations of St. George and the Dragon.
* * * *
When, as often happens, there is an absolute
“moral” in a two-horse race, and people don’t invest,
knowing that they won’t get their money back if they
support the foregone conclusion, it is comparatively easy
for an owner to strike a big cheque. A cute turfite
operated at a M.L. meet a while back. He distributed
£IOO among books all over the country, and sent his
certainty to the post. No investments on machine ; but
just before flag-fall he rushed in and planked £1 on his
own neddy and £6 on the no-chancer. Result (after
deducting tote’s 10 per cent.), £6 6s. div., though proper
price was about 20 to lon Outside pencillers paid
according to tote, and he netted £630.
* * * *
A versatile skewbald, competing at a recent
minor (M.L.) meet, unexpectedly sneaked home in an
“all-over-bar-shouting” hurdle event, and then went out
and finished in front of a surprised crowd of riders in a
trot.
* * * »
After his Wanganui victories, M.L. colt Ad-
vance was taken to Dunedin, where a “gift” awaited
him, weights for Dunedin Cup (1.) miles) being very un-
wisely declared before Wanganui meeting. With 8.7 he
made “no race ” of it, running the distance in 2.37. Only
three ventured to start against him, and Stead’s maiden
colt, St. Ilario (St. Leger—Cissy) finished second. Ad-
vance’s next appearance will be' at Easter in Auckland
Century Stakes of lOOOsovs., w.f.a., 1£ miles, in which he
■will, through penalties, have to carry 9st. A thousand
pities that Seahorse is wrong, for a “go” between these
two would have been worth a pilgrimage to witness.
* * * *
“Old Timer” : Golden Gate A.C. (Sydney) has
a novice tourney for 8-stoners, and Metrop. A.O. one with
an Bst. 71b. limit, in progress just now. Entrants totalled
120—most of them after the “ crown ” given to beaten
men in the initial round ; this to induce a big entry.
Referee Dan M’Donald, who officiates at both places
(writer wouldn’t mind wagering that each “club” is
being run in the same interests), is a ’cute man and has
dropped to the “ green ’uns* ” little game, with the result
that they are being disqualified wholesale—“ no fight, no
money 1 ” says the official named ; hence “crowns” won’t
have to be paid out quite so freely as hitherto. Fre-
quency of 8 and 8.7 competitions, as Well as the fact that
they always fill well, would indicate to the uninformed
mind that the pug. instinct is more pronounced in would-
be bruisers of that poundage, but this is wrong ; truth is
that the limit is elastic, and, judging by the appearance
of some of the competitors, can be stretched a couple of
stone Consequently middle and lightweights rose up
under the impression that the “ feathers ” and “ ban-
tams” would be cold-pie for them ; this class of stoush-
vendor wouldn’t dream of shaping up to men in his own
weight section. Saw that game little 8-stoner, “ Warri-
gal ” —a clean-built, good-looking Bananaland aboriginal
—give away close on 201 b. as well as a lot in reach and
height, and make things very hot right through five fast,
slogging rounds last Saturday night, but weight won the
day—“ Warrigal ” had to cry ago at last. The darkey
has figured in at least half-a-dozen of these affairs, and
has always been similarly handicapped. Under existent
conditions he and a lot more like him promise to remain
novices till they qualify for old-age pensions.
* * • *
“Old Timer”: Saw Cribb-Broughton farce at
Sydney Gaiety Theatre Monday night. Seldom was so
sorry an exhibition seen in a ring before. Broughton—
lathy, slabby, weedy—had no hope from the word go ; he
was powerless to punish in the slightest degree ; he had a
wild, woebegone look about the face, and, over a head
absolutely devoid of the faintest sign of hair, wore a black
bandage which made him look more like a hospital con-
valescent than anything else —indeed, his condition, ere
the first round closed, indicated that another 3min. of
similar exertion would create need for an ambulance
waggon. If ever there was a case in a boxing-ring
that called for the intervention of the 5.P.0.A., it
was Broughton. Cribb bashed and banged him about
without the semblance of opposition, while.the crowd
hooted and jeered, until soon after the opening of the
second round one of Broughton’s seconds threw up the
rag. Hoots and boohoos—long and loud—came from the
600 or 600 onlookers, many of whom demanded return of
their money. It was the most disgraceful thing writer
has seen in a life-long experience, and those who brought
it about have given the present pug. boom a “black eye”
that it will take a long time to recover from. Affairs
like this should be given a wide berth by the public.
* * * *
“ Steve ” : Went to a much-boomed “ champion-
ship boxing contest ” the other night, and came awav dis-
gusted. If it was boxing, neither contestant showed
more than fifth-rate form. If it was fighting —well, a
championship was won with a teaspoonful of claret and
half a black eye, and without a single clean knock-down
in 13 Queensberry rounds. Also, the heroes had four men
apiece to rake the lather off them in the third round.
Writer didn’t know whether the affair looked more a
paper-chase, a game of dominoes, or a “ dawnce ” —with
about 60 clinches in the 13 rounds 1 The modern prize-
fighter goes to battle with as manj‘ adjuncts as a fine
lady—fans, sponges, whisky, ice, and the rest ; and it
wouldn’t surprise me to see soon added to the list smell-
ing-bottles and manicure-sets.
• • * *
Present boxing-boom has extehded to Broken
Hill (N.S.W.), where, in the days of Goddard, Owen Sulli-
van and 00., there was plenty money for good men and no
hall big enough to hold the crowd that wanted to see
them. Mat. Michie and Jack M’Kell recently had a
“ cut ” for the lightweight championship of the “ Barrier,”
and Michie won on points. Now local promoters are look-
ing for “ Starlight ” to box Queensland Archer and “ Nick”
Duggan to meet Michie. Billy Mitchell knocked out
Ooates, last week, in the second round of a match for the
bantam-weight championship.
• * * *
Sydney bruiser and ex-Pirate Club footballer
Jack Conlon (whose victories over Dan O’Connell and Jack
Gaggin, awhile back, sent his stock up rocket-like, only to
be brought down with a thud, as a result of meeting 11.4
premier Mick Dunn, and.engaging in a return match with
Wyalong (N.S.W.) Jack Gaggin), fights Victorian Joe
Cullen at Golden Gate A.C. to-night (Saturday), after a
long spell from the game. Monday next will see the Vic.
heavyweight Fred Preston and Mick Dunn come together
at Metrop. A.C., Sydney. Dunn, though giving away
nearly 3st., thinks he’ll settle the big fellow.

“ Viator” : Stated that “ Snowy ” Sturgeon (Q.)
will be matched with Otto Cribb. “ Snowy,” in private
life is that rare bird amongst pugs.—a mild and un-
assuming youth ; but in the ring, quite another kind of
person. Fights at lOst. about, and takes care of his
health. Writer saw him win two bouts against heavy
odds.
* * * *
A boastful dark-skinned pug. fell in up south a
while ago. He used to stand on the block and bow to
and ogle all and sundry of the other sex on the off
chance; twice delivered sweeping bows and expansive
smiles to a lady relative of the chief of police. Next day
whilst Sambo was doffing his cady it accidentally struck
a small policeman who bobbed up under his aim. The
charge, assaulting the police, was withdrawn on condition
that he “ got ” before nightfall.
* • • *
Whilom Melbourne “Sewerage Champion”
Billy Edwards has been heard from. He is over in “ the
States ” and, attracted by the Sydney pug boom, promises
to he here shortly. Edwards did well in South Africa
and England, but two Yankees, “ Mysterious ” Billy-
Smith and the “ Barbadoes Demon’’—Chunky Joe Wal-
cott—broke him up. Edwards was ill-advised in flying at
such high game so soon. He tells his tale of woe : —“ I
had one referee (Frank Herald) deliberately hold me down
while he counted me out in my fight with Jim Burge in
Brooklyn ; if the money is against you your name is
‘mud’ here. A club manager got us in the ring ; then
left with all the gate-receipts. We got wind of it and re-
fused to fight; then the crowd broke loose and you should
have seen that club when they got through.
Outdoor* athletics*in N.S.W. have been going
from bad to worse as public attractions for some years.
The following suggested remedies were to have been con-
sidered at the annual meeting of the N.S.W. A.A.A. on
Wednesday night:—That the term “ Harrier” be dropped
—it is a misnomer as things are ; that the Association, as
at present constituted (excepting ’Varsity A.C., Public
Schools and Great Public Schools A.A.A.) disband and
that four clubs on an electoral basis be formed in Sydney
and suburbs ; that no other bodies but these be allowed to
affiliate. Also that provision be made for amateurs outside
the Metropolitan Clubs’ limits to come in as individuals.
The fund for sending top-notch mother-province
sprinter, Stan. Rowley (he holds the Australian champion-
ship) to compete in international supremacy events in
London and World’s Olympian Games at Paris during
July next, is swelling so well that it is more than likely
he’ll leave Melbourne for ’Oine 21st inst. Rowley is en-
gaged to meet W. Shea, G. Moir, and A. Jones (Victoria’s
three best) over 100 metres (110yds.) on 21st.
* * • *
German strong-boy Pagel, who beat Harry
Dunn at Sydney St. Patrick’s Day Sports last year, wants
to meet the Melb. Hindoo wrestler Gunga Brahm any-
where and, failing him, any other man in Australia for
£SO or £IOO a-side. Pagel is only 27 and writer knows
positively he is the strongest man in the business here-
abouts.
• • • *
Sydney bowling trouble has been temporarily
squelched till the Victorians, who are expected this week,
go home again. Then it will be revived, and, if what
some malcontents say proves correct, more coals heaped
on. Requisitionists who wanted the selection committee
bundled out of office withdrew their document, and the
other side consequently claims a climb-down. But this is
not admitted ; those who signed the paper aver that the
association officials’ unconstitutional methods of dealing
with the matter left no other action open to them.
Meanwhile the fire is only smouldering. The home team
objected to shapes well, and gives the lie to the pessimistic
section with whom the wish is alleged to be father to the
thought.
* * * •
N.S.W. Bowling Association’s championship
rink-tourney completed its second stage Saturday. There
are now eight quartettes left in, made up of —City, 2 ;
Annandale, Redfern, Ashfield, Balmain, and Waverley,
one each. City got away with first and second honors
last year, and looks like repeating the performance.
Numerically, City is about the weakest of all the clubs
affiliated, and yet in playing ability it is unequalled. It
holds all the N.S.W. premierships, and just now threatens
to hang on to them.
• * * •
Camberwell crack, Mr. Jas. Pullar, met the
M.O.C. top-notcher, Mr. R. S. Inglis, in the final of Vic.
Bowling Association’s Champion of Champions tourney,
and won by 31 to 20. The contest took place on Fitzroy
green. Mr. Pullar has put up a record for the Cabbage-
garden by winning the supremacy of the V.B.A. twice in
succession, the premiership of his own club and the
Champion of Champions competition already referred to.
* * - * *
Sydney Amateur Sailing Club hibernates for a
few months now —its season ended Saturday with a 16ft.
rating race —a scratch event. Mr. J. O. Fairfax’s Fay got
home first easily—4min. 2secs. ahead of Yankee. Bunyip
was third, 49sec. further behind.
* » • *
Sydney Sailing Club closed its season last Satur-
day with an event that was really a match between the
22-footers Effie and Plover for the Fogg-Talbot prize. In
three previous races these boats had scored equal points.
Effie conceded 2mins. and crossed the line lmin. 4secs. in
front. Plover has gathered in a heap of seconds during
the season but not one first.
* * * *
Re squabble at ’Ome between N.S.W. scullers
Towns (Hunter Rive.) and Wray (Shoalhaven). Late
English files indicate that the pair were no nearer coming
together than ever. Towns still stuck out for the Thames
course, while Wray insisted upon the Tyne. Latter has
right on his side, and explains that the fact of his sup-
porters being North countrymen prevents him giving
way. Conditions attached to the emblem of British row-
ing supremacy (the Sportsman Cup) enjoin that races
must be decided on the Thames and Tyne alternately—
the last took place on the Thames. When Perkins and
Bubear contended for the title (7th Feb., ’B7) both lived
in the South, yet the Cup trustees compelled them to
row on the Tyne to comply with the conditions. Same
thing in ’95, when Englishman Harding and Australian
Tom Sullivan met. Wray reminds Towns that on the day
(Ist May, ’9B) he (Towns) beat Barry for the English
championship, Towns promised to race on the Tyne.
Wray put up £SO as an earnest, but Towns never covered
it. “Unavoidable circumstances” caused a postpone-
ment of negotiations, and Wray “ had to go to America.”
Returning, Wray asked Towns to make his promise good,
hence the Thames or Tyne wrangle.
* * * *
S. A. Cycling League netted over £2OO from its
first meeting on Adelaide Oval on the new'track, which
is pronounced by all riders the best in Australia. How
long the revival of interest will last in S.A. is a question.
The vagaries of one or two prominent bikers have already
led to much talk of stiff riding on the last day of the
meeting.
» * * «
Crack cyclist Walne, purposes going to England
shortly, thence to Paris for the Olympian Games. He
intends making the trip entirely “on his own.” Wants
no exes., but seeks endorsement as Australia’s accredited
rep. The necessary credentials should be forthcoming,
as Walne is quite speedy enough, and no move has been
made in the direction of sending anyone else.
* * ♦ *
Macpherson, who dropped out of interprovincial
cricket five or six years back, is now playing almost as
well as ever. Playing for Mudgee recently his scores for
three successive Saturdays were 103, 90 and 74—a1l not
out —though, of course, it wasn’t against such bowling as
Noble’s, Howell’s or M'Beth’s. He was left out of big
cricket because he would get himself out through trying
to copy Gregory’s leg-stroke. If Mac. were in Sydney
now, he would be recognised as equal to Jansen and
several other promising colts, but when a country-player
leaves big cricket he leaves it once for all.
*. * * *
Central Cumberland got away with Sydney
premiership cricket competition, holding four points more
(12) than North Sydney and South Sydney. Waverley
was third (6), while Paddington and Redfern were
bracketed together with 2 each. 0.0. has won the honor
for the first time by first-class all-round play, to which
every member of the team contributed. The aid of
Howell, Pye, and Farquhar was certainly a big factor, but
they didn’t do it all. Neither E. Sydney nor Glebe won
a match.
David Syme has discovered a solution of
the Imperial Federation difficulty, and is so
pleased with himself that he prints the inspiration
in “caps.”:—
One method suggests itself to us, and only one, and it
is this—to provide that representatives' from the
COLONIES SHOULD SIT IN THE HOUSE OK COMMONS WITn FULL
RIGHTS OF SPEECH ON EVERY QUESTION, BUT WITHOUT THE
RIGHT OF VOTING ON ANY.
Thk Bulletin’s idea of Imperial Federation is to
send D. Syme and The Age to London, and let
them take possession of the Empire in the natural
course of events.
It is interesting to readG. W. Steevenson
the various British Generals and officers who
served in the Soudan, and consider his eulogies in
the light of recent events. Thus :—
Colonel Broadwood, a rapid, adroit and daring leader :
long-legged, light, built for a horseman, never tired,
never more than half asleep, never surprised, he is the
ideal of a cavalry general.
The “never surprised” stands badly in need of
correction since the affair of Koorn Spruit
the other day; and as to the “never more than
half asleep,” it looks as if somebody had been only
half awake when the Boers captured the seven
guns and 300 men more or less.
“David G.” ; If, as Frederic the Great
wrote, the commander who allows himself to be
surprised deserves to be hanged, what is to be said
for Colonel Broadwood, who, with plenty of
cavalry at his disposal, allowed himself to be
caught at Reddersberg in full daylight? The
whole story has not come out yet. The Boers
were allowed to carry off the 7 guns and 80
waggons they had seized, arid it took a division of
regular British infantry, and another of cavalry—
in all some 15,000 men—to get the relics of Broad-
wood’s column out of their clutches. Also, they
were allowed to cut off the water-supply from
Bloemfontein. A bombardment of Bloemfontein
is not at all off the cards,
A.n inspired article in-one of the Sydney
dailies points out that owing to the long Govt,
delays and tergiversations regarding the Pacific
cable, Australia is placed unreservedly in the
hands of the monopolistic Eastern Extension Co.
So it is ; —and all the more reason for getting out
of the monopoly’s hands as quickly as possible.
The Pacific Cable Board in London recommends
refusal of the conditions on which the Eastern Ex-
tension Co. bases its offer of a Cape cable—seeing
that these are designed to ruin the Pacific cable and
preserve the monopoly. And as for hints that
the co. may do terrible things unless it
gets its own way, the co. isn’t game. Any
attempt to mulct Australia would be followed by
British reprisals; and the whole Australian busi-
ness, is a fleabite to the Eastern Extension Co.
when compared with its interests in Britairi. So
it will take precious good care not to go too far
with the veiled threats now being circulated to
alarm the ignorant.

Wool. —Extreme quietude has been the
prevailing feature during the past week, and the
amount of business transacted by private treaty is
so small as to give no indication of the course of
values. Buyers show no inclination to operate,
and, generally speaking, the tone of the market is
weak and depressed. A further fall in the price
of “ tops,” amounting to l|d. per lb., is reported
from Bradford. This is a serious drop, and
present appearances indicate that the downward
course of prices for wool, which started in Janu-
ary, has not yet been arrested. The prospects,
therefore, for the next auction sales, to be held in
the local market on the 25th and 26th inst., and
also in London on the 8th prox;, are far from en-
couraging, and, certainly, no improvement i
values can be looked for. Sydney, April 10, 190

Stock-broker George Meudell, who is
standing for the vacant seat at Grenville, Vic.,
has introduced the phonograph as an election-
agent. As he can’t speak at every place in the
constituency, he has delivered a speech into the
’phone, which will be taken round the district and
fired off ten times a day. Electors, it is presumed,
will be allowed to ask questions of the machine —
with results quite as satisfactory as those usually
put to candidates in person. And as for pledges
given by phonographs—well, are they not im-
printed on wax ? Still, George Meudell is clearly
an original man.

Handsome Olga Nethersole is said to have an assured
fortune in the dramatic version of Daudet’s “Sapho,”
which she let go at Wallack’s Theatre, New York, a few
weeks back. Rmnor gave it credit in advance for being
an awfully naughty play. Then, just as New York was
preparing to feel shocked at “ the disgusting thing ” Olga
Nethersole fell sick, and sassiety had to postpone its
shock for a fortnight, during which time the health of
the patient “ gave much cause for alarm.” New York, in
fact, began to fear that the outrage on decency would
not be committed after all. With such a boomsome start
“ Sapho ” couldn’t fail to score a big success. Its moral
tone is generally deplored, and seats are booked six
weeks in advance, and Miss N. gets high praise for her
powerfully amorous appeal to her “ boy ” in the third act.
Somehow, the spiciest bits always seem to come in third
acts. Then Olga has been tried and acquitted on the
charge of producing an indecent play, which is a stupend-
ous advt.
Olga Nethersole, she of the goggly eyes and luscious kiss
—yum, yum ! —has been acquitted at New York on the
charge of producing an indecent play—“ Sapho,” to wit.
“ When the curtain fell upon Jean and Fanny at the top
of the spiral staircase, presumably in Fanny’s room, there
were calls for the twain. The curtain rose, but they did
not appear. Miss Nethersole was anxious not to spoil the
illusion.” Clement Scott (still in the States) protests :
“ We do not want these mock Magdalenes to come flaunt-
ing on the stage to ‘ fill the blood with devil’s leaps and
poison half the young.’ ” The local chief-of-police, it
seems, was quite pleased with the play: the prosecutor
was a private person. Yum, yum! Also Advt.
When the beauteous Maud Jeffries was here with Wilson
Barrett’s co. she threatened to grow fat, and people who
knew Barrett’s artistic tastes prophesied that she and the
Barrett co. would part company before —well, before many
years. Now it is announced she is severing from Barrett
in June. She has made no definite plans for the future,
but may visit either America or Australia. The fascinat-
ing Lillah McCarthy (who was “ second lead ” during the
Australian tour) will now be promoted to the heroine’s
parts. “ Mr. Barrett,” it is remarked, “ has had rather a
brilliant succession of feminine stars associated with him.
Miss Eastlake, the golden-haired favorite of London play-
goers 15 years, or more, ago, was succeeded by Miss Wini-
fred Emery, who later on climbed into fame from the role
of understudy to Ellen Terry. Then came, nine years ago,
Miss Jeffries. How long will Miss M'Oarthy’s reign last ? ”
It would seem that the scrumptious Maud “ would in any
case have to leave Mr. Barrett’s company for some months,
as she has a certain very complicated business matter to
attend to in America. It is concerned with some thou-
sands of acres of cotton plantations in the Mississippi
Valley, which have belonged to her family for many years.
The title is disputed, and her presence on the spot is con-
sequently necessary. Being entirely uncertain as to the
time the litigation will occupy, Miss Jeffries has con-
sidered it best to resign her engagement, at any rate for
the autumn season.”
After the Castles boom at least a dozen Australian
nightingales were going to fly north ; but so far only two
or three have had wing-feathers enough. The last to go
so far is Miss Lulu Gillespie, of Adelaide (a niece of
Premier Kingston) who travels under the wing of her
Australian music-master, Albert Fairbairn. Also, by the
way, he is her husband.
The squeamish patriotism now indulged in by sections of
the community is remarkable for the curious phases that
are sometimes presented. An advt. last week of “ a grand
patriotic concert ” at Sydney Town Hall, announced that
a patriotic cantata by Hugo Alpen would be repeated, to
be followed by Mons. Aug. Wiegand on-the piano. Master
R. Riemenschneider was to represent Young Australia
with a patriotic song, the Meistersingers were to come
along with their little lot, while it was given out that
Nicholas J. Gehde (composer of “ Sons of New Britannia”)
was to be accompanist to the whole show. What are
those individuals who are always carping about the
foreigners in the land going to say to this? Probably
another Boer plot, carried out by pro-Boers at the insti-
gation of Leyas 1
W. J. Turner, one-time a figure in Melbourne’s musical
world, died of brain-softening last week. Turner was a
traveller or warehouseman in the wholesale “ rags ” line
before he called himself a professional musician, and,
although he played organ solos and piano accompaniments,
nobody took him very seriously as an entertainer. His
talent was much less evident than his self-confidence on
the platform. But as a concert-manager “W. J.” long
held his own against all-comers. Ada Crossley, Lalla
Miranda, Gladstone Wright, and many other good artists,
were members of the co. that Turner got together at Melb.
Exhibition-building, some 10 years back. The concerts
yielded little grist to the co., no matter whether they
were on salary or sharing-terms, yet the “ conductor ”
somehow persuaded them to battle on under his banner.
Of late years, Turner’s concerts got to be few and far
between, and the man himself seemed to “ lose his head,”
in the physical sense of the term.
Ada Ferrar, when last heard of, whs playing Hermia in
" A Midsummer Night’s Dream ” at London Lyceum,
under the management of an aspiring actor named Benson.
Engagements at the Lyceum, unfortunately, are not the
solid things they were when Irving ran the house. Ben-
son’s season is only an eight weeks’ matter.
*• Fax ” : Somebody speaks of Irene Franklin, at Melb.
Bijou, as “ a petite American person.” She’s a rather tall
girl and not small-boned for her height, judging from her
figure in a green frock. Irene’s feet are large, as dainty
’Murkan feet go, nor has she small hands. But they are
very pretty hands, and her wrist play is the strongest
point in the young,stranger’s likeable performance.
Melbourne is suffering from a sudden influx of-negroes,
all of whom have managed to secure particularly fine
stylish girls. They parade the main streets in couples,
and the envious glances cast on the women by others of
their sex proclaim that there are many would-be Desde-
monas in quite unsuspected circles. A Melb. policeman
relates that when the Fisk Jubilee Singers were here, he
found the lane at the rear of the stage blocked up with
attractive young women. “What ever are all you girls
waiting for ?” he asked. “We’re waiting for our boys,”
was the smiling reply of a charming blonde. The enquirer
looked up at the programme. “What!” he asked,
incredulously, “ The Coons? ” And as the girls nodded ;
he inquired, “ Can’t you find enough white boys, without
taking up those black fellows ? ” The assemblage tossed
its golden curls contemptuously—“ Pooh ! ” the spokes-
woman proudly retorted, “.any girl can find a white boy,
bat it takes a pretty clever girl to mash a black One."
There are persistent rumors of a case of
plague in a certain high-placed Sydney family,
and that such is officially known, but patient has
not been removed, and only a state of semi-
q[uarantine has been established. If there is noth-
ing in this rumor it should be officially contra-
dicted. If there is, then the officials responsible,
no matter who they may be, should be biffed
right out of the public service.
At long last there are signs of a munici-
e -owned electric-lighting system in Sydney.
s ago, the City Council got an Act of Parlia-
ment authorising it to light Sydney and its
suburbs, and it lias lain insensible on top of the
precious document ever since. It is proposed to
float the inevitable loan, and experts reckon that
the work can be made to pay even by selling electri-
city at a rate which will be equal to gas at 3s. per
1000ft. In fact, though the matter has only got
so far as deciding to call for tenders, it is already
being estimated that, by making no serious effort
to pay off the debt incurred for the electric-light-
ing plant, but just letting the liability stand, it
may be possible to cut down the charge to some-
thing about equal to gas at 2s. 4d. or 2s. 2d. per
1000ft.
Ex-Premier Reid remarks, correctly
enough, that if the British Government insists on
altering the clauses in the Commonwealth Bill
dealing with Privy Council appeals, it may as well
be invited, at the same time, to put in the pro-
posed new clause allowing Westralia to maintain
its separate tariff for five years. There is not a
farthing’s-worth of evidence that the said conces-
sion will make John Forrest any less treacherous
a wobbler than he is now, but if the labor of put-
ting another amended Bill through has to be faced
in any case, the concession may as' well be made
by way of experiment. At the same time, there
is so little hope in the experiment that nobody
save a fool or an enemy would propose going
through the weary process of amendment merely
to propitiate Wobbler John.
“ Banjo ” writes to his daily papers : —
After disposing of Cronje Lord Roberts came round to
the hospital to see the wounded Canadians, and he again
expressed his delight at the style of the tents. “ Splen-
did hospital,” he said to Lieutenant Martin. “ Where
did you say it came from?” “New South Wales,” said
the doctor. “ Ha—that’s Adelaide, isn’t it” said the
little General. Comment is useless ! Adelaide, indeed.
When it was quickly but firmly explained to him that the
hospital came from a much better place than Adelaide,
he said to one of his staff —“ Ha ! Make a note of that.
Make a note where the hospital comes from.”
And that’s all they know about us, the best and
most intelligent of them, after all the fuss ! Yet
Lord Roberts is personally not unconnected with
Australia: two of his wife’s sisters reside in the
Yic. Western district —one the wife of Ven. Arch-
deacon Beamish, of Warrnambool.
Melbourne Age repeats the indignant
statement that “President Steyn ordered several ,
Englishmen who were Free State bur//tiers to be
shot because they refused to fight against the
British troops,” and Brisbane Telegraph cries
out for a terrible r-r-revenge, and calls these indi-
viduals “Martyrs for England,” and alludes to
the episode as “ Cruel Murder.” To any unbiassed
person it looks like the execution of several Boer
deserters by ordinary process of law. When
these individuals became Free State burghers,
and it is expressly stated that they were such,
they necessarily forswore and turned their backs
on England, and swore to be true citizens of the
Orange State, to obey its laws, and fight in its
quarrels when called on, against their own
country or any other country. In short, they
ceased to be English, or to have any English
rights whatever. And when the levy en masse
of the capable male population came they proved
traitors and deserters to their new country —and
were executed. You can’t eat your cake and have
it too, but that is what the Transvaal “fran-
chise” brigade desired.
London went fairly mad over the relief of
Ladysmith. The most serious outbreak of lunacy
occurred at the Bank of England where “the
Governor and principal officials assembled at half
past four in the private drawing-office and sang
the National Anthem, while the clerks from all
the offices attended with flags and banners .” To
anyone who has ever seen one Bank of England
Governor blow his nose during business hours
there is something awful in the mere thought of
him meeting together to sing at 4.30 p.m.
precisely. And the assemblage of clerks armed
with flags and banners !

At a Burial.*
Tenderly down the hill we bore them,
Riddled with bullets, shattered with shell;
Never a cry was lifted o’er them,
Never a tear above them fell.
Friendly came the Boers beside them,
Muttered, “ Poor fellows, so worn and thin !”
Helped us to hollow the trench to hide them,
Helped us to carefully lay them in.
Hornily-handed, rough of faces,
All their battle-wrath passed away ;
It seemed the hearts of the sundered races
Were one in love of the dead that day.
Solemnly, then, we read the verses,
“ Ashes to ashes ! dust to dust!”
We gave our mates to the last of nurses—
The pitiful dust in whose peace we trust.
Kindly up there stepped a foeman,
Stepped to the grave and prayed a prayer; »
Never a son of a British woman
But felt the breath of the Lord was there.
Faithfully, humbly, did he prav it—
Prayed to the Father of foe and friend
To look from Heaven at last and stay it,
Make of this terrible war an end.
Plaintively then uprose their chorus—
A hymn to the God of the warless years ;
The tender heart of a girl came o’er us ;
We sobbed, and turned from the grave in tears.
* It will be remembered that an English surgeon,
writing a description of the burial of English soldiers at
Ladysmith, tells how the Boers prayed for these and sang.
The English were deeply moved thereby. In Westmins-
ter Gazette appear the above verses by H. D. Rawnsley
that relate the story again.
Before landing at Kingstown the Queen asked for a sprig
of shamrock, and wore one plucked from the Victoria
Wharf. The journey from Kingstown to Dublin and
through the streets of the capital was marked by a display
of extraordinary and uninterrupted enthusiasm. Her
Majesty’s progress was described as a long triumph.—
Cable.
’Twas a noisy day for Erin when Her Majesty the Queen
Passed in triumph through the streets of Dublin town :
She wore a kind expression and a sprig of shamrock
green—
Which reminds one that the “ green ” is rather brown.
It was honey and molasses
For the pleasure-loving masses
(Shure, a fine, imposing show had been prepared),
And our aged Sov’reign Lady
Thought, when Mickey waved his “cadey,”
“lam glad to find how easily he’s ‘ squared ’ ” 1
All the illustrated papers had their “ specials ” on the job,
O, what fervent demonstrations we shall see
In the works of art intended to delight the honest snob
With his grovelsome ideas of Loyaltee.
There’ll be sketches unveracious
Of the Monarch looking gracious
Whilst the greatness of her goodness is declared,
- But they’ll give no indication
Of her inward jubilation
At the thought of how the Micks are being “ squared.”
THE WHISKY OF THE WISE
Is the title bestowed on “D.C.L.” Scotch (Black
Bottle) by many hotelkeepers. They remark that
the more brainy customers, veterans who think
twice before ordering, say—“ Mine is ‘ D.C.L.’ ”*
“ How is Fred ?” was a cant query among
Capetown stockjobbers for some time after the
Spion Kop affair. A German “bear” got a
warning telegram, “Fred is ill,” from his con-
federate in Natal on the strength of local rumour
that the Kop had been abandoned. The press
censor passed the message without the least
suspicion of the fact that Fred’s illness signified
another British check, and the Capetown Deutschor
sold a lot of Rand stocks before the inevitable
slump.

“ Food for Thought/'
A NYTHING which causes a drain on the nerves helps to
make a SUSPENSORY necessary. Excessive mental
effort, prolonged and exhaustive physical exercise, over-
indulgence in any way, dissipation, irregularity of diet,
loss of sleep, anything which produces fatigue has its
first effect on the vital organs. A sick man, or a tired
mstn, has not much vital vigor, the strain tells on the
most delicate nerves first, fatigue there produces fatigue
everywhere. Fatigue of the vital organs leaves a man
with little energy, with little strength to work or think.
The connection between the brain and the vital organs
is most direct. Excessive mental work affects the vital
power, and abuse of the vital power affects the brain—a
strain on one is a strain on both. A SUSPENSORY stops
one strain on one. It is not a big strain, but it is there
all the time : it is one little strain multiplied by the
number of seconds in the day. It is a continual drag,
which empties the store of vitality just as a reservoir may
be emptied drop by drop.
We have been saying “A” SUSPENSORY. What we
really mean is an

FOXS’ PURE INDIGO.
As the result of many years of experience, FOXE PURE INDIGO DYE is recognised as the best in the Market. The superiority of Pure Indigo over other
dyes may be briefly summed up as follows :
FASTNESS OF THE DYE.—lndigotine, the colouring principle of Indigo, is insoluble in water, alcohol, ether, and dilute acids, and alkalies either hot or
cold, hence its extreme fastness for all conditions of ordinary wear and tear. It is also quite fast to Sunlight, and for an all-round dye there is nothing supe ior to
it. There is no need to point out the advantage there is in a perfectly fast dye, as compared with one which may be fast to light, but will not stand perspiration, or
washing, or vice versa.
STRENGTH AND HANDLE.—CIoth dyed Pure Indigo is stronger and tougher than the same cloth dyed in other ways, owing to the dye bath being at
a comparatively low temperature, and the absence of any injurious chemicals or mordaunts. In almost ail other dyes, the cloth is first boiled in the mordaunt and
then in the dye for some hours altogether. This always impoverishes the wool fibre, and destroys much of its natural lustre and softness, whereas the Indigo Vat
with its soft, mellow liquor, adds to the fulness and richness of the fabric. Indigo Dyeing being a mechanical process rather than a chemical one, actually feeds the cioth.

“Camel, Thou art Meek and
Mild!”
[FOR THE BULLEIN]
After considerable groaning, swearing and vexa-
tion of spirit, our camels were hobbled, and
shuffled off with grunts of relief. In a few
moments the salmon gums came and went as the
fitful flames of our fire curled round the “billy.”
“Tinned-dog” and damper were snatched from
the tucker-box and we were ready to assimilate,
when both of us abruptly started to our feet at
hearing the clank of a muffled bell striking against
hobble chains, suspended on the necks of camels
stalking quietly towards our fire.
Leading them was a white-bagged Afghan, who
halted at the fire and begged that he might camp
near us. We agreed, compelling him to drive his
camels in the contrary direction ours had taken,
for at that season these male beasts were not lamb-
like, and already ours had been chewed quite
enough by divers pugnacious bulls, which had
coursed our weaklings merrily through the mulga,
with an accompaniment of jangling bells and
bubbling roars.
The leading camel of the Afghan’s team was a
huge bull, tricked out in bright-dyed goat hair-
cloth, and shell-encrusted garters. Throwing his
head high in the air—his bladder dribbling from
his foam-covered mouth —he danced and reared.
The sight of it was disturbing.
Nose lines unfastened, hobbles adjusted, bells
swinging, there ensued a little pandemonium, for
the leader charged the others furiously, scattering
them with marvellous celerity. That done, he
started back to the fire, and was received with
vehement disapproval; he pondered a while as we
energetically banged him with assorted missiles,
then reluctantly retired to scare up diversion else-
where.
That camel kept the Afghan and a nigger he
had with him fully engaged while we ate our
damper and drank our tea unruffled —the camel
gurgling horrible threats every time the Afghan
found it necessary to administer to his wants, with
a slip-rail, screecning barbarous falsetto oaths the
while. Then, having perspired copiously, the
Afghan would resume his munching at the fire.
His journeys were frequent, but the last had
come. A resounding screech of agony succeeded
by repeated yells made me instantly remember
seeing a victim of an enraged bull-camel; and,
filled with alarm, rushed towards the place the
yells proceeded from.
Like a meteor-gleam flashed a white streak ; a
streaming bread-cloth shimmered in the wake of
the yelling heathen as, with mighty plunges, the
camel bounded after the unhappy Asiatic—the
hobbles jingling and the bell one perpetual clatter.
The Afghan reached a tree, and was now sobbing
with pure fright—my mate was valiant with a
rifle, ensconced in a fork of a tree. I had picked
up a dead gum limb, and approached the
camel, who steadily pursued his man round
and round. I had heard yarns* about eye-
ing beasts and soothing them, too; I didn’t
hanker to sooth this one—save with my timber;
and I did contrive to bring it with all the force I
could muster on his forehead. Crash went the
broken stick, and the camel flung back his head,
then thrust it forward and came for me. I didn’t
wait, but skipped, shrieking, over the landscape
till I also reached a tree with a thankfulness that
was extensive but fleeting, for with his long neck
the brute dexterously ousted me and forced me
out on an open flat with a dried watercourse inter-
secting it. I was a fair runner, but, though the
camel was hobbled, in a few seconds his loathsome
breath was on me. I dodged to one side and the
ungainly brute lurched on, while I made for the
timber again, and then, with a groan of terror,
tumbled into the unseen watercourse, and experi-
enced that “ freezing ” sensation as his big fore pad
thumped my shoulder. Another second and I was
stumblipg over box-bush at the base of a clump of
gimlet-mallee and yelling to my mate to shoot.
He, safely perched aloft, advised me to do those
things which I gladly would have done if I could,
and m answer I cursed him as we coursed round
the bushes, beseeching him at times to drop the
rifle. Expedients flashed across my mind, none
feasible, until happily I gained another clump of
“gimlet,” the radiating stems of which prevented
the near approach of my enemy. Charge after
charge he made, but meeting the strong poles with
his shoulders he could not reach me, while I
reached him, hard and often, with a stick.
Then I suppose he heard the bells of our camels
and charged towards them, leaving me free to get
my revolver, with which I made my mate drop
the rifle, and swore I’d shoot him if he came down.
The Afghan was weeping; and after some hard
swearing he went off to bring more Afghans to
help catch the refractory brute. Presently hear-
ing roars, clanging bells and gurglings, I knew
our camels were being patronised, and I made
towards the noise with the rifle ; but the sounds
were further and further off.
I dropped on to my blankets and fell asleep.
When I woke there were a number of Afghans
about, and the serang observed : “ Quiet camille !
he not hurt anyone—ver’ quiet, Meesta ! ” I told
him he’d better go out and led the “ quiet”
in !
My mate had come from the tree and was talk-
ing in that large, breezy style common to his type
in addressing supposed inferiors, when more
gehenna broke out in the scrub and out came our
camels across the clearing, straight for us. We
guessed who was following and the survival of our
simian arboreal instincts was well exemplified by
the agility with which we got up the nearest trees.
Then said Ito the serang, “ ver’ quiet camille; he
not hurt; good camille ! ”
It was cold up there, with my shirt fluttering in
the night breeze, so I clambered down, swearing
at myself for being such a funk, and picked up the
rifle for a pot-shot at that camel, but the Afghans
flooded me whilst I kicked my protestations.
At dawn the Afghans—each armed with a six-
foot sapling—surrounded the brute; but no one
went within an acre of him until after many false
alarms and yells to “Oushta!” they got a long
rope doubled round his legs and threw him. Then
amidst much screeching, roaring, froth and dust,
they civilized him with their saplings, and once
the nose-line was fastened, he became tractable,
though a week later I saw him again in the Afghan
camp, tied down, and being vigorously admonished
w**h saddle-straps !
O. THOOSER.
Thirsty.
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
The wide downs stretching northward were grey
as a dead face, and the timber, which marked the
creek, looked like a black wound upon it. The in-
sistent winds blew over it as the winds blow at sea;
yet, unlike the sea breezes, they did not refresh.
In every continent the desert and the horse
breed their type. Dan Maher —lean and wiry, high-
nosed and keen-eyed, with the thin straight lips
of the roughrider—was of it. Dan Maher who,
out of wind and sun under a coolibah, sat think-
ing. Yet his perception of trivial things was
closer than usual. Not a yellow bloom from the
sebbanias fell on the black water before him that
he did not see ; he did not miss the slightest eddy
of wind that, breaking through the tall cane:grass,
stirred those already shed, while all the time his
pulses hammered one refrain in his brain: “I
will not die thirsty ! ”
Long ago he recalled how his horse, wheeling on
a claypan where cattle had camped, came down
and broke his thigh. The fever of thirst and pain
was in his blood now as then. Yard by yard he
crawled the weary road again for water —that ten-
mile drag, where every tussock was a struggle and
the rises of the downs were mountains to be
overcome. And then the miserable shade of the
lightwood, two miles from his goal, where he had
almost given up —five years ago ! Yet every limb,
almost every leaf, was in his vision still. He had
taken out his revolver then as now, but was with-
held from “ chucking up the sponge” by that one
thought—he would not die thirsty. And at last,
having won the miserable three days’ supply, how
he had defended it against crow and plover
and pigeon ! Then came the recurring thought,
“I won’t die thirsty now, and nothing else shall
drink with me,” with the same grm of self-
contempt and pain.
A tiger-snake on the opposite bank crawled
along the soft mud left by the vanishing water,
hnd thrust its head and neck down crab-hole after
crab-hole in search of food. As Dan idly sighted
the Dean and Adams on its crimson markings,
he wondered whether the snake ate crabs, or was
only looking for a stray frog; but the central
thought never left him.
His horse, tied close by, which, fly-tortured and
restless, had been pounding the grass to powder
with its hoofs, now stood motionless with head-up
and pointed ears facing the west, the bes't of
sentinels. Maher sprang up and looked at the
sun. “An hoipr high”—then under it with
shaded eyes, atid saw her coming. He went
slowly forward to meet the woman he thirsted
for. The wind, falling with the sun, allowed the
dust to rise a little, and formed a golden halo.
She saw him, and struck into a canter, but, when
Maher threw the reins over his horse’s head and
put his foot in the stirrup, she pulled again into a
walk.
Once more he went towards her, and caught
her rein without a word.
“You let go my horse, Dan Maher, or it will
be the worse for you.”
He looked her over, every curve and line, with-
out answering. How he wanted her ! He knew
her, coarse and greedy and vain, and had a certain
scorn of her, but more of himself. Still, how
thirsty he was!
“Get down, Lizzie,” he said at last—“l want
to ask you something.”
She hesitated, looked in his face, and slipped
from the saddle. “ Well, what is it ? ”
He tied the two horses to a sheep-bitten bush
beside them. “Is it true you’re going to marry
Bob Stirrat ? ”
“ I’m going to marry Mr. Stirrat, sheep over-
seer, your boss. And what about it ? ”
He almost pitied her ; she was so unconcerned.
“ You promised me 1 ” he said, and caught her in
his arms.
She writhed in his grasp, pushed against his
breast, and struck him in the face with her hands.
“I’ll get you the sack. You’ll see! How dare
you ? I’m for your master, I am. Oh, Dan ! the
horses have broken away ! ” she panted.
“ We want them no more, Lizzie.”
Then she knew ! “ Oh’! for God’s sake, no,
Dan ! Dear Dan, I always liked you best. Oh,
Dan ! Dan ! not your poor Lizzie, and she fawned
on lurr> - and held up her quivering lips to his.
The crow that, startled by the first report, flew
cawing away, did not get so far but that it heard
the second ; and Mr. Bob Stirrat, overseer, also
saw two puffs of smoke against the dark creek
timber, and swore that travellers would burn the
run out before the rains.
R. A. FAIRLY.
Paddy Mack.
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
Ladies and gentlemen, this is my friend Paddy
Mack—l might say everybody’s friend Paddy
Mack. He has been digging off and on for over
30 years. He is a fossicker—a genuine out-and-
out digger. He owns a big heart and a big bushy
beard, and his name is a proverb among the dig-
ging fraternity—a keynote for many a merry tale.
Little “ Tassy ” knows him, and Maoriland, Croy-
don, Kimberley, the Palmer, and the Towers;
so does the New Guinea coast, also the Golden
West. In fact, everybody knows him.
And Paddy has struck it to-day ! There’s never
a digger from Cape York to Coolgardie but will
be glad to know of it, will be proud to know of it.
Seven thousand pounds! I’ll give him six months
to get quit of it. For six months he may cast off
his plum-duff-pattern pants ; his blue-black frowsy
flannel, with the Home Rule patches up the back;
his shirt with the herring-bone stitches—such a
shirt! —Joseph’s (or was it Jacob’s ?) coat was a
fool to it; his boots bound in greenhide; his
hat that appears to have crossed Australia in a
dust-storm. Yes ! I’ll give them all a six months’
holiday. But we must not lose sight of them, for
surely they will be needed again. Come now, ye
barmaids! Come, all hard-up cronies. Salvation
majors, boozers, parsons, priests, Hospital Satur-
day people, and kindred cattle. Make hay while
the sun shines ! This is my friend Paddy Mack.
What are ye drinlcin’ ?
Christmas has come and gone. There’s a pub.
on the top of the hill; there’s a store at the foot.
There’s a fine spanking, team on the road. Many
a heart is made glad. There are stars at the
throats of the maids, on their fingers, are rings.
But here in his shaft, with his, plum-pudding
pants and his pipe and his shirt ; with his grim
happy face and his great bushy beard and a smile
on his face like the moon when she’s young, here,
ladies and gentlemen, is my friend Paddy Mack.
DAVID AMBROSE.

Island Lover.
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
The rumbling breaker recoils and doubles
Its pliant shape for the crashing blow;
The rock-shelf shakes, and the white froth bubbles
Up on the reef like a wreath of snow;
We are alone, and the loud world’s troubles
Are far away and are long ago.
The bold hibiscus red in your tresses
Flings me a challenge of frank desire;
Like incense-wafts from the wildernesses
The drowsy scent of a sandal fire ;
A wistful, wandering zephyr presses
The strings of some seolian lyre.
The South was bride and the North was lover
When you were born, with a kiss from each;
Your breast is white as the snows that cover
The great, drear plains where the wild fowl
screech;
Your locks are black as the swift-winged plover
That whistle out on the sea-lapped beach !
Oh, soft caresses ! the trades are turning—
Another sunset, and then good-bye !
Oh, clinging kisses ! your lips are learning
Love’s luring language, and night is nigh.
But ah ! to-morrow . . . not teardrops burning,
Salt dews of sorrow that never dry.
And then—next year, when the land-crab traces
Weird cross-stitch patterns upon the sand,
I shall be longing for your embi-aces
And I shall seek you ; and when I land
Meet but a bevy of strange young faces
And know the meaning—and understand.
Oh, dainty blossom that buds and flowers
And fades away at the noonday’s height!
Oh, butterfly that wastes bright hours
Of wanton freedom and dies at night!
Oh, foolish heart that the wild god dowers
With one short semblance of love’s delight!
Oh, Tuahina ! that youth’s full measure
Should pass away like a summer eve !
That just the one gift that women treasure
Should be so stinted, so poor, and leave
A taste of sweetness, a note of pleasure
And . . . grey-hued twilight to mourn and
grieve !
Fate is the breath, and you are the feather;
Time is the reaper, and you the rye ;
And yet, and yet, we have loved together,
And I am living, and you must die.
Oh, Tuahina ! I wonder whether
You are the loser, or I, or I.
I feel as old as the seas that sever
This summer isle fi’om the busy West;
I feel as if I were damned for ever
To live, and wander in search of Rest—
To walk the cycles in vain endeavor
With sickened heart on an endless quest.
I feel as old as the grey palms weaving
Their leafage into a mazy mesh ;
That year for year see a new vine cleaving
Around them, fondling, and frail and fresh.
What of Thy justice, Lord, that is leaving
Love to the taint of withering flesh ?
And for an answer the gulls are screaming
With mocking laughter across the main.
I sometimes fancy that we are dreaming,
And shall awaken and see again
After this night of hoping and scheming,
And God will smile —and perhaps explain.
VON KOTZE.
The King of Sydney Domain.
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
The King of Sydney Domain is a man of caste.
He stands 6ft. in his bare soles : his frock-coat is
a miracle of string and patches. We meet him
daily. He sits for hours under Mrs. Macquarie’s
Chair with the sea-wind in his white mane, his
deep-set eyes fixed on the ships that pass and go
down. He will smoke anything, and is a perfect
glutton for matches ; I believe he swops them for
sheets of brown paper—the blankets of the Domain.
The King has a past; he unfolded it yesterday in
exchange for a match.
“ Yer never had anythin’ ter do wiv boardin’-
houses, sir, eh? Well, I’ve bin froo ’em all.
“ The Dermain boys call me Barnacle Bill ’coz
I’m a born sticker. I made up me mind at a early
age never tew pay me board. Nobody ever bat-
tened on me. The game o’ knowin’ how tew live
is full of pints. Old age is agen me now. I’ve
lost the sparkil of me youth an’ me clothes. If I
had me clothes now nothin’ wud keep me out of a
boardin’-house. Stinker Jones turned dawg an’
pinched me portmanto two years ago. It wuz me
Waterloo. I’ve been destitoot since. Yes, sir, I
enjiy a heasy chair an’ a cigar better that these
cold rocks. Put yer ear on the ground an’ listen
tew the water drip. When yer hear that in the
night it makes yer think of nice warm boardin’-
houses an’ hot curry.”
He smiled loosely and drew a murky handker-
chief across his lips after the manner of the fat
gentlemen in the swell restaurants. “But,” he
continued, “ landladies are a hard lot, sir. Some
of ’em are as strong as three Jews, an’ as sharp as
that French feller Voltaire. It’s dealin’ wiv
battlers like me what kills all that is nice an’ soft
in ’em. They get narrer-minded an’ want tew be
paid reg’lar. If she ain’t narrer-minded an’ allows
herself to be ruled by low female emotions, it’s any
odds on the bailiff. I never had much to do wiv
bailiffs ; I cud have been one onst, but I ain’t
crool or hard enough tew fire lirrle widders inter
the cold street.
“ The bailiff is a nasty beast wiv a pipe an’ a
torn macintosh. This is how he starts business :
‘ ’Ow are yer, gor’ damn yer ? Where’s the
sticks, missus ? Eny kids ? ’Ow many ? Where’s
yer bloke ? Hout of work, I s’pose. Well, so ’elp
me ! Where’s the bloomin’ furniture ? Ain’t yer
goin’ ter shout, missis, afore I breaks up the ’appy
’otne ? ’ Then he sits down on the meat-safe, an’
sez fings to the pore lirrle widder. k
• * *
“I hate bein’ in a boardin’-house wiv theatrical
pipple. They deal out Shakespeare an’ stink the
place wiv fish-suppers. The low-comedy man
drops live crayfish inter the pianner. When the
lirrle girl boarder starts to play a hymn you can
hear a thing wiv nine legs walkin’ up the varia-
tions.
“Crayfish ain’t judges of moosio. I onst saw
one feelin’ his way out of the pedal-holes when
somebody struck up ‘ The Man that Broke the
Bank at Monty Carlo.’
“Me favorite toon’s "Ome, Sweet ’Ome,’ es-
pishully when it’s played on a boardin’-house
pianner what goes foo! whoof! in the soft parts.
An’ the lirrle girl boarders never seem tew notice
it; they jest tinkle-pinkie along while muvver
does a prime sniff in the corner. I ’ave never
complained about the bad insterments. One lady
sez to me onst—she had the loveliest nose I ever
see on a woman —Lord, it uster whistle in
the middle of the night when I wuz makin’ up me
' mind to do a slant! —she sez to me, ‘ Mister Prim-
rose, ’ave you eny objection to the pianner ? It
cost poor Mister Higgins ninety guineas on the
day we wuz married.’ ‘Ma’am,’ I sez politely
(she was a terror for bein’ paid reg’lar), ‘ your
pianner is still in the fashion, slightly meller wiv
age, but if you’ll excuse me, ma’am, it’s growin’
whiskers in the top notes.’ She didn’t know
wever ter bring out the pancake or make me stuff-
up for the three weeks I owed.”
At this point the King produced a pipe with an
enormous bowl charred like the rim of Vesuvius
which he tapped against his heel without looking
at me. I passed the match and a well-filled
tobacco-poucn. His innocent surprise was a study
in tragedy and burlesque.
A gun boomed across the Cove ; a jaunty yacht
with a crew of larrikins shook her white skirts
under the cannon-flanked ribs of the Royal
Arthur. The King sighed as the tobacco-smoke
built haloes over his white head.
“Ah, sir, me bones craves for a heasy chair, or
a soft bed wiv blankets. It’s me own fault, me
own fault. I’ve frown away chance arter chance;
an’ now in me lars days there aint so much as a
sup o’ wine or beer.”
I hinted delicately that cash would be sudden if
necessary. He took the proffered coin, placing it
with religious care somewhere in the depths of his
hat.
Puff . . . puff . . . puff. ...
“One night I wuz in the back room talkin’ to
the ladies. It wuzn’t much of a house, but I had
a good heap of clothes in me portmanto, an’
wuzn’t no trouble shiftin’ ’em when pay-day came
—jest frew ’em out the back winder, takin’ me
chance of hittin’ the dawg or the slavey. Then
I’d walk out, pick ’em up, an’ there I wuz ready
for another place. Everybody sez me profile wuz
like Sir ’Enery Parkes’s. I ’ad a bute of a frock-
coat—a real bute, an’ me black top-hat shone like a
nigger’s heel. Nobody wud ever sispect me of
doin’ a slant. But I’d made up me mind never
ter pay me board —never ! Nobody ever battened
on me. I wuz a squatter in one ’ouse, a lecturer in
another, a benivolent lecturer what had been to
the ’Oly Land an’ Bathsheba—that wuz me lars
charicter, the lecturer from Bathsheba. Me frock-
coat, me fat portmanto, an’ me steady eye, filled
the lirrle ladies wiv confidence. P’raps I wuz a
bit of a glutton over me porridge, but 1 alwus con-
ducted meself like a gentleman.
“ Well, one night 1 wuz sittin’ in the back room
givin’ the East what for, an’ all the ladies wus
sittin’ round me sayin’, ‘ Ho’ow verra naice ! ’ when-
ever I’d rip out a hunger of a lie about Jerusalem
the ’oly city, an’ Bathsheba; an’ just as me caravan
wuz crossin’ inter Galilee pursood by Bedowins,
we heerd a vice callin’ among the clothes-lines.
The vice sez, ‘ Bill! Bill! ’ an’ stopped. I stiffened
in me chair. ‘What’s that?’ sez the landlady,
lookin’ at me. ‘ It’s Stinker Jones, ma’am,’ I sez.
Y’see, Stinker an’ me hadn’t bin friends for a long
time, an’ I thought he wuz cornin’ to show me up.
I perceeded cautiously. ‘ It’s Stinker,’ I sez again ;
‘a low drunken feller what hasn’t a shirt to his back.
Ler him be.’ ‘Oh!’ sez the landlady, ‘then it
was Stinker who took my 12 dorkin’ hens. I
know his vice.’ I ler her say no more. ‘ Leave
him to me, ma’am ! ’ I sez quietly ; an’ wiv that I
■went out to Stinker. He hit me in the chest
afore I wuz ready, but I collared him by the
pants an’ frew him hard at the tank. ‘ Don’t you
dare hit me like that agin, Bill,’ he sez, sparrin’
wild ; ‘ don’t you dare hit me like that agin ! ’
‘ Where’s them 12 dorkin’ hens ? ’ I sez; ‘ where’s
the pore wuman’s poletry ? ’ ‘Eor Gorsake, Bill,
lemme go, an’ I won’t m’lest yer,’ he sez ; ‘ leinme
go, Bill.’ I ler him go. I wuz on me dignity. I
ler him go. But afore he went I ixplained a few
things, an’ he sez : ‘Yes, Bill, I’ll do anythin’ for
yer.’ Then I ler him go.
“ The ladies in the house called me a hero; but a
man like what’s bin collared by Bedowins don’t
want credit fer throwin’ a pore man like Stinker
at a tank. ‘ Don’t praise me, ladies! ’ I sez ;
‘ I’ll alius protect yer hearth and home while me
strength lasts.’ But I wuz in a fix in that house.
They uster bolt the back gate arter eleven, an’ it
wuz too high fer me ter climb wiv me portmanto.
So I resolved to git it out the front door. This is
how it happened. At the end of the monf—l
alwuz pay monfly—the landlady tapped me very
firmly. ‘Will you oblige me now, Mister Prim-
rose,’ she sez, “ wiv your board-money ? ’ I never
heerd a woman say noiv like her afore. I pulled
out me brass watch, gave her me lecturer’s cough,
went to the door an’ come back. ‘ Excuse me,
ma'am, while I go upstairs an’ get a five-poun’-
note out of me portmanto. Everybody in the
house heard what I said, an’ they all waited
behind the drawin’-room door ter see me come
down an’ give the landlady her money. Pipple
are very cur’ous about money matters in boardin’
houses. There is times when everybody sispects
everybody. But the breath of suspicion had never
been blown on me name —thank Gawd !
“I went upstairs slowly; I wuz sparrin’ fer
time. I dumped me port across the floor and pre-
tended ter rummage fer the fiver. ‘ He’s a verra
long time,’ sez somebody; ‘let’s go up.’ The
landlady sez : ‘ What nominee ! I’d trust Mister
Primrose wiv me live.’ Jest then a familar voice
rushed in the front door an’ shouted upstairs,
* Bill, Bill, yer mother’s dead an’ left yer a for-
tune ! ’ I collared me port wiv both arms an’
rushed downstairs. ‘Out of me way, ladies!’ I
shouts. - Stand away, me mother’s left it me at
larst! ’ an’ out I went like a bloomin’ tornader.
Stinker jined me round the corner. Me conscience
hurt me a bit though. I couldn’t forgit how
the ladies had sat at me feet an’ looked into me
face. So I wrote the landlady a note: ‘ Deer
ma’am, —What Stinker Jones said about mother
wuzn’t true. I’m a pore man yet. Me trouble is
killin’ me. I owe you 3£ 155.. Pleas don’t forgit
that I defended your home; so I my
account squares us —fightin’ berglar, 3£ 15s.—Bill
Peimhose.’
“ But Stinker turned dawg on me at lars. He
wuz waitin’ outside a boardin’-’ouse for me onst
tew collar me port, when I frew it over the veran-
dah. I frew if over. He picked it up an’ scooted
—me after him. How he did run ! —yer couldn’t
see him fer distance. ‘ Stop, Stinker !’ I shouted.
‘ Stop, fer Heaven sake! ’ He never stopped,
never till he got to a pawnshop. Turned dawg on
me arter knowin’ him fer years. That wuz me
Waterloo.”
The King of Sydney Domain gazed sadly sea-
ward. All that—l reflected—for sixpence and
nine matches, I left the King alone with his
emotions.
ALBA DORION.
“Scotty the Wrinkler ” holds forth:—
Dear B.—Melb. Weekly Times (24/3/’00) on “the
rifle club movement ” :
Sir Frederick Sargood points out that “(a) the Boers
have never been noted for long range shooting, and have
been constantly beaten in competitions with members of
the home volunteer force, while as a matter of fact in
peace time they are chary of wasting ammunition, and
hence never fire at game at more than 200 or 300yds.,
and (b) during the present war is it not a fact that the
serious losses they have inflicted upon our troops have
invariably been at short ranges.”
Any sane man is “ chary of wasting ammunition.”
Shooting game at more than “ 200 or 300yds.” is
bosh. The vital parts of a deer (and I suppose a
springbok or gemsbok is a deer) are the same as if
you put for a target a pint flask, a well-rope 3ft.
long in a horizontal line, and a tea-cup below
them. That represents the head, spine and heart.
A broken hind-leg does not give you the deer.
Many a black-buck have I sent off with a swinger.
Shot through the bowels they’ll gallop
five miles, and it takes good man, good
horse, and good country to get up with
them. Sportsmen (including Boers) stalk game
such as blackbuck, sambur, chinkara, para,
oorial, marklioor, ibex, etc., up to where
they can plug them vitally. I remember the
crack shot of the 82nd, with a Whitworth rifle
and all sorts of sights, never got half as many
horns as I and my three chums brought down with
a double-barrelled Enfield, a single Express, and a
16-bore fowling-piece ! Fellows talk of sniping at
1000yds. Now take a good pea-rifle (I’ll lend you
one) at 50yds. That’s the 20th part of 1000. Mark
on a sheet of paper a man 3|in. high and half-an-
inch wide. Pot him, if you please, vitally. The
long ranges are only for firing at masses, and a
company is very small not only in height but in
width at half-a-mile. The Rifle Club movement
is new, affords a means of getting free ammunition
for kangarooing, slaughtering bullocks, making a.
noise, and playing Kriegspiel. What is wanted is
to teach the handling of fire-arms without danger,
the reasons of the different loadings and sightings,
the saving (not the wasting) of ammunition, and
the mechanism of lock, stock and barrel.

PASTEURISED The same remarks that apply to milk apply equally to butter. Pasteurised butter is made from the very finest
BUTTER • • •
cream, consequently the butter has a fine aroma that only Pasteurisation can give. There are a few, we are
pleased to say, only a few, idiotic folks who will not be convinced of the value of the process ; but it is satisfactory
to know that these are getting less daily. The difference in price between Pasteurised, beautifully-flavored butter and the
ordinary make is only per lb. It would pay consumers well to give six times this difference. Heads of households should insist
that their grocer supplies Pasteurised butter, and if he will not, send your orders to US, and we will deliver it at your doors.
Good butter such as we have described is appreciated always, whereas at afternoon teas or for ordinary use bad butter is dear
at any price. The famous “ Berry ” brand Pasteurised butter can be obtained only from this Company.
